Interpreting 'Yawm' in Oaths: Haqiqah vs. Majaz in Hanafi Usul
Explains the impossibility of combining literal and metaphorical meanings, and sets rules for interpreting 'yawm'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for interpreting 'yawm' in oaths by classifying the action as extensive (muttadd) or non-extensive (ghayr muttadd) and determine whether the literal or metaphorical meaning governs.

Core Idea
When 'yawm' cannot be literal, Hanafi usul prefers metaphorical meaning over homonymy.

In oaths involving the word 'yawm' (day), a critical usul question arises: does it mean daytime (haqiqah) or absolute time (majaz)? The Hanafi scholars resolve this by analyzing the extensiveness of the action tied to the oath. This lesson unpacks the rule and its application, showing how a single word can carry two distinct legal consequences based on the act described.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two meanings of 'yawm'
Recognize that 'yawm' has a literal meaning (daytime) and a metaphorical meaning (absolute time, including night). In Hanafi usul, when the literal meaning is impossible, the metaphorical is used, not homonymy.
2
Understand impossibility of combining haqiqah and majaz
The text states that in a single utterance, one cannot intend both the literal and figurative meanings simultaneously. The intended meaning must be either one or the other.
This principle avoids ambiguity in oaths.
3
Classify the action as muttadd or ghayr muttadd
Determine if the action referred to in the oath can be extended to cover the entire daytime (e.g., riding) or cannot (e.g., arriving). Extensive actions (muttadd) can be measured by the whole day; non-extensive (ghayr muttadd) cannot.
The classification is based on customary duration.
4
Apply the rule: literal if extensive, metaphorical if not
If the action is muttadd, 'yawm' means daytime (haqiqah). If ghayr muttadd, it means absolute time (majaz). This is because the literal meaning is preferred when possible, and when impossible, the metaphor is used.
5
Consider the operator vs. the object
When both the verb (operator) and the object (mudaf ilayh) are considered, the decisive factor is the operator's extensiveness. If the operator is extensive, daytime is intended; if not, absolute time.
This resolves potential conflicts when the object differs from the operator.

Source Text

بيان استحالة الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز
بيان أقسام التقسيم الثالث مجازي شامل للنهار والليل، فيحنث باعتبار عموم المجاز لا باعتبار الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز، وقيل: هو مشترك بين النهار وبين مطلق الوقت، فأريد ههنا معنى الوقت، وبالجملة لابد ههنا من بيان ضابطة يعرف بها أنه في أي موضع يراد به النهار وفي أي موضع يراد به الوقت، فقيل: إذا كان الفعل ممتدا يراد به النهار؛ لأنه زمان ممتد يصلح أن يكون معيارا للفعل، وإن كان غير ممتد يراد به الوقت المطلق؛ لأنه يكفي لذلك الفعل جزء من الوقت، ولكنهم اختلفوا في أنه أيّ فعل يعتبر في هذا الباب المضاف إليه أو العامل، فالضابطة أنه إذا كانا
الفعل
وقيل إلخ إشارة بكلمة التمريض إلى أن كون لفظ اليوم مشتركا بين النهار ومطلق الوقت، ليس بجيد، وإن كان يشعر به كلام "المحيط"، وأقر به أعظم العلماء أي مولانا عبد السلام الله - والأصح أنه مجاز في مطلق الوقت ترجيحا للمجاز على الاشتراك كما تقرر في مقره كذا في "التحقق". (القمر) وقيل: هو مشترك إلخ هذا عند البعض، وعلى هذا فليس مما نحن فيه فلا يراد أصلاً، وعند الأكثر مجاز فيه، وفي الكشف وهو الأصح ترجيحا للمجاز على الاشتراك. (السنبلي) إذا كان الفعل ممتدا : هو ما يصح فيه ضرب المدة: أي يصح تقديره بمدة كالركوب، فإنه يصح أن يقال: ركبت هذه الدابة يوما، وغير الممتد بخلافه كالقدوم، وقال شارح "الوقاية": إن المراد بالفعل الممتد ممتد يمكن أن يستوعب امتداده النهار لا مطلق الامتداد؛ لأنهم جعلوا التكلم من قبيل غير الممتد، ولا شك أن المتكلم ممتد زمانًا طويلاً لكن لا يمتد بحيث يستوعب النهار عادة وعرفًا. (القمر) ممتدا إلخ وهذه الضابطة تؤيد بأن تقدير "في" يوجب الاستيعاب وههنا لما كان في مقدرة وجب استيعابه للمظروف، فإذا كان ممتدا فيمكن استيعاب النهار إياه، فأمكن المعنى الحقيقي، فيحمل عليه لأصالته، وأما إذا كان غير ممتد فلا يمكن استيعاب النهار إياه فلا يحمل عليه بل على مطلق الوقت الأعم من أجزائه وأجزاء الليل والعلامة العموم، فإن مطلق الوقت عام من النهار، وهذا يرشدك إلى أن العبرة لعامله المظروف لا لما أضيف إليه فتدبر. (السنبلي)
يراد به إلخ إلا إذا دل الدليل والقرينة على أن المراد باليوم الوقت كما تقول: "أركبوا يوم يأتيكم العدو". (القمر) لأنه أي لأن النهار زمان ممتد إلخ مع أنه معنى حقيقي للفظ اليوم، فكان أولى بالإرادة. (القمر) يراد به الوقت المطلق أي سواء كان من النهار أو من الليل إلا إذا دل الدليل والقرينة على أن المراد باليوم النهار كما تقول: "عبدي حر يوم ينكشف الشمس". (القمر) إذا كانا أي المضاف إليه والعامل. (القمر)
بيان أقسام التقسيم الثالث
بيان استحالة الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز ممتدين مثل: "أمرك بيدك يوم يركب "زيد يراد باليوم النهار، وإن كانا غير ممتدين مثل: "عبدي حر يوم يقدم "فلان" يراد باليوم الوقت، وإن كان أحدهما ممتداً دون الآخر مثل: "أمرك بيدك يوم يقدم فلان أو أنتِ طالق يوم يركب زيد، فالمعتبر هو العامل دون المضاف إليه بالاتفاق.
Explanation of the impossibility of combining the literal and metaphorical [meanings]. Explanation of the divisions of the third division: metaphorical includes both night and day, so he breaks the oath by virtue of the generality of the metaphor, not by virtue of combining the literal and the metaphorical. It was said: it is homonymous between daytime and absolute time, but here the meaning of time is intended. In short, a rule must be given here by which it is known when the daytime is intended and when the time is intended. It was said: if the action is extensive, daytime is intended because it is an extended time suitable as a measure for the action. If it is not extensive, absolute time is intended because a part of the time suffices for that action. However, they differed regarding which action is considered in this chapter: the one added to [the time] or the operative [verb]. The rule is: when both are [extensive or non-extensive]...
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage discusses a usul principle: the impossibility of combining a literal and a metaphorical meaning when interpreting the word 'yawm' (day) in oaths. The issue arises because 'yawm' can be used literally for daytime (nahar) or metaphorically for any period of time (mutlaq waqt). The text first refutes the idea that 'yawm' is homonymous (mushtarak) between the two, asserting instead that the metaphorical meaning is more correct. It then establishes a rule: if the action referred to is extensive (muttadd), meaning it can span the entire daytime (e.g., riding), the literal meaning (daytime) is intended. If the action is non-extensive (ghayr muttadd), such as arriving, which cannot cover the whole day, then 'yawm' means the absolute time. A key point of discussion is whether to consider the attribute of the action (mudaf ilayh) or the verb/operator ('amil). The rule concludes that the operator is primary, but the example given indicates that when both the operator and the object are extensive, daytime is meant; when both are non-extensive, absolute time is meant; and when they differ, the operator is considered. This analysis demonstrates the Hanafi method of prioritizing the literal meaning when possible, and resorting to metaphorical interpretation only when necessary due to incompatibility.

Key Terms
اليوم (al-yawm): In this context, 'yawm' has two meanings: literal (daytime) and metaphorical (absolute time including parts of night and day).
حقيقة (haqiqah): The original, literal meaning of a word ('daytime' for 'yawm').
مجاز (majaz): A transferred, figurative meaning ('absolute time' for 'yawm').
ممتد (muttadd): An action that can be extended to cover the entire daytime (e.g., riding).
غير ممتد (ghayr muttadd): An action that cannot be extended to cover the entire daytime (e.g., arriving).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi rule established in the passage, what is the default interpretation of 'yawm' when the action is non-extensive (ghayr muttadd)?
A) Daytime (haqiqah) because the literal meaning is always preferred.
B) Absolute time (majaz) because the literal meaning is impossible to apply.
C) Homonymous (mushtarak) leaving the meaning ambiguous.
D) The same as the operator's extensiveness regardless of the action.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, when interpreting 'yawm' in oaths, the literal meaning (daytime) is intended if the action is extensive; otherwise, the metaphorical meaning (absolute time) governs, providing a consistent rule for legal practice.

The Third Division of Nadhr and Yamin: Combining Haqiqa and Majaz
Analyzing whether a single utterance can combine vow and oath through literal and metaphorical meaning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that a single utterance can carry two distinct legal effects (nadhr and yamin) through its literal meaning (haqiqa) and its necessary consequence (muwjib), and evaluate the dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the combination of haqiqa and majaz in intention.

Core Idea
A vow by its wording, an oath by its consequence — no conflict between haqiqa and majaz when one is literal and the other is necessary.

This passage from Noor al-Anwar addresses a nuanced usul question: can a single utterance simultaneously create both a vow (nadhr) and an oath (yamin)? The dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two foremost disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, turns on whether the combination of literal meaning (haqiqa) and metaphorical meaning (majaz) in one intention is possible. The author resolves this by distinguishing between the literal meaning intended (haqiqa for nadhr) and the necessary legal consequence (muwjib for yamin), which does not require separate intention. The Quranic treatment of tahrim al-mubah (prohibiting the permissible) as an oath provides the evidential basis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenarios of intention
The text presents three main scenarios: (1) intending both nadhr and yamin, (2) intending only yamin with negation of nadhr, (3) intending nothing or only nadhr with negation of yamin. Understand which scenario triggers the dispute.
Scenarios 1 and 2 are the basis of the ikhtilaf.
2
Understand Abu Hanifa's ruling for each scenario
Abu Hanifa holds that in scenario 1 (intending both) the utterance is both nadhr and yamin; in scenario 2 (only yamin) it is only yamin; in scenario 3 it is only nadhr by agreement. The Sahibayn differ in scenario 1, saying it is only one of them based on intention.
The disagreement is confined to scenario 1.
3
Analyze the author's response to the objection
The author argues that the combination is possible because the utterance is a nadhr by its literal wording (صيغة نذر) and a yamin by its necessary consequence (موجب). The consequence of a nadhr is to make permissible acts prohibited (tahrim al-mubah), and tahrim al-mubah is itself a yamin by Quranic designation. Therefore, both effects coexist without requiring intention for the yamin meaning as a majaz.
Key distinction: haqiqa for nadhr, muwjib for yamin, avoids conflict of haqiqa and majaz in intention.
4
Examine the Quranic proof and its application
The Quran (Surah At-Tahrim, 1-2) calls the Prophet's prohibition of lawful things an 'oath' and prescribes its expiation. This establishes that prohibiting the permissible is a form of yamin. Thus, when a nadhr causes tahrim al-mubah, the yamin ruling applies automatically as a necessary consequence.
The dalil is qat'i in both thubut and dalala, strengthening the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان استحالة الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز
بيان أقسام التقسيم الثالث
فإنه عنده نذر في الأول ويمين في الثاني، وإن لم ينو شيئًا أو نوى النذر مع نفي اليمين، أو بلا نفيه يكون نذرًا بالاتفاق، وإن نوى اليمين مع نفي النذر يكون يمينًا بالاتفاق، عملًا بنفيه.
والإيراد إنما هو على الوجهين الأولين على مذهبهما. فأجاب المصنف بأنه إنما أريد النذر واليمين جميعا في هذه الصورة.
لأنه نذر بصيغته، يمين بموجبه، وتحريره: أن قوله: الله علي صيغة نذر، وهو معناه الموضوع له، وكان صوم رجب مثلا قبل النذر مباح الفعل والترك، وبعد النذر صار الفعل واجبًا، والترك حرامًا، فيلزم من موجب هذا النذر تحريم المباح الذي هو الترك، وتحريم الحلال يمين؛ لأن الرسول قد حرم مارية أو العسل على نفسه، فسمى الله ذلك يمينًا، في قوله تعالى: يَا أَيُّهَا النَّبِيُّ لِمَ تُحَرِّمُ مَا أَحَلَّ اللَّهُ لَكَ تَبْتَغِي مَرْضَاتَ أَزْوَاجِكَ وَاللَّهُ غَفُورٌ رَحِيمٌ (التحريم: 1)، قَدْ فَرَضَ اللَّهُ لَكُمْ تَحِلَّةَ أَيْمَانِكُمْ (التحريم: 2). فعلم أن تحريم الحلال يمين فيكون موجبًا للكلام لا مرادًا بطريق المجاز، ولكنه يرد عليه أنه إذا كان موجبًا ينبغي أن يثبت بدون النية؛ لأن موجب الشيء لا يحتاج إلى النية إلا أن يقال: لا مرادًا فلا يلزم الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز في الإرادة.
وأما تحريم مارية فما رواه النسائي عن أنس أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم كانت له أمة يطؤها، فلم تزل به عائشة وحفصة حتى حرمها على نفسه، فأنزل الله عزوجل يَا أَيُّهَا النَّبِيُّ لِمَ تُحَرِّمُ مَا أَحَلَّ الله لَكَ (التحريم: 1) إلى آخر الآية. وأما تحريم العسل فما رواه البخاري ومسلم عن عائشة.
Statement of the impossibility of combining the literal and metaphorical meanings. Statement of the divisions of the third division. For according to him (Abu Hanifa) it is a vow in the first case and an oath in the second. If he intended nothing, or intended a vow with negation of the oath, or without negating it, it is a vow by agreement. If he intended an oath with negation of the vow, it is an oath by agreement, acting upon his negation. The objection is only against the first two cases according to their (the two companions') school. The author answered that in this case both the vow and the oath are intended. Because it is a vow by its wording, and an oath by its necessary consequence. The explanation: his saying 'Upon me is God' is a wording of vow, and it is its assigned meaning. For example, fasting in Rajab before the vow was permissible to do or leave, and after the vow the act became obligatory and leaving it became prohibited. So from the necessary consequence of this vow follows the prohibition of what is permissible, which is leaving (the fast). And prohibiting what is permissible is an oath, because the Messenger prohibited Maria or honey upon himself, and God called that an oath in His saying: 'O Prophet, why do you prohibit what God has made lawful to you, seeking the approval of your wives? And God is Forgiving and Merciful.' (At-Tahrim: 1) 'God has already ordained for you the dissolution of your oaths.' (At-Tahrim: 2). So it is known that prohibiting what is lawful is an oath. Thus it is a necessary consequence of the utterance, not intended in a metaphorical way. But it is objected: if it is a necessary consequence, it should be established without intention, because the necessary consequence of a thing does not need intention. Unless it is said: it is not intended, so there is no combination of literal and metaphorical meaning in the intention. As for the prohibition of Maria, it is narrated by an-Nasa'i from Anas that the Messenger of God had a slave girl with whom he had relations, and Aisha and Hafsa kept at him until he prohibited her upon himself, so God revealed 'O Prophet, why do you prohibit what God has made lawful to you' to the end of the verse. As for the prohibition of honey, it is narrated by al-Bukhari and Muslim from Aisha.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis from Noor al-Anwar, a Hanafi usul work. It addresses the third division regarding someone who says 'Allahu alayya' (upon me is God) and the determination of whether it counts as a vow (nadhr) or an oath (yamin). The text presents Abu Hanifa's position that in the first case (intending both nadhr and yamin) it is both; in the second (intending only yamin) it is only yamin. The two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differ: they hold that it is either one or the other based on intention. If the person intends both, they consider it only the one intended, not both. The objection (الايراد) is directed at the disciples' view. The author (Al-Sadr al-Shahid) responds that the utterance necessarily combines both: it is a nadhr by its wording (صيغة نذر) and a yamin by its necessary legal consequence (موجب). The consequence of a nadhr is that what was permissible (like fasting or leaving a fast) becomes obligatory to do and prohibited to leave. Prohibiting what is permissible (tahrim al-mubah) is itself a form of yamin, as proven by the Quranic verses (Surah Tahrim, 1-2) where the Prophet's prohibition of honey or Maria was called an oath by God. Therefore, the utterance entails both meanings: nadhr directly, and yamin indirectly through its necessary effect. The text then addresses a counterargument: if yamin is a necessary consequence, it should apply without intention, but it does require intention in practice. The resolution is that the yamin meaning is not intended directly (لَا مَرَادًا), so there is no combination of literal (haqiqa) and metaphorical (majaz) in the same intention. The passage also narrates the two incidents of the Prophet prohibiting Maria and honey as evidence.

Key Terms
نذر (nadhr): A vow: a verbal commitment to perform a voluntary act of worship, making it obligatory.
يمين (yamin): An oath: a verbal affirmation or prohibition invoking God's name, making the action subject to expiation (kaffara) if violated.
موجب (muwjib): The literal or legal consequence inherent in the utterance, not requiring separate intention.
حقيقة (haqiqa): Literal, primary meaning of a word as originally assigned in language.
مجاز (majaz): Metaphorical or extended meaning used when the literal meaning is not intended.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if someone says 'Allahu alayya' intending both a vow and an oath, what is the ruling?
A) It is only a vow, because the wording is for vows.
B) It is only an oath, because the consequence is prohibiting the permissible.
C) It is both a vow and an oath: vow by wording, oath by necessary consequence.
D) It is invalid because one cannot combine two legal categories in one utterance.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle that a single utterance can yield two rulings through literal meaning and necessary consequence, without combining haqiqa and majaz in intention, is established by the Quranic treatment of tahrim al-mubah as an oath.

The Inability to Combine Literal and Figurative Meaning in One Intention
Refuting the possibility of combining literal and figurative meanings in a single intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that haqiqah and majaz cannot be combined in a single intention to an ambiguous oath/vow utterance, and evaluate the opposing argument.

Core Idea
One word cannot simultaneously convey its literal and figurative meanings in a single intention.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi principle in usul al-fiqh: the impossibility of combining literal (haqiqah) and figurative (majaz) meanings in one intention. The discussion arises from the legal analysis of a person who says 'for Allah upon me to make X forbidden' and intends both an oath (yamin) and a vow (nadhr). The Hanafi position is that such a dual intention is invalid because it would require the same word to be used in both its literal and figurative senses simultaneously. We will trace the arguments, the counterarguments, and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the utterance and its default classification
When someone says 'for Allah upon me to make X forbidden,' the default is that it is a vow, not an oath, because the phrasing is customarily used for vows.
Custom (urf) governs the interpretation.
2
Recognize the issue of combining haqiqah and majaz
If the speaker intends both an oath and a vow, then the word 'for Allah' would need to simultaneously mean a vow (literal) and an oath (figurative) in the same intention. Hanafi usul holds this is impossible.
This is the core principle.
3
Examine the Hanafi proof
The Quranic verses (66:1-2) and the analogy of buying a close relative (where literal ownership leads to consequential manumission) show that literal and consequential meanings are distinct and cannot be merged in one intention.
The analogy is a qiyas jaliy.
4
Review the opposing view and the Hanafi response
Some scholars claim the wording can serve both purposes, but the Hanafi response is that it would force an impossible combination, or alternatively, the wording is customarily dominant for vows.
The opposing view is considered weak.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference is whether the utterance is treated as a vow (requiring fulfillment) or an oath (requiring expiation) or both, and whether intention counts.
If only vow, no expiation for oath breaking.

(التحريم: ١)
(التحريم: ٢)
تحريم الحلال ،يمين فيكون موجبًا للكلام لا مرادًا بطريق المجاز، ولكنه يرد عليه أنه إذا
أي لازماً
اليمين
كان موجبًا ينبغي أن يثبت بدون النية؛ لأن موجب الشيء لا يحتاج إلى النية إلا أن يقال:
لا مرادًا فلا يلزم الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز في الإرادة. (القمر) يرد عليه الإيراد نقله صاحب "الكشف" عن الإمام السرخسي (القمر) ينبغي أن يثبت إلخ مع أنه لا يثبت بدون النية كما مر. (القمر) إلا أن يقال إلخ: توضيحه أن تحريم المباح وإن كان لازما لهذا النذر لكن سلب عنه معنى اليمين عادةً كما سلب معنى اليمين عن يمين اللغو عند الشافعي ، هو عنده ما يجري على لسانه بحكم العادة من غير إرادة لفظها ولا معناه نحو: لا والله، وبلى والله، فصار اليمين حينئذ كالحقيقة المهجورة؛ فلذا يحتاج إلى النية مثلها. وفيه خدشة تقريرها: أن اليمين ما صار داخلاً تحت الإرادة والنية، وهو معنى مجازي، والنذر أيضًا ،مراد فيلزم اجتماع الحقيقة والمجاز في الإرادة، فلزم القرار على ما عنه الفرار، ولعله لهذا أشار الشارح إلى الضعف وقال: إلا أن يقال إلخ. (القمر) إلا أن يقال إلخ يعني أن تحريم المباح الذي ثبت في ضمن إيجاب المباح لا يسمى يمينًا عرفا؛ لأن هذه الصيغة ليست متعارفة في تحريم المباح حيث غلبت في النذر المجرد فصارت إرادة اليمين من هذه الصيغة كالحقيقة المهجورة، فاشترط النية ليصير تحريم المباح يمينًا. قيل: إن تحريم المباح وإن ثبت بموجب النذر استلزاما ولا يتوقف على القصد إلا أن كونه يمينا يتوقف على القصد؛ لأن الشرع لم يجعله يمينًا إلا عند القصد، بخلاف شراء القريب، فإن الشرع جعله إعتاقا قصد أو لم يقصد، وقيل: إن الشرع لم يعتبر تحريم المباح الثابت في ضمن إيجاب
المباح يمينًا دفعا للحرج، إذ لو اعتبر يمينًا لوجبت الكفارة بدون قصد الحالف، وفيه حرج ظاهر . (السنبلي) أما تحريم مارية فما رواه النسائي في "سننه" ، رقم: ۳۹۵۹، باب الغيرة، عن أنس أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم كانت له أمة يطؤها، فلم تزل به عائشة وحفصة حتى حرمها على نفسه، فأنزل الله عزوجل يَا أَيُّهَا النَّبِيُّ لِمَ تُحَرِّمُ مَا أَحَلَّ
الله لَكَ ﴾ (التحريم: (۱) إلى آخر الآية. وذكر البغوي هذا الحديث، وصرح باسم مارية. [إشراق الأبصار: ١٠] وأما تحريم العسل فما رواه البخاري رقم: ٤٩٦٧ ، باب لما تحرم ما أحل الله لك، ومسلم، رقم: ١٤٧٤، باب وجوب الكفارة على من حرم امرأته ولم ينو الطلاق، عن عائشة ، وفيه: قال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم سقتني حفصة شربة عسل إلخ. وفي رواية: شربت عسلاً عند زينب بنت جحش ، ولن أعود إليه، فنزلت: لِمَ تُحَرِّمُ مَا أَحَلَّ اللَّهُ لَكَ ﴾ (التحريم: ١) أخرجه البخاري رقم: ٦۳۱۳ ، باب إذا حرم طعاما ،ومسلم، رقم: ١٤٧٤ ، باب وجوب الكفارة على من حرم امرأته و لم ينو الطلاق، والنسائي رقم ٣٤٢١ باب تأويل قوله عز وجل: يَا أَيُّهَا النَّبِيُّ لِمَ تُحَرِّمُ مَا أَحَلَّ اللَّهُ لَكَ ) (التحريم: ()، وأبو داود رقم: ۳۷۱٤ ، باب في شراب العسل وأحمد في مسنده رقم: ٢٥٨٩٤، عن عائشة

۲۹۱
بيان أقسام التقسيم الثالث بيان استحالة الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز إنها كالحقيقة المهجورة، فلذا يحتاج إلى النية، وقيل: إن اليمين هي المرادة من اللفظ، والنذر ليس بمراد، بل جاء بصيغة اللفظ، ولكن هذا إنما يصح إذا نوى اليمين فقط، وأما
إذا نواهما فقد دخل النذر تحت الإرادة، وإن لم يكن محتاجًا إليه، وقيل: إن قوله: "الله"
الإرادة
بمعنى والله صيغة يمين، وقوله: "علي" صيغة نذر، فلا يجتمعان في لفظ واحد.
فهو كشراء القريب، فإنه تملك بصيغته تحرير بموجبه تشبيه لمسألة النذر به توضيحا
وهو الملك
وتأييدا، فإن من شرى القريب يكون تملكا باعتبار صيغته، لأن صيغته موضوعة للملك، ولكن يكون تحريراً وإعتاقًا بموجبه؛ لأن موجب الملك مع القرابة هو العتق قال عليه:
"من ملك ذا رحم محرم منه عتق وإلا فبين الشراء والتحرير منافاة بحسب الظاهر.
وقيل : القائل صاحب "التوضيح . (القمر) ليس بمراد : فلا يلزم الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز في الإرادة. (القمر) تحت الإرادة: فلزم الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز في الإرادة. (القمر) وقيل: القائل شمس الأئمة. (القمر) بمعنى والله كما قال ابن عباس : دخل آدم الجنة، فلله ما غربت الشمس حتى خرج أي بالله وقال ابن الملك لقائل أن يقول: إن اللام إنما تجيء للقسم إذا كان الموضع موضع تعجب كما في قول ابن عباس ها، وقد نص على ذلك في كتب النحو. (القمر)
فلا يجتمعان أي الحقيقة والمجاز في لفظ واحد بل في كلمتين إلا أن هذا الكلام غلب عند الإطلاق في النذر
من
عادة، فيحمل على النذر ، فإذا نوى اليمين والنذر ، فقد نوى بكل لفظ ما هو محتمل له فتعمل النية. (القمر) كشراء أي صار اليمين بعد انضمام النية مثل عتق القريب لازما للنذر ، وفيه نظر لا وسعة لبيانه لها. (المحشي) ملك ذا رحم إلخ روى أبو داود عن سمرة عن النبي من ملك ذا رحم محرم فهو حر". (القمر) هذا اللفظ رواه البيهقي والنسائي، وضعفاه بسبب أن ضمرة انفرد به عن سفيان، وصححه عبد الحق، وقال: ضمرة ثقة، وإذا أسند الحديث ثقة فلا يضر انفراده به ولا إرسال من أرسله ولا وقف من وقفه، وصوب ابن القطان کلامه وممن وثق ضمرة بن معين وغيره وإن لم يحتج به في الصحيحين هكذا في "فتح القدير". [إشراق الأبصار: ١٠]
وروى أحمد في أحمد في مسنده :رقم: ۲۰۱۷۹ ، وأبو داود رقم: ٣٩٤۹، باب فيمن ملك ذا رحم محرم، والترمذي
رقم ١٣٦٥ ، باب ما جاء فيمن ملك ذا رحم محرم، وقال الترمذي: هذا حديث لا نعرفه مسندًا إلا من حديث حماد سلمة، وابن ماجه رقم ٢٥٢٤ ، باب من ملك ذا رحم محرم فهو حر، عن سمرة بن جندب عن النبي أنه قال: من ملك ذا رحم محرم فهو حر . قال الشيخ ابن حجر: ورجح جمع من الحفاظ أنه موقوف. [إشراق الأبصار: ١٠]
بن
Statement of the divisions of the third classification: Statement of the impossibility of combining the literal and metaphorical meanings. And He said: "Why do you forbid what Allah has made lawful for you?" then He said: "Allah has already ordained for you the dissolution of your oaths." So it became known that forbidding the permissible is an oath, so it is a necessary consequence of the utterance, not intended by way of metaphor. But it is objected to him that if it is a necessary consequence, it should be established without intention, because the necessary consequence of a thing does not need intention—unless it is said: it is not intended, so combining literal and metaphorical meanings in intention does not arise. (Al-Qamar) The objection is reported by the author of Al-Kashf from Imam al-Sarakhsi (Al-Qamar): "it should be established" etc. whereas it is not established without intention as mentioned before. (Al-Qamar) "unless it is said" etc.: its clarification is that the prohibition of the permissible, even though it is necessary for this vow, the meaning of oath is customarily stripped from it, just as the meaning of oath is stripped from laghw oath according to al-Shafi'i. According to him, it is what runs on the tongue by force of custom without intending its word or its meaning, like "No, by Allah" and "Yes, by Allah." So the oath then becomes like an abandoned literal meaning; therefore it needs intention like it. But there is an objection: its formulation is that the oath is that which falls under intention and the act of intending, and it is a metaphorical meaning, and the vow is also intended, so combining literal and metaphorical meanings in intention would arise, thus one must remain upon what he sought to avoid. Perhaps for this reason the commentator pointed out weakness and said: "unless it is said" etc. (Al-Qamar) "unless it is said" etc. means that prohibiting the permissible which is established within the obligation of the permissible is not called an oath customarily, because this phrasing is not customary in prohibiting the permissible, since it prevails in a bare vow; thus intending an oath from this phrasing becomes like an abandoned literal meaning, so intention is stipulated so that the prohibition of the permissible becomes an oath. It is said: Although the prohibition of the permissible is established by virtue of the vow by necessary implication and does not depend on intent, its being an oath depends on intent, because the Sacred Law did not make it an oath except with intent, unlike buying a close relative, for the Sacred Law made it a manumission whether he intended or not. And it is said: The Sacred Law did not consider the prohibition of the permissible established within the obligation of the permissible as an oath in order to avoid hardship, for if it were considered an oath, expiation would be obligatory without the swearer's intent, and that would be clear hardship. (Al-Sanbali) As for the prohibition of Mariyah: what al-Nasa'i narrated in his Sunan, no. 3959, chapter on jealousy, from Anas that the Messenger of Allah ﷺ had a slave girl he used to have intercourse with, and Aisha and Hafsa kept at him until he forbade her to himself, so Allah revealed: "O Prophet, why do you forbid what Allah has made lawful for you?" (Al-Tahrim: 1) to the end of the verse. Al-Baghawi mentioned this hadith and specified her name as Mariyah. [Ishraq al-Absar: 10] And as for the prohibition of honey: what al-Bukhari narrated no. 4967, chapter on when he forbids what Allah has made lawful for you, and Muslim no. 1474, chapter on the obligation of expiation upon one who forbids his wife without intending divorce, from Aisha, and in it: the Prophet ﷺ said: "Hafsa gave me a drink of honey" etc. And in a narration: "I drank honey at Zainab bint Jahsh's and I will not return to it", so was revealed: "Why do you forbid what Allah has made lawful for you?" (Al-Tahrim: 1) reported by al-Bukhari no. 6313, chapter on when he forbids food, and Muslim no. 1474, chapter on the obligation of expiation upon one who forbids his wife without intending divorce, and al-Nasa'i no. 3421, chapter on the interpretation of the saying of Allah: "O Prophet, why do you forbid what Allah has made lawful for you?" (Al-Tahrim: 1), and Abu Dawud no. 3714, chapter on the drink of honey, and Ahmad in his Musnad no. 25894, from Aisha. [PAGE 292] 291 Statement of the divisions of the third classification: Statement of the impossibility of combining the literal and metaphorical meanings. It is like an abandoned literal meaning; therefore it needs intention. And it is said: The oath is what is intended from the wording, and the vow is not intended, but rather comes in the form of the wording. But this is only valid if he intended only the oath. But if he intended both, then the vow falls under intention, even though it is not needed. And it is said: His saying "Allah" is like "by Allah"—a formula of oath, and his saying "upon me" is a formula of vow, so they cannot combine in one word. So it is like buying a close relative, for it is ownership by its wording (it is ownership) but manumission by its necessary consequence—a simile to the issue of the vow by way of clarification and support. For whoever buys a close relative acquires ownership by virtue of its wording, because its wording is placed for ownership, but it becomes manumission and emancipation by its necessary consequence, because the necessary consequence of ownership with kinship is emancipation. He ﷺ said: "Whoever owns a close relative who is mahram, that relative becomes free." Otherwise, there is apparent contradiction between purchase and emancipation. It is said: the speaker is the author of Al-Tawdih. (Al-Qamar) "it is not intended": so combination of literal and metaphorical in intention does not arise. (Al-Qamar) "under intention": then combination of literal and metaphorical in intention would arise. (Al-Qamar) And it is said: the speaker is Shams al-A'imma. (Al-Qamar) meaning "by Allah" as Ibn Abbas said: "Adam entered Paradise, and by Allah the sun had not set until he came out"—i.e., by Allah. And Ibn al-Malik said: One may respond that the prefix 'lam' comes for oath only when the context is exclamatory, as in Ibn Abbas's statement here, and this is stipulated in grammar books. (Al-Qamar) So they do not combine—i.e., the literal and metaphorical in one word, but rather in two words. However, this phrasing customarily prevails in the case of a bare vow, so it is interpreted as a vow. So if he intends both the oath and the vow, he intended with each word what it is capable of, so the intention operates. (Al-Qamar) "like buying" i.e., the oath after the addition of intention becomes like the emancipation of a relative, necessary for the vow. But there is consideration in this, no room to elaborate. (Al-Muhashi) "Whoever owns a close relative" etc. Abu Dawud narrated from Samura from the Prophet ﷺ: "Whoever owns a close relative who is mahram, that relative is free." (Al-Qamar) This wording was narrated by al-Bayhaqi and al-Nasa'i, and they declared it weak because Damrah alone reported it from Sufyan. Abd al-Haqq authenticated it and said: Damrah is trustworthy, and if a trustworthy person narrates a hadith with a complete chain, his isolation in reporting it does not harm, nor does the mursal of one who sends it, nor the mawquf of one who stops it. Ibn al-Qattan approved his statement, and among those who considered Damrah trustworthy are Ibn Ma'in and others, even if he is not relied upon in the two Sahihs. This is in Fath al-Qadir. [Ishraq al-Absar: 10] And Ahmad narrated in his Musnad no. 20179, and Abu Dawud no. 3949, chapter on whoever owns a close relative who is mahram, and al-Tirmidhi no. 1365, chapter on whoever owns a close relative who is mahram, and al-Tirmidhi said: This hadith is not known to us as marfu' except from the hadith of Hammad ibn Salama, and Ibn Majah no. 2524, chapter on whoever owns a close relative who is mahram, such a relative is free, from Samura ibn Jundab from the Prophet ﷺ that he said: "Whoever owns a close relative who is mahram, that relative is free." Ibn Hajar said: A group of memorizers of hadith held that it is mawquf. [Ishraq al-Absar: 10]
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage is a linguistic and legal analysis of the impossibility of combining literal (haqiqah) and figurative (majaz) meanings in a single intention. The context is the ruling on a person who says, "For Allah upon me, I will not do X" or similar, intending both an oath (yamin) and a vow (nadhr). The author refutes the possibility that one word can simultaneously convey both literal and metaphorical meanings in the same act of intention.

The discussion begins by citing the Quranic verse: "Why do you forbid what Allah has made lawful for you?" (Q. 66:1) and the following verse about expiation of oaths. From these, it is understood that forbidding the permissible is an oath, and thus it is a necessary consequence (mujab) of the utterance, not intended by way of metaphor. However, an objection arises: if it is a necessary consequence, it should be established without intention, because a necessary consequence does not require intention. The response is that the prohibition of the permissible established by the vow is not an oath customarily; the customary usage has stripped the meaning of oath from this phrasing, similar to how the meaning of oath is stripped from 'laghw oath' according to Imam al-Shafi'i. Therefore, intention is needed to make it an oath.

But then a further objection is raised: if we say that the prohibition is a necessary consequence of the vow (which is intended), and the oath meaning is also intended (by way of majaz), then we have combined haqiqah and majaz in the same intention, which is impossible. The author presents several responses. One response distinguishes between the literal meaning (vow) and the figurative meaning (oath) as being intended from different aspects of the wording (e.g., the word "Allah" is for oath, "upon me" for vow). Another response compares it to buying a close relative: the literal meaning is ownership, but the necessary consequence is emancipation; there is no conflict because one is the literal intent and the other is a legal consequence. Similarly, in a vow, the literal meaning is the obligation of the act, and the oath meaning is a consequence that arises only if intended.

The passage also provides historical context by recounting the occasions of revelation for the verses, including the Prophet's prohibition of Mariyah and of honey.

Overall, the passage argues that one cannot intend both haqiqah and majaz from the same word in a single act of intention, supporting a key Hanafi usul principle.

Key Terms
حقيقة (haqīqah): Literal meaning; the original, direct meaning of a word as established by custom (wad').
مجاز (majāz): Figurative meaning; the secondary, transferred meaning of a word that is not its original usage.
يمين (yamīn): Oath; a binding statement invoking Allah to confirm or emphasize a matter, which requires expiation (kaffārah) if broken.
نذر (nadhr): Vow; a commitment to perform an act of worship or abstinence for the sake of Allah.
موجب (mūjib): Necessary consequence; a legal effect that follows automatically from an action or utterance, regardless of intention.
استحالة (istiḥālah): Impossibility; the logical or legal impossibility of a given proposition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi analysis in this passage, what is the problem with intending both an oath (yamīn) and a vow (nadhr) from the phrase 'for Allah upon me to make something forbidden'?
A) It is possible because the meaning of oath is a necessary consequence of the vow.
B) It would require combining literal and figurative meanings in one intention, which is impossible.
C) It is allowed because the wording is ambiguous.
D) It is the only way to make the vow effective.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, one cannot intend both the literal and figurative meanings of the same word in a single act of intention; this principle governs the interpretation of ambiguous oath/vow utterances.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

12 min
Impossibility of Combining Haqiqah and Majaz in One Word: Oaths and Vows
Textual analysis of the impossibility of combining haqiqah and majaz

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that 'Allah upon me' cannot simultaneously be an oath and a vow, and apply the usul principle of impossibility of combining haqiqah and majaz to analogous phrases.

Core Idea
A single word cannot bear both literal and figurative meaning in one intention; intention or custom determines which applies.

In Hanafi usul, when a word has both a literal (haqiqah) and a figurative (majaz) meaning, they cannot be combined in a single utterance. This passage applies that rule to the phrase 'Allah upon me' used in oaths and vows, exploring differing scholarly views and drawing an analogy from a hadith about buying a mahram relative. Understanding this principle helps clarify many ambiguous expressions in oaths and vows.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Usul Principle
Recognize that the passage begins by asserting the impossibility of combining haqiqah and majaz in one word. This is a fundamental rule in Hanafi usul al-fiqh.
The term 'haqiqah' means literal meaning, 'majaz' figurative meaning.
2
Apply to the Phrase 'Allah upon me'
Note that the phrase can be interpreted as an oath (using 'Allah' as a swearing) or as a vow ('upon me' indicating obligation). The debate is whether one can intend both simultaneously.
3
Analyze the Textual Argument
Consider the view that 'Allah' is a form of oath and 'upon me' is a form of vow, so they are two separate words. This avoids combining haqiqah and majaz in one word.
The passage cites authorities like Shams al-A'imma and the author of al-Tawdih.
4
Incorporate Intention and Custom
Understand that if the phrase is customarily (ghalib) used for vows, it is treated as such unless the speaker specifies otherwise. Intention can specify one meaning but cannot create two simultaneous meanings in one word.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.
5
Examine the Analogy with Purchasing a Relative
The analogy with buying a mahram relative shows that a single word ('purchase') has a literal meaning (ownership) but its legal effect (muqtada) is emancipation. This does not constitute combining haqiqah and majaz; rather, it is a single literal meaning with a consequential legal effect.
The hadith 'Whoever owns a mahram relative, he is free' is cited as proof.

(القمر) ليس بمراد : فلا يلزم الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز في الإرادة. (القمر) تحت الإرادة: فلزم الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز في الإرادة. (القمر) وقيل: القائل شمس الأئمة. (القمر) بمعنى والله كما قال ابن عباس : دخل آدم الجنة، فلله ما غربت الشمس حتى خرج أي بالله وقال ابن الملك لقائل أن يقول: إن اللام إنما تجيء للقسم إذا كان الموضع موضع تعجب كما في قول ابن عباس ها، وقد نص على ذلك في كتب النحو. (القمر) فلا يجتمعان أي الحقيقة والمجاز في لفظ واحد بل في كلمتين إلا أن هذا الكلام غلب عند الإطلاق في النذر من عادة، فيحمل على النذر ، فإذا نوى اليمين والنذر ، فقد نوى بكل لفظ ما هو محتمل له فتعمل النية. (القمر) كشراء أي صار اليمين بعد انضمام النية مثل عتق القريب لازما للنذر ، وفيه نظر لا وسعة لبيانه لها. (المحشي) ملك ذا رحم إلخ روى أبو داود عن سمرة عن النبي من ملك ذا رحم محرم فهو حر". (القمر) هذا اللفظ رواه البيهقي والنسائي، وضعفاه بسبب أن ضمرة انفرد به عن سفيان، وصححه عبد الحق، وقال: ضمرة ثقة، وإذا أسند الحديث ثقة فلا يضر انفراده به ولا إرسال من أرسله ولا وقف من وقفه، وصوب ابن القطان کلامه وممن وثق ضمرة بن معين وغيره وإن لم يحتج به في الصحيحين هكذا في "فتح القدير". [إشراق الأبصار: ١٠] وروى أحمد في أحمد في مسنده :رقم: ۲۰۱۷۹ ، وأبو داود رقم: ٣٩٤۹، باب فيمن ملك ذا رحم محرم، والترمذي رقم ١٣٦٥ ، باب ما جاء فيمن ملك ذا رحم محرم، وقال الترمذي: هذا حديث لا نعرفه مسندًا إلا من حديث حماد سلمة، وابن ماجه رقم ٢٥٢٤ ، باب من ملك ذا رحم محرم فهو حر، عن سمرة بن جندب عن النبي أنه قال: من ملك ذا رحم محرم فهو حر . قال الشيخ ابن حجر: ورجح جمع من الحفاظ أنه موقوف. [إشراق الأبصار: ١٠] بن
Explanation of the sections of the third division: Explanation of the impossibility of combining haqiqah and majaz. It is like an abandoned truth, so it needs intention. And it is said: the oath is the intended meaning of the utterance, and the vow is not intended, but it comes in the form of the utterance. But this is correct only if he intended the oath alone. However, if he intended both, then the vow enters under the intention, even if it was not needed. And it is said: the word 'Allah' meaning 'by Allah' is the form of an oath, and the word 'upon me' is the form of a vow, so they are not combined in a single word. So it is like buying a relative: it constitutes possession by its wording, but emancipation by its implication, as a simile for the issue of the vow to clarify and support it. For one who buys a relative acquires possession by virtue of its wording, because its wording is placed for possession, but it is emancipation and manumission by its implication, because the implication of possession with kinship is emancipation. He said: 'Whoever owns a mahram relative, he becomes free'; otherwise there is an apparent contradiction between purchase and emancipation. And it is said: the speaker is the author of al-Tawdih. (al-Qamar) He did not intend [the vow]: so the combination of haqiqah and majaz in intention is not necessary. (al-Qamar) Under the intention: so the combination of haqiqah and majaz in intention is necessary. (al-Qamar) And it is said: the speaker is Shams al-A'imma. (al-Qamar) Meaning 'by Allah' as Ibn Abbas said: Adam entered Paradise, and by Allah the sun had not set until he left, i.e. by Allah. And Ibn al-Malik said that one could say that 'lam' only comes for oath when the context is one of wonder, as in Ibn Abbas's statement; and this has been stated in grammar books. (al-Qamar) So they are not combined, i.e. haqiqah and majaz in one word, but rather in two words. However, this phrase is predominantly used for vow by custom, so it is interpreted as a vow. If he intends both the oath and the vow, then he intended with each word what it can bear, so the intention is effective. (al-Qamar) Like purchase: i.e. the oath, after the intention is joined, becomes like the emancipation of a relative, binding for the vow. There is a consideration, but no space to elaborate. (al-Muhashi) 'Whoever owns a mahram...' Abu Dawud narrated from Samurah from the Prophet: 'Whoever owns a mahram relative, he is free.' (al-Qamar) This wording was narrated by al-Bayhaqi and al-Nasa'i, and they declared it weak due to Damrah's sole narration from Sufyan. Abd al-Haqq authenticated it, saying: Damrah is trustworthy; if a trustworthy person narrates a hadith, his sole narration does not harm it, nor does mursal or mawquf. Ibn al-Qattan verified his statement, and among those who declared Damrah trustworthy were Ibn Ma'in and others, even though it is not relied upon in the two Sahihs. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. [Ishraq al-Absar: 10] And Ahmad narrated in his Musnad: no. 20179, and Abu Dawud no. 3949, chapter on whoever owns a mahram relative, and al-Tirmidhi no. 1365, chapter on whoever owns a mahram relative, and al-Tirmidhi said: We do not know this hadith as musnad except through Hammad ibn Salamah. And Ibn Majah no. 2524, chapter on whoever owns a mahram relative is free, from Samurah ibn Jundub from the Prophet that he said: 'Whoever owns a mahram relative is free.' Shaykh Ibn Hajar said: A group of memorizers favored that it is mawquf. [Ishraq al-Absar: 10] (end of page)
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis from a Hanafi usul work discussing the impossibility of combining literal meaning (haqiqah) and figurative meaning (majaz) in a single word, specifically applied to oaths and vows. The author explores different scholarly opinions on whether the wording 'Allah' and 'upon me' can simultaneously constitute an oath and a vow. Some argue that 'Allah' is inherently an oath form and 'upon me' is a vow form, so they cannot be combined in one expression. Others hold that intention determines the meaning. The author draws an analogy with buying a mahram relative: the act of purchase (shira') is literally a possession contract, but its legal effect (muqtada) is emancipation due to kinship, resulting in an apparent contradiction between the literal act and its implication. This analogy illustrates how a single utterance can carry both literal and figurative import, but the combination is resolved through intention or legal effect. The hadith 'Whoever owns a mahram relative, he is free' is cited as a proof (dalil) and discussed regarding its authenticity (thubut), noting differences among scholars. This exemplifies the Hanafi method of integrating usul principles with substantive fiqh examples.

Key Terms
الحقيقة (al-haqiqah): The literal, original meaning of a word as it was coined for.
المجاز (al-majaz): The figurative use of a word for a meaning other than its original, with a relation (علاقة) and a preventing indication (مانع).
اليمين (al-yamin): An oath, typically invoking Allah's name to affirm or deny something.
النذر (al-nadhr): A vow, making an act obligatory upon oneself by saying e.g. 'upon me for Allah'.
الموجب (al-muqtada / al-mujib): The legal effect or implication of a contract or utterance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi analysis in this passage, what is the ruling on a person who says 'Allah upon me' without any specific intention?
A) The phrase is void because it cannot combine two meanings in one word.
B) It is valid as both an oath and a vow because the words are general.
C) It is treated as a vow only, because the phrase is customarily used for vows.
D) It is treated as an oath only, because the word 'Allah' is specifically for oaths.

Key Takeaway
A single word cannot combine haqiqah and majaz in one intention; in ambiguous oaths/vows, custom determines the default, and intention can specify but not duplicate meanings.

Majaz and Isti'arah in Hanafi Usul: Definitions and Relations
Defining isti'arah as synonymous with majaz in usul and listing the twenty-five relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi usul definition of isti'arah from the balagha definition and identify the twenty-five relations of majaz.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, isti'arah equals majaz; all figurative usage requires a recognized relation.

This passage clarifies a crucial terminological distinction between the usul and balagha perspectives on isti'arah. While in rhetoric isti'arah is a subset of majaz requiring similitude, Hanafi usul treats isti'arah as synonymous with any figurative usage that has a valid relation. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly interpreting legal texts, where the presence of a recognized relation justifies a non-literal reading. The passage also systematically enumerates the twenty-five relations that can exist between literal and figurative meanings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the terminological dispute
Note the difference: in usul al-fiqh, isti'arah is synonymous with majaz; in balagha, isti'arah is a subset of majaz requiring the relation of similitude. This is the central point of the passage.
The text explicitly states: 'والاستعارة في عرف الأصوليين يرادف المجاز'.
2
Define the 'way' of isti'arah
The 'way' (طريق) is the connection between literal and figurative meanings, either in form or meaning. This connection is the basis for figurative usage.
The text says: 'وطريق الاستعارة الاتصال بين الشيئين صورة أو معنى'.
3
Survey the four divisions of isti'arah in balagha
Although the Hanafi usul view does not require these divisions, the text lists them for context: kinaayah, takhiliyyah, tasrihiyyah, tarshihiyyah. Recognize that these are irrelevant in usul interpretation.
These divisions are from the science of balagha, not usul.
4
Enumerate the twenty-five relations of majaz
The text provides examples of each relation type, such as cause-effect, whole-part, necessary-necessity, etc. For each, understand the mechanism that allows the figurative shift.
Examples include using 'rain' for 'plants' (cause-effect) and 'neck' for 'self' (part-whole).
5
Apply to legal interpretation
Given a figurative expression in a legal text, identify which relation justifies the non-literal meaning and confirm that a qarinah (preventing indication) exists.
The Hanafi rule: no figurative interpretation without a relation and a qarinah.

(القمر) من صورة أو معنى الترديد على سبيل منع الخلو، فيجوز أن يكون الاتصال صورة ومعنى معا. (القمر) البيان: هو علم من علوم البلاغة. (القمر) يسمى استعارة كاستعمال الأسد في الرجل الشجاع لمشابهته إياه في الشجاعة. (القمر) بأقسامها وهي أربعة الكناية وهي تشبيه شيء بشيءٍ في النفس، وترك جميع أركانه أي المشبه والمشبه به ووجه التشبيه وحرف التشبيه سوى المشبه والتخيلية وهي إثبات لازم المشبه به المتروك للمشبه والتصريحية وهو ذكر المشبه به وإرادة المشبه والترشيحية وهو إثبات ملائم المشبه به للمشبه. Explanation of the sections of the third division [page 292] Explanation of the relations of majaz [margin: Explanation of the relations of majaz] Then when the author finished the sub-branches, he began to explain the relations of majaz, saying: The way of isti'arah is the connection between two things in form or meaning. And isti'arah in the terminology of the usuliyyun is synonymous with majaz, while according to the scholars of balagha it is a type of majaz. For majaz according to them: if it contains the relation of similitude (tashbih), it is called isti'arah with its divisions; if it contains a relation other than similitude from the twenty-five relations, such as cause and effect, condition and locus, similitude is called... [he] began explaining the relations. This is a refutation of the misconception that the author from here is explaining the states of isti'arah, which is a type of majaz. Since he was intending to explain majaz as the counterpart of haqiqah, he should explain the states of majaz unconditionally, whichever type of majaz it is. For it is mentioned in the science of balagha that majaz is divided into mursal and isti'arah. If the relation is a ground [for similitude], it is isti'arah; otherwise it is mursal. He indicated by this speech that it is refuted because this distinction is according to the terminology of balagha. However, according to the terminology of usul, isti'arah and majaz are synonymous. (al-Sinbali) 'Between two things': i.e. the literal meaning and the figurative meaning. (al-Qamar) 'In form or meaning' is disjunctively [meaning] to prevent vacuity; so it is possible that the connection be in form and meaning together. (al-Qamar) Al-Bayan: it is a science from the sciences of balagha. (al-Qamar) 'It is called isti'arah' like using 'lion' for a brave man due to similarity in bravery. (al-Qamar) Its divisions: they are four: al-kinaayah: which is likening a thing to another in the mind, leaving all its pillars except the likened; al-takhiliyyah: which is affirming a necessary property of the omitted likened-to for the likened; al-tasrihiyyah: which is mentioning the likened-to and intending the likened; al-tarshihiyyah: which is affirming a compatible property of the likened-to for the likened. (al-Qamar) The twenty-five relations: using the name of the cause for the effect, like using 'rain' for plants; its opposite like using 'wine' for grapes; using the name of the whole for the part, like 'fingers' for fingertips; its opposite like using 'neck' for the self; using the name of the necessary for the necessity, like 'speech' for indication; its opposite like 'tightening the waist-wrapper' for abstaining from women; using the name of the qualified for the absolute, like 'shuqar' (which is the lip of a camel) for the absolute lip; its opposite like 'day' for the Day of Judgment; using the name of the specific for the general, its opposite and their examples are obvious; deletion of the added and substituting the addressee in its place, like 'Ask the village: its people'; deletion of the addressee due to adjacency, like 'waterspout' for water; naming a thing by what it results in, like calling a student 'virtuous'; naming a thing by what it was, like calling an adult 'orphan'; using the name of the place for what is in it, like 'pitcher' for water; its opposite like 'in the mercy of Allah' i.e. Paradise, for it is the locus of mercy; using the name of the instrument of a thing for it, like 'tongue' for remembrance; using one of two substitutes for the other =
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage is an usul definition from a Hanafi commentary explaining the relations (علاقات) of majaz (figurative language). The author begins by clarifying that in usul terminology, isti'arah (metaphor) is synonymous with majaz, unlike the science of balagha (rhetoric) where isti'arah is a subclass of majaz requiring the relation of similitude (tashbih). The Hanafi usul view takes a broader approach: any figurative usage with a relation qualifies as majaz/isti'arah. The text then defines the 'way' (طريق) of isti'arah as a connection between literal and figurative meanings in form or meaning. It lists the four divisions of isti'arah according to balagha: kinaayah, takhiliyyah, tasrihiyyah, and tarshihiyyah. Finally, it enumerates the twenty-five relations (علاقات) that can exist between literal and figurative senses, giving examples for each: cause-effect, whole-part, necessary-necessity, qualified-absolute, specific-general, deletion of mudaf, adjacency, result, former state, place, instrument, and substitution. This detailed classification shows the systematic approach of Hanafi usul in analyzing the linguistic mechanisms underlying figurative usage, which is essential for correctly interpreting legal texts.

Key Terms
الاستعارة (al-isti'ara): The connection between literal and figurative meanings; synonymous with majaz in usul terminology.
علاقات المجاز (alaqat al-majaz): The relationships between literal and figurative meanings that justify figurative usage, such as cause-effect, whole-part, etc.
المجاز المرسل (al-majaz al-mursal): Figurative usage with a relation other than similitude (as per balagha).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, isti'arah is synonymous with majaz; any figurative usage with a recognized relation is permissible for legal interpretation.

Cause-Effect Metaphor in Legal Speech: When the Cause Stands for the Effect
Expansion of the cause-effect type of majaz mursal and its conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a metaphorical usage of cause for effect (or vice versa) is valid in Hanafi legal speech and justify your answer using the rule of sabab and musabbab.

Core Idea
Using the cause to express the effect is valid; using the effect to express the cause is not, because the effect depends on the cause.

In this passage, the author expands on the second type of majaz mursal: the relation of cause and effect. Understanding this relation is crucial for correctly interpreting legal utterances that are not literal. The key rule is that you may use the cause (sabab) to refer to the effect (musabbab), but not the reverse. We will explore the definition, the rationale, and apply it to concrete examples from Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Define Sabab and Musabbab
Identify the technical meanings: sabab is a means to the ruling, not the 'illa; musabbab is the legal effect. Note the distinction from 'illa.
The sabab is a path to the ruling; obligation and existence are not attributed to it.
2
State the Core Rule
From the text: 'فصح استعارة السبب للحكم دون عكسه' – it is valid to borrow the cause for the effect, but not the opposite.
This is the central legal principle.
3
Analyze the Rationale
The effect depends on the cause for its existence (shar'an), but the cause does not depend on the effect. Therefore, using the cause to imply the effect is natural, but using the effect to imply the cause is misleading.
Example: emancipation (cause) leads to removal of marriage rights (effect).
4
Apply to Key Examples
Work through the given examples: 'anta hurra' (cause) intended for talaq (effect) is valid; 'anta taliq' (effect) intended for 'hurra' is invalid. Also, sale (cause) for marriage (effect) valid; marriage for sale invalid.
These examples illustrate the rule in practice.
5
Distinguish from 'Illa
Note that the sabab is not the direct 'illa; there may be an intervening 'illa. For instance, the statement 'anta hurra' is a cause, but the 'illa of emancipation is the removal of ownership.
This refinement prevents confusion in istihsan and qiyas.

Source Text

بيان علاقات المجاز

(القمر) لأنه نوى إلخ لا لأنه لا يصح الاستعارة، فإن الاستعارة تصح كما مر . (القمر)
فيصير متهما : لأنه يحتمل أنه قال كاذبًا تخفيفًا عليه : إني نويت الملك بالشراء. (القمر)
في الصورة الأولى: أي فيما إذا نوى الشراء بالملك. (القمر)
لذكر القضاء أي لم يذكر أنه يصدق قضاء أم لا بل سكت عنه. (السنبلي)
سواء في أنه إلخ فيعتق النصف الثاني في الوجهين أعني الملك والشراء. (القمر)
11
والوصف في الحاضر لغو : كمن حلف لا يدخل هذه الدار لا يعتبر فيها صفة العمران وتعتبر في غير المعينة. (القمر) لغو إلخ لأن الوصف للتعيين، وإذا قال: "هذا العبد صار معينًا، فيكون الوصف لغوا. (السنبلي) ما يكون طريقا إلخ كقوله: "أنت حرة" فإنه سبب للحكم، وطريق مفض إليه وهو زوال ملك المتعة، وليس بمضاف إليه، بل هو مضاف إلى علته وهو زوال ملك الرقبة وهذه العلة واسطة بين السبب والحكم. (القمر) إليه: العائد يرجع إلى يرجع إلى "ما" وكذا ضمير "فيه" و "بينه". (القمر) وجوب ولا وجود أي وجوب الحكم ولا وجوده قيل: بلفظ الوجوب احترز عن العلة، وبلفظ الوجود احترز عن الشرط. (القمر)

بيان أقسام التقسيم الثالث ولا تعقل فيه معاني العلل لكن يتخلل بينه وبين الحكم علة يضاف إليها كما سيأتي، كاتصال زوال ملك المتعة بزوال ملك الرقبة، فإنه إذا قال لأمته: "أنت حرة" يزول به
أي بقوله: أنت حرةً
ملك الرقبة، وبواسطة زواله يزول ملك المتعة، فلا يحل الوطء بعده إلا بالنكاح، وهكذا اتصال ثبوت ملك المتعة بثبوت ملك الرقبة بأن يقول: اشتريت هذه الأمة" فيثبت به ملك الرقبة، وبواسطة ثبوته يثبت ملك المتعة.
"أنت طالق"
فيصح استعارة السبب للحكم دون عكسه بأن يقول: "أنت حرة" ويريد به أو تقول: "بعت نفسي منك وتريد به النكاح، ولا يجوز أن يقول: "أنت طالق" ويريد أنت حرة، وأن يقول: "نكحتُك" ويريد بعتكِ؛ لأن المسبب محتاج إلى السبب من حيث الثبوت، والسبب لا يحتاج إلى المسبب من حيث الشرعية؛ لأن العتاق لم يشرع إلا لأجل زوال ملك الرقبة وزوال ملك المتعة إنما حصل معه اتفاقًا في بعض الأحيان،

(السنبلي) يضاف إليها أي يضاف الحكم إلى العلة، وفي بعض النسخ: لا تضاف إليه أي لا تضاف العلة إلى السبب. (القمر) كما سيأتي: أي عن قريب في ذيل شرح قول المصنف كاتصال .إلخ (القمر) بثبوت ملك الرقبة: أي بقوله: اشتريت هذه الأمة. (القمر) أنت حرة أي يقول لزوجته: "أنت" "حرة وفيه زوال ملك الرقبة، ويريد به أنت طالق وفيه زوال ملك المتعة، فاستعير السبب للمسبب فيصح (القمر) بعت نفسي منك أي تقول المرأة: بعت نفسي منك"، وفيه ثبوت ملك الرقبة وتريد به النكاح وفيه ثبوت ملك المتعة فاستعير السبب للمسبب فيصح . (القمر) أنت طالق أي يقول لأمته: أنت طالق" ويريد "أنت حرة"، المسبب للسبب فلا يصح (القمر) نكحتك أي يقول: "نكحتك"، ويريد "بعتك"، فاستعير المسبب للسبب فلا يصح (القمر) من حيث الشرعية أي لم يشرع السبب لذلك المسبب؛ لأن العتاق إلخ. (القمر) في بعض الأحيان أي فيما إذا أعتق جارية لا فيما إذا أعتق عبدا. (القمر)
فاستعير
Explanation of the relations of metaphor. Explanation of the sections of the third division in this last [case]... And the second type: the connection of the cause to the effect. The intended meaning of 'cause' is that which is not a cause (illa) to which the ruling is attributed. In terminology, it is that which is a path to the ruling, and neither obligation nor existence is attributed to it... So it is valid to borrow the cause for the effect (using the cause to mean the effect) but not the opposite, because the effect needs the cause in terms of existence, while the cause does not need the effect in terms of legislation; for emancipation was only legislated for the removal of ownership of the neck, and the removal of the right to enjoy (mul'at) only occurs accidentally in some cases.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the second type of majaz mursal (metonymy) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: the relation of cause and effect (al-sabab wa al-musabbab). The author defines 'cause' (sabab) as that which is a means to a legal ruling but not the direct 'illa (ratio legis). For example, saying 'you are free' (anta hurra) is a cause for the legal effect of emancipation (zawal mamlakat al-raqaba). However, the cause is distinct from the 'illa; the 'illa is the intermediate cause (like removal of ownership of the neck) that directly brings about the ruling. The key rule derived is that it is permissible to use the cause to express the effect (istihdam al-sabab li al-musabbab) but not vice versa. The rationale is that the effect (musabbab) depends on the cause for its existence, but the cause does not depend on the effect in terms of shar'i legislation. Examples: saying 'anta hurra' intending talaq (divorce) is valid because the cause (emancipation) implies the effect (removal of right to enjoy) which is similar to divorce. Conversely, saying 'anta taliq' intending 'anta hurra' is invalid because talaq is an effect and cannot stand for its cause. Similarly, in a contract, saying 'birtu nafsi minka' (I sold myself to you) intending nikah (marriage) is valid (sale is cause, marriage is effect), but saying 'nakahtuki' intending 'birtuki' is not. The passage also clarifies that the cause in such majaz must have a real connection; if an 'illa intervenes, the cause may become like an 'illa itself, as in istihsan.

Key Terms
السبب (as-sabab): The cause: a means leading to a legal ruling without being the direct illa (ratio legis). It is a path to the ruling but not attributed with obligation or existence.
المسبب (al-musabbab): The effect: the legal consequence that results from the cause.
المجاز المرسل (al-majaz al-mursal): Metaphor by association: a figurative usage where a word is used in a meaning connected to its original meaning by a relation other than similarity, such as cause-effect, part-whole, etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi usul text, which of the following metaphorical usages is considered valid?
A) Saying 'anta hurra' (you are free) intending 'anta taliq' (you are divorced).
B) Saying 'anta taliq' (you are divorced) intending 'anta hurra' (you are free).
C) Saying 'nakahtuki' (I married you) intending 'birtuki' (I sold myself to you).
D) Saying 'anta taliq' to mean freeing a non-wife slave.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, it is valid to use the cause (sabab) to express the effect (musabbab), but not the opposite, because the effect depends on the cause.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
8 min
The Hanafi Rule on Metaphorical Usage Between Cause and Effect
Establishes the rule for metaphorical usage between cause and effect with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on metaphorical usage between cause and effect, identify valid and invalid examples, and apply the exception when the effect is specific to the cause.

Core Idea
Cause can stand for effect, but effect cannot stand for cause, unless the effect is unique to that cause.

The Hanafi school of usul al-fiqh establishes a clear rule regarding metaphorical usage (isti'arah) when a cause-effect relationship exists. This principle governs how legal expressions are interpreted, ensuring that the intended legal effect is accurately derived. In this lesson, you will learn the rule, its exceptions, and how to apply it to examples from emancipation and divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two directions of metaphorical transference
There are two possibilities: using the word for the cause to mean the effect, and using the word for the effect to mean the cause. The passage establishes different rulings for each.
The cause is the sabab, the effect is the musabbab.
2
State the default Hanafi rule
It is valid to use the cause metaphorically for the effect. Example: saying 'You are free' (cause: emancipation) to one's wife intending divorce (effect: dissolution of marriage). But it is invalid to use the effect for the cause generally.
The effect depends on the cause; the cause does not depend on the effect.
3
Recognize the exception
If the effect is specific to the cause (i.e., it only comes from that cause), then using the effect for the cause is valid. The Quranic verse 'I see myself pressing wine' (grapes) is the proof: wine only comes from grapes, so 'wine' can mean 'grapes'.
This exception is based on necessity and specificity.
4
Apply the rule to examples
From the passage: 'You are free' intending divorce is valid (cause→effect). 'You are divorced' intending emancipation is invalid (effect→cause). Similarly, saying 'I sold myself to you' intending marriage is valid (cause→effect), but 'I married you' intending sale is invalid.
The legal effect of emancipation is removal of ownership; removal of right of enjoyment is incidental.

بيان أقسام التقسيم الثالث
. وكذا البيع إنما شرع لملك الرقبة، وحل الوطء إنما حصل معه اتفاقا في بعض الأحوال، فلا يجوز أن يذكر المسبب ويراد به السبب، إلا إذا كان المسبب مختصا بالسبب كقوله تعالى: ﴿إِنِّي أَرَانِي أَعْصِرُ حَمْراً﴾ فإن الخمر لا يكون إلا من العنب، فيجئ الافتقار من الجانبين، وقال الشافعي له: يجوز استعارة العتاق للطلاق وبالعكس، لأن كلا منهما يبتني على السراية واللزوم، فيدخلان في الاتصال المعنوي، ونحن نقول: الطلاق موضوع لرفع القيد، والعتاق موضوع لإثبات القوة، فلا يتشابهان أصلاً،
وكذا البيع إلخ صورته رجل زوّج أمته من رجل آخر ثم اشتراها منه رجل ثالث، ففي هذه الصورة وجد ملك الرقبة بدون ملك المتعة؛ لأنها منكوحة الغير هذا معنى قول المصنف : وكذا البيع إلخ. (السنبلي) في بعض الأحوال أي فيما إذا كان المبيع أمة (القمر) فلا يجوز أن يذكر إلخ فلا يصح استعارة الحكم كالطلاق للسبب الذي هو الحرية، وههنا قلق، فإن قوله تعالى: ﴿فَإِذَا قَرَأْتَ الْقُرْآنَ فَاسْتَعِذْ بِاللَّهِ﴾ الآية، معناه : إذا أردت قراءة القرآن إلخ، والإرادة سبب للقراءة وليست بعلة له؛ فإن الإرادة قد تنفك عن المراد، والعلة لا تنفك عن المعلول، فقد تحقق استعارة المسبب للسبب. (القمر) مختصا إلخ فحينئذ يكون السبب في معنى العلة، فكان السبب موضوع ومشروع لهذا المسبب، فحصل الافتقار من الجانبين كذا قيل.(القمر) كقوله تعالى : أي حاكيًا عن قول الفتى الذي دخل مع يوسف في السجن إنِّي أَرَانِي أَعْصِرُ حَمْرا (يوسف: ٣٦) أي عنبا والعنب سبب للخمر، فاستعير المسبب للسبب؛ لاختصاص المسبب بالسبب، لأن الخمر هو التي من ماء العنب إذا غلى واشتد وقذف بالزبد كما مر ويمكن أن يقال: إن الخمر اسم للعنب ببعض اللغات، فلعله يكون هذا الكلام وارداً على لغتهم، فحينئذ لا مجاز في الكلام، وأن يقال هذه الاستعارة من قبيل تسمية الشيء باعتبار ما يؤل إليه، فالعنب سمي حمرا باعتبار ما يؤل إليه فلا يكون حينئذٍ استعارة المسبب للسبب. (القمر) للطلاق أي يذكر العتاق ويراد به الطلاق. (القمر) وبالعكس أي يذكر الطلاق ويراد به العتاق. (القمر) على السراية واللزوم المراد بالسراية: ثبوت الحكم في الكل بسبب ثبوته في البعض بأن يقول مثلاً: "نصفك طالق أو وجهك حر"، والمراد باللزوم: عدم قبول الفسخ (القمر) فيدخلان إلخ لاشتراكهما في المعنى أي الإزالة للملك. (القمر) موضوع لإثبات إلخ فيه أنه لا يفهم شرعًا وعرفًا من الإعتاق إلا إزالة الملك، والخلاص عن الرّق، فهو الموضوع له لا إثبات القوة كالمالكية وأهلية الشهادة، فيكون العتاق والطلاق حينئذٍ متشابهين؛ لأن كلاً منهما
Explanation of the relations of metaphor. Explanation of the divisions of the third division: causes are not understood in their literal meanings, but between them and the ruling there is a cause (illa) to which [the ruling] is attributed, as will come. Like the connection of the removal of the right of enjoyment (milk al-mut'a) with the removal of ownership of the corpus (milk al-raqaba): when he says to his slave-girl 'You are free', ownership of the corpus is removed by [his saying], and by means of its removal the right of enjoyment is removed, so intercourse is not permissible after it except through marriage. Similarly, the connection of the establishment of the right of enjoyment with the establishment of ownership of the corpus: when he says 'I bought this slave-girl', ownership of the corpus is established by it, and by means of its establishment the right of enjoyment is established. 'You are divorced': it is valid to metaphorically use the cause for the effect but not the opposite. Such as saying 'You are free' intending divorce, or the woman saying 'I sold myself to you' intending marriage. It is not permissible to say 'You are divorced' intending 'you are free', nor to say 'I married you' intending 'I sold you', because the effect is dependent on the cause in terms of existence, while the cause is not dependent on the effect in terms of legislation. For emancipation is legislated only for the removal of ownership of the corpus, while the removal of the right of enjoyment occurs with it incidentally in some cases. [Commentary: 'between the ruling and the cause' etc. is a precaution against the cause of the cause. For the real cause (sabab) according to the usul scholars is that which has no effect at all in the existence of the ruling. If it has an effect, as when a cause intervenes between the sabab and the ruling, then that sabab is treated as a cause (illa). For example, emancipation is a cause for the removal of the right of enjoyment, as will come in the discussion of qiyas. (al-Sunbuli): 'attributed to it' means the ruling is attributed to the illa. In some copies: 'not attributed to him' meaning the illa is not attributed to the sabab. (al-Qamar): 'as will come' i.e. shortly at the end of the commentary on the author's statement 'like the connection'. (al-Qamar): 'by the establishment of ownership of the corpus' i.e. by his saying 'I bought this slave-girl'. (al-Qamar): 'You are free' means he says to his wife 'You are free' which includes removal of ownership of the corpus, intending 'you are divorced' which includes removal of the right of enjoyment, so the cause is used metaphorically for the effect and it is valid. (al-Qamar): 'I sold myself to you' means the woman says 'I sold myself to you' which includes establishment of ownership of the corpus, intending marriage which includes establishment of the right of enjoyment, so the cause is used metaphorically for the effect and it is valid. (al-Qamar): 'You are divorced' means he says to his slave-girl 'You are divorced' intending 'You are free', using the effect for the cause, so it is invalid. (al-Qamar): 'I married you' means he says 'I married you' intending 'I sold you', using the effect for the cause, so it is invalid. (al-Qamar): 'in terms of legislation' means the cause is not legislated for that effect; because emancipation etc. (al-Qamar): 'in some cases' i.e. when he emancipates a female slave, not when he emancipates a male slave. (al-Qamar): So it is used metaphorically. Explanation of the divisions of the third division. And likewise, sale is legislated only for ownership of the corpus, while the permissibility of intercourse occurs with it incidentally in some circumstances. So it is not permissible to mention the effect and intend the cause, except when the effect is specific to the cause, like the saying of Allah Most High: 'I see myself pressing grapes' (Yusuf 12:36), for wine comes only from grapes, so dependence arises from both sides. Al-Shafi'i held that it is permissible to use emancipation metaphorically for divorce and vice versa, because each is based on permeation (saraya) and irrevocability (luzum), so they fall under spiritual connection. But we (Hanafis) say: divorce is placed for removing a restriction, while emancipation is placed for establishing strength, so they are not similar at all. The example of sale etc.: a man marries his slave-girl to another man, then a third man buys her from him. In this situation, ownership of the corpus exists without the right of enjoyment because she is the wife of another. This is the meaning of the author's statement 'and likewise sale etc.' (al-Sunbuli): 'in some circumstances' i.e. when the sold item is a female slave. (al-Qamar): 'so it is not permissible to mention etc.' i.e. it is not valid to use the effect (like divorce) metaphorically for the cause (which is freedom). And here there is unease: for the verse 'When you recite the Quran, seek refuge with Allah' (An-Nahl 16:98) means 'when you intend to recite the Quran' etc. Intention is a cause for recitation, not a cause (illa) for it, for intention can be separate from the intended, while the cause (illa) is not separate from the effect. Thus the metaphorical use of the effect for the cause has occurred. (al-Qamar): 'specific' etc. then the cause is in the sense of an illa. So the cause is placed and legislated for this effect, so dependence exists from both sides, as was said. (al-Qamar): 'like His saying' i.e. narrating the statement of the youth who entered with Yusuf into prison: 'I see myself pressing wine' (Yusuf 12:36) meaning grapes, and grapes are the cause of wine, so the effect is used metaphorically for the cause due to the specificity of the effect to the cause, because wine is that which comes from grape juice when it boils and becomes strong and throws foam as has passed. It can also be said that 'wine' is a name for grapes in some languages, so this speech might be in their language, and then there is no metaphor in the speech. And it can be said that this metaphor is of the type of naming a thing by that which it will become: grapes are called wine by virtue of what they become, so then it is not a metaphor of using the effect for the cause. (al-Qamar): 'for divorce' i.e. he mentions emancipation intending divorce. (al-Qamar): 'and vice versa' i.e. he mentions divorce intending emancipation. (al-Qamar): 'on permeation and irrevocability' – permeation means the establishment of the ruling over the whole due to its establishment over a part, e.g. saying 'half of you is divorced' or 'your face is free'. Irrevocability means not being subject to cancellation. (al-Qamar): 'so they fall under' i.e. due to their sharing in the meaning of removal of ownership. (al-Qamar): 'placed for establishing' etc. – there is [objection] that from emancipation, only removal of ownership and release from slavery is understood legally and customarily, so that is its placement, not establishing strength like ownership and eligibility for testimony. Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage is a usul discussion of the permissibility of metaphorical usage (isti'arah) between a cause (sabab) and its effect (musabbab) in the context of legal rulings. The commentator, likely from the Hanafi school, expands on the author's classification of the third type of relational cause (al-taqsim al-thalith). 

The key principle established is that it is valid to use the cause metaphorically to refer to the effect, but not vice versa, unless the effect is specific to the cause. The reasoning is that the effect depends on the cause for its existence, but the cause is not dependent on the effect in terms of legislation. For example, when a master says to his slave-girl 'You are free', the intended legal effect is the removal of ownership of the corpus, but by implication the right of enjoyment also ends. Since emancipation is the direct cause of removal of ownership, it can be used metaphorically for the removal of the right of enjoyment (divorce-like effect). Similarly, saying 'I bought this slave-girl' establishes ownership, so it can be used to imply the right of enjoyment. Conversely, using the effect (e.g., 'You are divorced') to intend the cause (emancipation) is invalid because the effect is not legislated to produce the cause.

The commentator also addresses an exception: if the effect is exclusively specific to the cause, then metaphorical usage from effect to cause is permissible, as in the Quranic verse 'I see myself pressing wine' (Yusuf 12:36), where 'wine' (the effect) is used to mean 'grapes' (the cause), because wine only comes from grapes. The commentator notes alternative explanations: that 'wine' may be a name for grapes in some languages, or that it is naming something by its future state, thus avoiding metaphor from effect to cause.

A significant inter-madhhab dispute is presented: Imam al-Shafi'i permits mutual metaphorical usage between emancipation and divorce due to their shared characteristics of permeation (seraya) and irrevocability (luzum). The Hanafi position rejects this, arguing that divorce is for removing a restriction (qayd) while emancipation is for establishing strength (quwa) — thus they are fundamentally distinct and not interchangeable.

The passage uses marginal notes from al-Sunbuli and al-Qamar, which clarify terms and provide additional examples.

Key Terms
سبب (sabab): The cause that leads to a ruling; e.g., emancipation is the cause for removal of ownership.
مسبب (musabbab): The legal effect; e.g., divorce is the effect of the cause of emancipation.
استعارة (isti'arah): Metaphorical usage based on a relation such as cause-effect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi rule, which of the following metaphorical uses is valid?
A) Saying 'You are divorced' to one's slave-girl intending emancipation.
B) Saying 'You are free' to one's wife intending divorce.
C) Saying 'I married you' to a vendor intending to buy an item.
D) Saying 'You are my slave' to one's wife intending she is free.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi usul rule on metaphorical usage between cause and effect is that the cause can stand for the effect, but the effect cannot stand for the cause unless the effect is unique to the cause.
