The Dispute with al-Shafi'i: Are Emancipation and Divorce Interchangeable?
Presents the inter-madhhab dispute on metaphorical interchangeability between emancipation and divorce.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on metaphorical interchangeability between emancipation and divorce, and evaluate the Hanafi response.

Core Idea
Emancipation and divorce are fundamentally different: one establishes strength, the other removes a restriction.

Beyond the general rule, Imam al-Shafi'i argued that emancipation and divorce share enough characteristics to allow mutual metaphorical substitution. The Hanafi school firmly rejects this, emphasizing the distinct legal purposes of each act. This dispute illustrates how usul principles like cause-effect and linguistic analysis shape actual legal rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the Shafi'i position
Al-Shafi'i holds that it is permissible to use emancipation (itq) metaphorically for divorce (talaq) and vice versa, because both involve permeation (saraya) and irrevocability (luzum).
Permeation: the ruling spreads from part to whole. Irrevocability: cannot be rescinded.
2
State the Hanafi position
Hanafis reject this, arguing that the legal purposes are different: divorce removes a restriction, emancipation establishes strength. They are not similar.
The Hanafi position is that metaphor from cause to effect is valid, but not between two different legal acts.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response points out that emancipation is legislated only for removal of ownership, not for establishing strength. The incidental effects (like removal of right of enjoyment) are not its purpose.
The thamarah al-khilaf: if one says 'You are divorced' intending emancipation, it does not work; if one says 'You are free' intending divorce, it works (cause for effect).
4
Determine the practical consequence
The practical difference: a person who uses 'you are divorced' for a slave-girl does not effect emancipation; a person who says 'you are free' to his wife effects divorce.
This underscores the importance of understanding linguistic usage in legal acts.

Explanation of the relations of metaphor. Explanation of the divisions of the third division: causes are not understood in their literal meanings, but between them and the ruling there is a cause (illa) to which [the ruling] is attributed, as will come. Like the connection of the removal of the right of enjoyment (milk al-mut'a) with the removal of ownership of the corpus (milk al-raqaba): when he says to his slave-girl 'You are free', ownership of the corpus is removed by [his saying], and by means of its removal the right of enjoyment is removed, so intercourse is not permissible after it except through marriage. Similarly, the connection of the establishment of the right of enjoyment with the establishment of ownership of the corpus: when he says 'I bought this slave-girl', ownership of the corpus is established by it, and by means of its establishment the right of enjoyment is established. 'You are divorced': it is valid to metaphorically use the cause for the effect but not the opposite. Such as saying 'You are free' intending divorce, or the woman saying 'I sold myself to you' intending marriage. It is not permissible to say 'You are divorced' intending 'you are free', nor to say 'I married you' intending 'I sold you', because the effect is dependent on the cause in terms of existence, while the cause is not dependent on the effect in terms of legislation. Commentary
This passage is a usul discussion of the permissibility of metaphorical usage (isti'arah) between a cause (sabab) and its effect (musabbab) in the context of legal rulings. The commentator, likely from the Hanafi school, expands on the author's classification of the third type of relational cause (al-taqsim al-thalith). 

The key principle established is that it is valid to use the cause metaphorically to refer to the effect, but not vice versa, unless the effect is specific to the cause. The reasoning is that the effect depends on the cause for its existence, but the cause is not dependent on the effect in terms of legislation. For example, when a master says to his slave-girl 'You are free', the intended legal effect is the removal of ownership of the corpus, but by implication the right of enjoyment also ends. Since emancipation is the direct cause of removal of ownership, it can be used metaphorically for the removal of the right of enjoyment (divorce-like effect). Similarly, saying 'I bought this slave-girl' establishes ownership, so it can be used to imply the right of enjoyment. Conversely, using the effect (e.g., 'You are divorced') to intend the cause (emancipation) is invalid because the effect is not legislated to produce the cause.

The commentator also addresses an exception: if the effect is exclusively specific to the cause, then metaphorical usage from effect to cause is permissible, as in the Quranic verse 'I see myself pressing wine' (Yusuf 12:36), where 'wine' (the effect) is used to mean 'grapes' (the cause), because wine only comes from grapes. The commentator notes alternative explanations: that 'wine' may be a name for grapes in some languages, or that it is naming something by its future state, thus avoiding metaphor from effect to cause.

A significant inter-madhhab dispute is presented: Imam al-Shafi'i permits mutual metaphorical usage between emancipation and divorce due to their shared characteristics of permeation (seraya) and irrevocability (luzum). The Hanafi position rejects this, arguing that divorce is for removing a restriction (qayd) while emancipation is for establishing strength (quwa) — thus they are fundamentally distinct and not interchangeable.

The passage uses marginal notes from al-Sunbuli and al-Qamar, which clarify terms and provide additional examples.

Key Terms
سراية (saraya): Permeation: the ruling applies to the whole from part.
لزوم (luzum): Irrevocability: the ruling is binding.
استعارة (isti'arah): Metaphorical usage based on a relation.

When Haqiqah Yields to Majaz: The Abu Hanifa–Sahibayn Dispute
Explaining when literal meaning is abandoned for metaphorical interpretation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle for when literal meaning is abandoned for metaphorical interpretation, and distinguish Abu Hanifa's view from that of the Sahibayn regarding the nature of substitution (khalaf).

Core Idea
When the literal meaning is impossible, Abu Hanifa saves speech by majaz; the Sahibayn require the literal ruling to be possible first.

This lesson explores a classical Hanafi usul dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two main disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn). The core issue is whether majaz (metaphorical usage) can be employed when the literal meaning (haqiqah) is impossible. The disagreement hinges on whether majaz is a substitute in utterance (khalaf fi al-takallum) or in the ruling (khalaf fi al-hukm). The practical outcome (thamarah) is seen in a man's statement to his older slave, 'This is my son' – does this effect the slave's freedom?

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
Determine the exact question: Under what conditions can a statement be interpreted metaphorically when the literal meaning is impossible?
The passage uses the example 'This is my son' said to an older slave sonship is impossible because the slave is older than the speaker.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa holds that if the expression is grammatically sound and the literal meaning is impossible, one must turn to majaz to avoid nullifying speech. Substitution is in the utterance.
For Abu Hanifa, the word itself can be used metaphorically; the literal meaning being impossible does not block majaz.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
The Sahibayn hold that majaz is a substitute for the literal ruling, so the literal ruling must be possible. If the literal meaning is impossible, the substitution cannot occur and the statement is void.
They consider substitution in the hukm (legal consequence), not in the utterance.
4
Analyze the dala'il
Examine the proofs each side presents. Abu Hanifa cites Quranic usage like 'The Most Merciful established Himself upon the Throne' (20:5), where literal meaning is impossible but majaz is accepted. The Sahibayn argue that if the literal ruling is impossible, the statement fails.
The Hanafi principle is that necessity (to avoid pointless speech) justifies majaz.
5
Apply to the example and note thamarah
For a man saying 'This is my son' to an older slave: Abu Hanifa rules the slave is freed (majaz for emancipation); the Sahibayn rule the statement is void and no freedom results.
This practical difference shows the importance of the usul principle.

بيان المواضع التي تترك فيها الحقيقة والمجاز بيان أقسام القسيم الثالث إن "هذا ابني مرادا به الحرية خلف عن قوله : هذا حر ، والأول أولى؛ لأنه يبقى الأصل والخلف على حالهما عليه بخلاف الثاني، فإنه يتبدل الأصل بأصل آخر، وبالجملة فعنده: لابد لصحة المجاز من استقامة الأصل من حيث العربية وإن لم يستقم المعنى الحقيقي، فيصار إلى المعنى المجازي، وعندهما: المجاز خلف عن الحقيقة في الحكم أي حكم "هذا ابني" مرادًا به الحرية خلف عن حكمه مرادا به البنوة، فينبغي أن يستقيم الحكم الحقيقي ولم يعمل هذا ابني بعارض حتى يصار إلى المجاز ، فإذا كانت الخلفية عنده في التكلم، فالتكلم بالحقيقة أولى؛ لأن اللفظ موضوع لأجل المعنى الحقيقي وهو مستعمل في العادة غير مهجور فيها، فأية المعنى الحقيقي ضرورة داعية إلى صيرورته مجازاً، وعندهما لما كان خلفا عنه في الحكم، المجاز العادة خلف عن قوله: "هذا حر فالتكلم باللفظ الذي يفيد ذلك المعنى كالحرية مثلاً بطريق المجاز خلف عن التكلم باللفظ الذي يفيد عين ذلك المعنى بطريق الحقيقة. (القمر) لأنه يبقى إلخ : توضيح المقام: أن الأصل الحقيقة هذا ابني مرادا به البنوّة، والفرع المجاز هذا ابني مرادا به الحرية وهذا عندهما، فعلى التقرير الأول لكلام الإمام يبقى الأصل والخلف على حالهما لا يتغيران أصلاً ويكون الخلاف بينهما وبينه في جهة الخلفية فقط، وأما على التقرير الثاني لكلام الإمام، فالأصل الحقيقة هذا حر، فوقع الاختلاف بينهما وبينه في الأصل الحقيقة مع أنهم قالوا: إنه لا خلاف بينهما وبينه إلا في جهة الخلفية، فلذا كان التقرير الأول أولى فتأمل. (القمر) وعندهما المجاز :إلخ قالا: إن الحكم مقصود من الكلام والعبارة وسيلة إلى المقصود، فاعتبار الخلفية في المقصود أولى وقال الإمام: إن الحقيقة والمجاز من أوصاف اللفظ على ما مرّ، فالحقيقة في التكلم الذي هو استخراج اللفظ ،أولى، والحق قول إمام يشهد به تبتع الاستعمالات، فإن الحكم الحقيقي للكلام كثيرًا ما يكون محالاً نحو: الرَّحْمَنُ عَلَى الْعَرْشِ اسْتَوَى (طه:٥)، ويصار عند البلغاء إلى المجاز، قال أعظم العلماء أي مولانا عبد السلام ه كل مجتهد مكلف بما يظهر له من المرجح ،والنكتة ونكتة الإمام أظهر عندنا. (القمر) أن يستقيم: أي يمكن فلو كان المعنى الحقيقي ممتنعا لا يصح المجاز عندهما. (القمر) ولم يعمل إلخ كما أنه لم يعمل المعنى الحقيقي في قوله: "هذا ابني" مشيراً إلى العبد الذي هو معروف النسب، ويولد مثله لمثله لعارض شهرة نسبه من الغير وإن كان يمكن؛ لأنه يولد مثله لمثله. (القمر) حتى يصار إلخ احترازًا عن إلغاء الكلام (القمر) فإذا كانت إلخ شروع في بيان وجه البناء.(القمر) بيان أقسام القسيم الثالث ٣٠٩ بيان المواضع التي تترك فيها الحقيقة والمجاز ولحكم المجاز رجحان على حكم الحقيقة إما باعتبار كونه غالب الاستعمال أو باعتبار كونه عاماً شاملاً للحقيقة أيضًا، فلا بد أن يكون العمل بالمجاز أولى للضرورة الداعية إليه. وهو تعارف المجاز ويظهر الخلاف في قوله لعبده وهو أكبر سنًا منه : "هذا ابني" أي تظهر ثمرة الخلاف بين أبي حنيفة وصاحبيه له في قول الرجل لعبده: "هذا ابني" والحال أن العبد أكبر سنًا من القائل حيث يعتق العبد عنده لا عندهما؛ فإن عند أبي حنيفة لله هذا الكلام صحيح بعبارته من حيث كونه مبتدأ وخبرًا موضوعًا لإثبات الحكم، وليس معنى كونه صحيحًا استقامة العربية فقط كما ظنه علماؤنا؛ لأن أبا حنيفة له قال في قول الرجل لعبده: "أعتقتك قبل أن تخلق مع أنه بحسب العربية صحيح أيضا، بل معناه أن يكون كون الكلام صحيحًا أو أُخلق" أنه باطل لا يصح تكلمه مع صحيحًا بعبارته وتستقيم الترجمة المفهومة منه لغةً أيضًا، ولم يمتنع عقلاً، فقوله: "أعتقتك قبل أن تخلق أو أخلق" ليس كذلك، بخلاف قوله: "هذا ابني؛ لأنه صحيح مع ترجمته، وإنما الاستحالة جاءت من أجل أن المشار إليه أكبر من القائل؛ ولهذا لو قال: العبد الأكبر مني قائل ابني لغا هذا الكلام، فإذا كان قوله : "هذا ابني صحيحًا من حيث العربية والترجمة، وكان المعنى الحقيقي محالاً بالنظر إلى الخارج، صير إلى المجاز ؛ لئلا يلغو الكلام وهو العتق المجاز رجحان إلخ والمرجوح في مقابلة الراجح ساقط، فيترك، فالعبرة حينئذ بالمجاز. (القمر) :وهو : أي العبد أكبر سنًا من المولى، أو يكون مساويًا سنًا له، وتخصيص ذكر الأكبر للتمثيل أو لكونه أوضح لا للتقييد. (القمر) ثمرة الخلاف إلخ : أقحم الشارح لفظ الثمرة إيماء إلى أنه لا معنى لظاهر قول المصنف: ويظهر الخلاف في إلخ؛ لأن الخلاف لا خفاء فيه حتى يظهر، فهذا القول على حذف المضاف. (القمر) ليس كذلك فإن ترجمته اللغوية ممتنعة عقلاً. (القمر) لغا هذا الكلام لعدم استقامة الترجمة المفهومة منه لغة (القمر) إلى الخارج وهو كبر المشار إليه.(القمر) إلى المجاز إلخ : أي بطريق ذكر الملزوم وإرادة اللازم لاستلزام البنوّة في المملوك الحرية. (القمر) صير
Explanation of the places where haqiqah (literal meaning) and majaz (metaphorical meaning) are abandoned. Explanation of the divisions of the third division: Indeed, 'This is my son' intended to mean freedom is a substitute for his statement 'This is free,' and the first is preferable because it keeps the original and the substitute both unchanged, contrary to the second, for it changes the original to another original. In summary, according to him (Abu Hanifa), for the validity of majaz, the original must be sound from the perspective of Arabic grammar even if the literal meaning is not sound, so one turns to the metaphorical meaning. According to them (the two companions), majaz is a substitute for haqiqah in the ruling, meaning the ruling of 'This is my son' intended to mean freedom is a substitute for its ruling intended to mean sonship, so the literal ruling should be sound, and 'this is my son' should not be obstructed by an impediment until one turns to majaz. Since for him the substitution is in the utterance, speaking the literal is preferable because the word is placed for the literal meaning and it is customarily used, not abandoned. So the literal meaning is a necessity necessitating turning it into majaz. According to them, since it is a substitute for it in the ruling, the customary majaz is a substitute for his statement 'This is free,' so speaking the word that conveys that meaning like freedom by way of majaz is a substitute for speaking the word that conveys that exact meaning by way of haqiqah. (Al-Qamar) 'Because it remains etc.': Clarification of the context: that the literal original is 'This is my son' intended to mean sonship, and the metaphorical branch is 'This is my son' intended to mean freedom, and this is according to them. On the first interpretation of the Imam's statement, the original and the substitute remain as they are, unchanged entirely, and the disagreement between them and him is only in the aspect of substitution. As for the second interpretation of the Imam's statement, the literal original is 'This is free,' thus disagreement occurred between them and him in the literal original, even though they said there is no disagreement between them and him except in the aspect of substitution. Hence the first interpretation is preferable, so reflect. (Al-Qamar) 'And according to them, majaz etc.': They said: The ruling is the purpose of speech, and the expression is a means to the purpose, so considering substitution in the purpose is more appropriate. The Imam said: Haqiqah and majaz are attributes of the word as previously mentioned, so haqiqah in the utterance, which is the extraction of the word, is more appropriate. The truth is the Imam's statement, testified by following usages, for the literal ruling of speech is often impossible, like 'The Most Merciful established Himself upon the Throne' (20:5), and the eloquent turn to majaz. The greatest of scholars, our master Abd al-Salam, said: Every mujtahid is obligated by what appears to him as preponderant, and the subtle point of the Imam is more apparent to us. (Al-Qamar) 'That it be sound': i.e., possible; if the literal meaning were impossible, majaz would not be valid according to them. (Al-Qamar) 'And it was not obstructed etc.': just as the literal meaning was not obstructed in his statement 'This is my son' pointing to a slave known by lineage, and he could be born like him due to a hindrance of the fame of his lineage from another, even though it is possible because he can be born like him. (Al-Qamar) 'Until one turns etc.': as a precaution against nullifying speech. (Al-Qamar) 'Since it is etc.': start of explaining the basis. (Al-Qamar) 'Explanation of the divisions of the third division': 309. Explanation of the places where haqiqah and majaz are abandoned, and the ruling of majaz has preponderance over the ruling of haqiqah either by being predominant in usage or by being general encompassing haqiqah as well, so it is necessary that acting on majaz is preferable due to the necessity leading to it, and it is the customariness of majaz. The disagreement appears in his statement to his slave who is older than him: 'This is my son' — meaning the fruit of the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his two companions appears in a man's statement to his slave: 'This is my son' while the slave is older than the speaker, where the slave is freed according to him (Abu Hanifa) but not according to them. For according to Abu Hanifa, this statement is sound in its phrasing from the perspective of being a subject and predicate placed to establish a ruling, and the meaning of it being sound is not merely the grammatical soundness as our scholars thought; because Abu Hanifa said regarding a man's statement to his slave: 'I freed you before you were created' while it is grammatically sound also, but rather its meaning is that the speech being sound or 'or I create' that it is invalid, his utterance is not valid with its phrasing being sound and the understandable translation from it being linguistically sound, and it is not rationally impossible, so his statement 'I freed you before you were created or I create' is not like that, contrary to his statement 'This is my son' because it is sound with its translation, and the impossibility arises only because the pointed-to is older than the speaker; hence if he said 'the slave older than me, the speaker, is my son' this speech is null. So if his statement 'This is my son' is sound from the perspective of Arabic and translation, and the literal meaning is impossible with respect to external reality, one turns to majaz so that speech is not nullified, and it is emancipation. 'Preponderance etc.': and the preponderated against in front of the preponderant is dropped, so it is abandoned, so the consideration then is to majaz. (Al-Qamar) 'And he is': i.e., the slave is older than the master, or equal in age, and specifying the older is for illustration or because it is clearer, not for restriction. (Al-Qamar) 'Fruit of the disagreement etc.': The commentator inserted the word 'fruit' to indicate that there is no meaning to the apparent statement of the author 'and the disagreement appears in...' because the disagreement is not hidden so that it appears, so this statement has a deleted annexed word. (Al-Qamar) 'It is not like that' because its linguistic translation is rationally impossible. (Al-Qamar) 'This speech is null' due to lack of soundness of the understandable translation from it linguistically. (Al-Qamar) 'To external reality' which is the older age of the pointed-to. (Al-Qamar) 'One turns to majaz etc.': i.e., by way of mentioning the concomitant and intending the necessary consequence, because sonship in a slave necessitates freedom. (Al-Qamar) 'one turns'.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi usul discussion on when to abandon literal meaning (haqiqah) in favor of metaphorical interpretation (majaz). The key dispute is between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn). The example is a man saying to his older slave 'This is my son.' Literally, this is absurd because the slave is older, so the literal meaning (sonship) is impossible. The question is: does this statement effect the slave's freedom as a metaphorical expression? According to Abu Hanifa, yes, because the statement is grammatically sound and the literal meaning cannot be intended, so it must be metaphorical. The Sahibayn say no, because for majaz to be valid, the literal ruling must be possible; here, the literal ruling (sonship) is impossible, so the statement is void and does not effect freedom. The passage explains their underlying usul principles: Abu Hanifa focuses on the linguistic aspect—the word itself is a substitute for the literal meaning in the utterance, so as long as the grammar is sound, one can use it metaphorically. The Sahibayn focus on the legal ruling—majaz is a substitute for the literal ruling, so the literal ruling must be possible for the substitution to occur. The passage also notes that the verse 'The Most Merciful established Himself upon the Throne' is an example where the literal meaning is impossible, so majaz is used. The commentator (perhaps al-Qamar) provides clarifications, emphasizing that Abu Hanifa's position is preferred because it is supported by common usage and avoids nullifying speech. The practical outcome (thamar) of this disagreement is seen in the case of saying 'This is my son' to an older slave: Abu Hanifa rules the slave becomes free, while the Sahibayn do not, because according to them the statement is invalid.

Key Terms
الحقيقة (al-haqiqah): The literal, original meaning for which a word was coined.
المجاز (al-majaz): Metaphorical usage where a word is used for something other than its original meaning due to a relationship and a hindrance to the literal meaning.
الخلف (al-khalaf): Substitute or deputy; in this context, the relationship between haqiqah and majaz.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, what condition is necessary for a statement to be interpreted metaphorically when the literal meaning is impossible?
A) The literal ruling must be possible.
B) The statement must be grammatically sound and the literal meaning impossible.
C) The speaker must intend the metaphorical meaning explicitly.
D) The metaphorical meaning must be more common in usage.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa's principle: when literal meaning is impossible and expression sound, majaz operates to save the utterance; the Sahibayn require the literal ruling to be possible first.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
advanced
15 min
Preferring Majaz over Haqiqah: The Case of the Older Slave
Usul dispute on preferring majaz over haqiqah when literal meaning is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that majaz is preferred over haqiqah when literal meaning is impossible, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn in the case of a man saying 'This is my son' to his older slave.

Core Idea
When literal meaning is impossible, metaphor saves the speech from being void.

This passage delves into a classic usul al-fiqh dispute: when a word's literal meaning (haqiqah) is impossible in external reality, can we resort to its metaphorical meaning (majaz)? The Hanafi school, led by Abu Hanifa, says yes—to avoid voiding the speech. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree, requiring possibility of literal meaning for metaphor. We'll explore this through the concrete example of a man declaring to his older slave 'This is my son.'

Guided Path
1
Identify the usul principle
First, recognize that the core issue is about the relationship between haqiqah (literal) and majaz (metaphorical) when literal meaning is impossible. The passage states that majaz is preferred because it is more common or general.
The principle is rooted in avoiding void speech: if literal meaning is impossible, metaphor steps in.
2
Analyze the concrete case
The case: a man says to his slave, who is older than him, 'This is my son.' According to Abu Hanifa, the statement is valid in Arabic and linguistically sound; the impossibility is only external (age difference). So we must adopt the metaphorical meaning: 'son' implies freedom (since a father cannot own his son). Thus, the slave is freed.
Notice that Abu Hanifa distinguishes between linguistic validity and external possibility.
3
Understand the Sahibayn's objection
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad argue that the possibility of the literal meaning is a condition for metaphor. Since the literal meaning (being his actual son) is absolutely impossible due to age, the statement is void, and no metaphor can rescue it. Hence, the slave is not freed.
Their position is that if haqiqah is impossible, the entire speech collapses.
4
Examine the Hanafi response to objections
The passage then addresses potential objections. One objection: if 'Zaid is a lion' is valid despite impossibility, why would 'This is my son' not be? The Hanafi response: 'Zaid is a lion' is actually haqiqah by omission of 'like' (i.e., 'Zaid is like a lion'), not metaphorical. Another example 'I saw a lion shooting' is metaphorical, but the impossibility is not intended (the purpose is to report the vision, not to claim lion-hood). So the principle holds: when impossibility is external and not intended, metaphor is used.
This shows the subtlety: the distinction between intended impossibility and accidental impossibility.
5
Identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The thamarah al-khilaf: According to Abu Hanifa, the slave is freed; according to the Sahibayn, he remains a slave. The passage also notes that if the statement is coerced, it does not free the slave, indicating that it is an acknowledgment (iqrar) of freedom, not an initiation (insha').
This shows the fatwa implication: judicial vs. religious effect.

بيان المواضع التي تترك فيها الحقيقة والمجاز ولحكم المجاز رجحان على حكم الحقيقة إما باعتبار كونه غالب الاستعمال أو باعتبار كونه عاماً شاملاً للحقيقة أيضًا، فلا بد أن يكون العمل بالمجاز أولى للضرورة الداعية إليه.
وهو تعارف المجاز
ويظهر الخلاف في قوله لعبده وهو أكبر سنًا منه : "هذا ابني" أي تظهر ثمرة الخلاف بين أبي حنيفة وصاحبيه له في قول الرجل لعبده: "هذا ابني" والحال أن العبد أكبر سنًا من القائل حيث يعتق العبد عنده لا عندهما؛ فإن عند أبي حنيفة لله هذا الكلام صحيح بعبارته من حيث كونه مبتدأ وخبرًا موضوعًا لإثبات الحكم، وليس معنى كونه صحيحًا استقامة العربية فقط كما ظنه علماؤنا؛ لأن أبا حنيفة له قال في قول الرجل لعبده: "أعتقتك قبل أن تخلق مع أنه بحسب العربية صحيح أيضا، بل معناه أن يكون كون الكلام صحيحًا أو أُخلق" أنه باطل لا يصح تكلمه مع صحيحًا بعبارته وتستقيم الترجمة المفهومة منه لغةً أيضًا، ولم يمتنع عقلاً، فقوله: "أعتقتك قبل أن تخلق أو أخلق" ليس كذلك، بخلاف قوله: "هذا ابني؛ لأنه صحيح مع ترجمته، وإنما الاستحالة جاءت من أجل أن المشار إليه أكبر من القائل؛ ولهذا لو قال: العبد الأكبر مني قائل ابني لغا هذا الكلام، فإذا كان قوله : "هذا ابني صحيحًا من حيث العربية والترجمة، وكان المعنى الحقيقي محالاً بالنظر إلى الخارج، صير إلى المجاز ؛ لئلا يلغو الكلام وهو العتق
المجاز رجحان إلخ والمرجوح في مقابلة الراجح ساقط، فيترك، فالعبرة حينئذ بالمجاز. (القمر) :وهو : أي العبد أكبر سنًا من المولى، أو يكون مساويًا سنًا له، وتخصيص ذكر الأكبر للتمثيل أو لكونه أوضح لا للتقييد. (القمر) ثمرة الخلاف إلخ : أقحم الشارح لفظ الثمرة إيماء إلى أنه لا معنى لظاهر قول المصنف: ويظهر الخلاف في إلخ؛ لأن الخلاف لا خفاء فيه حتى يظهر، فهذا القول على حذف المضاف. (القمر) ليس كذلك فإن ترجمته اللغوية ممتنعة عقلاً. (القمر)
لغا هذا الكلام لعدم استقامة الترجمة المفهومة منه لغة (القمر) إلى الخارج وهو كبر المشار إليه.(القمر) إلى المجاز إلخ : أي بطريق ذكر الملزوم وإرادة اللازم لاستلزام البنوّة في المملوك الحرية. (القمر)
صير

بيان أقسام القسيم الثالث
٣١٠
بيان المواضع التي تترك فيها الحقيقة والمجاز من حين ملكه؛ لأن الابن يكون حراً على الأب دائمًا، وعندهما: لما كانت الخلفية في الحكم وكان إمكان المعنى الحقيقي شرطًا لصحة المجاز لغا هذا الكلام؛ لأن البنوة من الأصغر سنا لا يمكن حتى يحمل على المجاز الذي هو العتق لا يقال: فينبغي أن يكون قوله: زيد أسد لغواً؛ لعدم إمكان الحقيقة؛ لأنا لا نسلم أنه مجاز بل حقيقة بحذف حرف التشبيه أي زيد كالأسد وأما قوله رأيت أسدًا يرمي؛ فإنه وإن كان مجازاً لكن المقصود بالحقيقة خبر من رجل الرؤية لا كونه أسدًا، حتى يلزم المحال قصدًا، وقيل: يمكن كونه أسدًا بالمسخ وهو بعيد.
من حين ملكه إلخ فعلى هذا يعتق قضاء، وأما ديانة فإن كان تحقق منه الإعتاق فيعتق، وإلا لا، فيصير أم هذا العبد أم ولد له هذا إذا كان هذا القول إقرارًا للحرية من وقت الملك كما قيل وأما إذا لم يكن إقرارًا بل إنشاء للإعتاق بمنزلة أنت" "حر" من حين الملك كما قيل فيعتق قضاء وديانة، لكن لا يصير أمه أم ولد له والأول أي كونه إقرارًا أصح، فإنه إذا أكره رجل على قول هذا ابني" لعبده لا يعتق عليه قاله محمد في الإكراه، والإكراه إنما يمنع صحة الإقرار بالعتق لا الإنشاء فعلم أنه إقرار السنبلي) الخلفية: أي خليفة المجاز عن الحقيقة.(القمر) لغا هذا الكلام أي قوله للعبد الأكبر هذا" ابني . (القمر) الذي هو العتق بخلاف ما إذا قال للأصغر سنًا "هذا ابني" وهو معروف النسب من غيره الجواز أن يكون مخلوقا من مائه بالزنا أو بالوطء بالشبهة، وأشتهر النسب من غيره. (السنبلي) فينبغي إلخ حاصله أن قول الصاحبين خلاف أهل العربية، فإنه يلزم على قولهما أن يكون زيد أسد الغوا؛ لعدم إمكان الحقيقة مع أنهم قائلون بصحته. (القمر)
هذا
لأنا لا نسلم إلخ متعلق بالنفي في قوله : لا يقال إلخ. (القمر) لا كونه أسدًا إلخ: أقول: إن المقصود من الكلام رؤية الأسد الذي يصدر منه فعل الرمي لا كون الشخص أسدًا وذا ممكن؛ لأنه لا استحالة في أن يصدر أداة الرمي منه بالتعليم أو غيره لكنه لا يوجد عادة فلذا صير إلى المجاز. هذا ما في بعض الشروح، وقال صاحب "التوضيح" في بيان الفرق بين نحو زيد أسد ونحو: رأيت أسدًا يرمي إن الأول يشتمل على دعوى أمر مستحيل قصدًا، فيفتقر إلى تقدير أداة التشبيه ليخرج عن الاستحالة أي الاستقامة بخلاف رأيت أسدًا يرمي، فإنه وإن اشتمل على إثبات الأسدية لزيد لكنه لم يقع قصدًا بل القصد إنما هو إثبات الرؤية، فلا يفتقر إلى تقدير التشبيه للتصحيح. (السنبلي) حتى يلزم المحال فيه أن الكلام المشتمل على المحال باطل، سواء كان المحال مقصوداً أو غير مقصود، فلا بد من التأويل في ذلك الكلام لأجل تصحيحه كذا قيل. (القمر) يمكن إلخ ومثل هذا الإمكان يكفي للمصير إلى المجاز (القمر) وهو بعيد: لعل وجه البعد أنه لا مسخ في هذه الأمة على أنه لو اعتبر المسخ لما يلغو "هذا ابني" مشيراً إلى الأكبر سنًا عند الصاحبين؛ لأنه يمكن أن يكون ابنا منه بالمسخ تأمل. (القمر)
Explanation of the divisions of the third division. [310] Explanation of the cases where the literal and metaphorical meaning are abandoned, and because the ruling of metaphor has preponderance over the ruling of the literal meaning, either because it is more common in usage or because it is general encompassing the literal as well, so it is necessary that acting upon metaphor be more appropriate due to necessity calling for it. And it is the common usage of metaphor. And the difference appears in his saying to his slave while he is older than him: 'This is my son' — i.e., the fruit of the difference between Abu Hanifa and his two companions appears in the statement of a man to his slave: 'This is my son' while the slave is older than the speaker, because the slave is freed according to him but not according to them. According to Abu Hanifa, this speech is valid in its wording in terms of being a subject and predicate placed for establishing the ruling, and the meaning of its being valid is not merely correctness of Arabic as our scholars thought; because Abu Hanifa said regarding a man's statement to his slave: 'I freed you before you were created' — even though according to Arabic it is also correct — rather its meaning is that the speech is valid in wording and the translation understood from it linguistically is also sound, and it is not intellectually impossible. His statement 'I freed you before you were created or I created [you]' is not like that, unlike his statement 'This is my son' because it is valid with its translation, but the impossibility arises because the one pointed to is older than the speaker; therefore if he said: 'The older slave from me is my son' this speech becomes void. So when his statement 'This is my son' is valid in terms of Arabic and translation, and the literal meaning is impossible with respect to external reality, one turns to metaphor so that the speech is not void, and it is emancipation. Metaphor has preponderance, etc. And the preponderated in front of the preponderant is dropped, so it is abandoned, so then the consideration is with metaphor. (Al-Qamar): it means the slave is older than the master, or equal in age to him, and specifying the older is for example or because it is clearer, not for restriction. (Al-Qamar): fruit of the difference, etc.: The commentator inserted the word 'fruit' indicating that there is no meaning to the apparent statement of the author: 'And the difference appears in...' because the difference is not hidden so that it should appear, so this statement is with the omission of the genitive. (Al-Qamar): It is not like that because its linguistic translation is intellectually impossible. (Al-Qamar): this speech becomes void due to the lack of soundness of the translation understood from it linguistically (Al-Qamar): to the external, which is the older age of the pointed to. (Al-Qamar): to metaphor, etc.: i.e., by way of mentioning the necessary and intending the consequence, because paternity in a slave necessitates freedom. (Al-Qamar): one turns. [311] Explanation of the cases where the literal and metaphorical meaning are abandoned from the time of his ownership; because a son is always free from the father. According to them (the two companions): when there is deputyship in the ruling and the possibility of literal meaning is a condition for the validity of metaphor, this speech becomes void; because being born from a younger one is impossible, such that it could be interpreted as metaphor which is emancipation. It cannot be said: Then the statement 'Zaid is a lion' should be void due to the impossibility of literal meaning; because we do not accept that it is metaphor, rather it is literal by omission of the particle of simile, i.e., 'Zaid is like a lion'. As for the statement 'I saw a lion shooting', although it is metaphor, the intended meaning is the report of the vision of a man, not his being a lion, such that the impossible is intended. And it was said: It could be possible that he is a lion by metamorphosis, which is far. From the time of his ownership, etc. Accordingly, he is freed judicially. As for religiously, if emancipation actually occurred from him, then he is freed, otherwise not; then the mother of this slave becomes a mother of a child for him. This is if this statement is an acknowledgment of freedom from the time of ownership as was said. However, if it is not an acknowledgment but rather an initiation of emancipation akin to saying 'You are free' from the time of ownership as was said, then he is freed judicially and religiously, but his mother does not become a mother of a child for him. The first (being an acknowledgment) is more correct. If a man is coerced into saying 'This is my son' to his slave, it is not effective upon him. Muhammad said this in the Book of Coercion. Indeed, coercion prevents the validity of acknowledgment of emancipation, not initiation; so it is known that it is an acknowledgment. (Al-Sunbuli): deputyship: i.e., the deputy of metaphor from literal. (Al-Qamar): this speech becomes void, i.e., his saying to the older slave 'this is my son'. (Al-Qamar): which is emancipation — unlike when he says to the younger 'this is my son' while he is known to have lineage from someone else — it is possible that he is created from his semen by adultery or by dubious intercourse and his lineage from someone else is well-known. (Al-Sunbuli): 'Then it is incumbent' etc. The summary is that the statement of the two companions contradicts the people of Arabic; because according to them it would follow that 'Zaid is a lion' is void due to impossibility of literal meaning, while they say it is valid. (Al-Qamar): This: 'For we do not accept' etc. pertains to the negation in his statement 'It cannot be said', etc. (Al-Qamar): 'not his being a lion' etc.: I say: The purpose of the speech is seeing a lion that performs the action of shooting, not the person being a lion, and that is possible; because there is no impossibility in that the tool of shooting issues from him by training or otherwise, though it does not usually occur, so metaphor is used. This is from some commentaries. The author of al-Tawdih said in explaining the difference between examples like 'Zaid is a lion' and 'I saw a lion shooting': the first contains a claim of an impossible matter intentionally, so it needs the estimation of a simile tool to exit from impossibility (i.e., correctness), unlike 'I saw a lion shooting', for although it includes the affirmation of lion-ness for Zaid, it is not intended but rather the intention is only affirmation of the vision, so it does not need estimation of a simile for correction. (Al-Sunbuli): 'such that the impossible occurs' — speech that contains impossibility is invalid, whether the impossibility is intended or not, so ta'wil is necessary in that speech for its correction, as was said. (Al-Qamar): 'it is possible' etc., and such possibility suffices for the move to metaphor. (Al-Qamar): 'and it is far': perhaps the aspect of its being far is that there is no metamorphosis in this nation, although if metamorphosis were considered, the phrase 'this is my son' pointing to the older would not be void according to the two companions, because it could be his son by metamorphosis. Ponder. (Al-Qamar)
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining a usul principle: when the literal meaning (haqiqah) of a statement is impossible, but the metaphorical meaning (majaz) is possible and yields a sensible ruling, the metaphorical meaning is given precedence. The commentator illustrates this with the case of a man saying to his slave, who is older than him, 'This is my son.' According to Abu Hanifa, this statement is valid because it is grammatically correct and the translation is sound, but the literal meaning (that the slave is actually his son) is impossible due to the age difference. To avoid making the statement void, the metaphorical meaning is taken: 'son' here implies freedom (since a father cannot own his son). Thus, the slave is freed. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree: they hold that the possibility of the literal meaning is a condition for metaphor to be valid. Since the literal meaning is impossible, the statement is void, and the slave is not freed. The passage then addresses an objection: why is 'Zaid is a lion' not considered void? The response is that it is not metaphor but a literal statement with the particle of simile omitted. Another example 'I saw a lion shooting' is metaphor, but the intended meaning is the vision of a man, not that he is literally a lion, so no impossibility is intended. The commentator also distinguishes between the statement being an acknowledgment (iqrar) of freedom from the time of ownership (judicial effect only) and an initiation (insha') of emancipation (both judicial and religious effect). The correct view is that it is an acknowledgment. If coerced, the statement does not free the slave, showing it is an acknowledgment. The margins (al-Qamar, al-Sunbuli) add clarifications: the condition for metaphor is possibility of literal meaning, but here impossibility is external (due to age), not linguistic; the distinction between 'Zaid is a lion' and 'I saw a lion shooting' is that the former necessitates the impossible intentionally, while the latter does not; and the possibility of metamorphosis is dismissed as far-fetched.

Key Terms
حقيقة (haqiqah): Literal meaning of a word used in its originally assigned meaning.
مجاز (majaz): Metaphorical meaning of a word used in a sense other than its original, with a relation (qarina) and prevention of literal meaning.
إقرار (iqrar): Acknowledgment of a right, such as stating that a slave is his son, which implies freedom from the time of ownership.
خلفية (khilafiyyah): Deputyship; here, metaphor acting as a deputy for literal meaning when literal is impossible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what is the condition that triggers the use of majaz (metaphor) instead of haqiqah (literal)?
A) When the literal meaning is uncommon in usage.
B) When the literal meaning is impossible in external reality.
C) When the speaker intends a lie.
D) When the word is ambiguous.

Key Takeaway
When haqiqah is externally impossible, majaz is used to give legal effect to speech, as illustrated by the freeing of an older slave upon the master's statement 'This is my son'.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

Refining the Five Qara'in: Custom as the Primary Indication
Critique and reduction of five qara'in to custom and reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five qara'in listed in the matn and explain why the Hanafi commentator reduces them to custom and reason.

Core Idea
The five qara'in collapse into custom; the literal is abandoned only when custom overrides it.

The matn lists five distinct indications for abandoning the literal meaning, but the commentary critically refines this. This lesson traces that refinement, showing how custom (ʿādah) subsumes most qara'in, and why reason (ʿaql) stands apart. Understanding this hierarchy is essential for correctly interpreting texts.

Guided Path
1
List the five qara'in as per the matn
Recall the five: indication of custom (ʿādah), wording itself (lafẓ fī nafsih), context of expression (siyāq al-nazm), state of speaker (ḥāl al-mutakallim), and place of speech (maḥall al-kalām).
These are enumerated by the matn author.
2
Identify the commentator's critique
Al-Qamr argues that the second and third are included in custom because words and context derive meaning from customary usage. The fourth and fifth are also custom-based.
The example: seller of fish is not called seller of meat due to custom.
3
Recognize the reduction to custom and reason
Al-Sunbulī explicitly reduces to four: reason (ʿaql), sense (ḥiss), general custom (ʿurf ʿāmm), specific custom (ʿurf khāṣṣ). Reason and sense are distinct from custom.
Reason example: God's command to incite evil cannot be literal.
4
Apply the principle to the vow example
A vow of prayer or Hajj is interpreted according to legal custom, not literal language. But if the vower is unaware of legal custom (e.g., a non-Muslim from Dar al-Harb), his vow is taken literally.
This shows the conditional nature of custom.
5
Note the condition for abandoning literal
If the literal meaning is actively used, it takes precedence over the figurative custom according to Imam Abu Hanifa.
So custom only applies when literal is not the intended usage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان أقسام التقسيم الثالث بيان قرائن العمل بالمجاز وترك الحقيقة ثم شرع المصنف له بعد ذلك في بيان قرائن العمل بالمجاز وترك الحقيقة، وهي خمسة على ما زعمه. بيان قرائن العمل بالمجاز وترك الحقيقة] فقال: والحقيقة تترك بدلالة العادة كالنذر بالصلاة والحج، فإن الصلاة في اللغة الدعاء وهي خمسة أي دلالة العادة ودلالة اللفظ في نفسه ودلالة سياق النظم ودلالة حال المتكلم، ودلالة محل الكلام. (القمر) على ما زعمه فيه إيماء إلى أن في الخمسة كلامًا على ما سيقول الشارح له. (القمر) على ما زعمه إلخ إنما قال الشارح ذلك أي خمسة على ما زعمه؛ لأن قوله: دلالة اللفظ في نفسه داخل في دلالة العادة كما أشار الشارح إليه بقوله : ولأن بائع السمك لا يسمى في العرف بائع اللحم، وكذا قوله: بدلالة سياق النظم أيضا داخل في دلالة العادة كما أوماً الشارح أيضًا؛ لأن هذا الكلام إنما يقع عند إرادة عجز المخاطب، والمراد بالعادة في قول المصنف: العرف سواء كان عاما أو خاصاً كما بينه الشارح، فلم يكن الأقسام خمسة في الواقع. وقال في بعض الكتب : اعلم أن القرينة المانعة عن إرادة الحقيقة على أربعة أقسام: العقل والحس، والعرف العام والعرف الخاص، الأول كقوله تعالى: ﴿وَاسْتَفْزِزْ مَنِ اسْتَطَعْتَ إلخ (الإسراء: ٦٤) فههنا العقل مانع عن حقيقة الأمر، فإن الله حكيم لا يأمر بالغي، وكذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿فَمَنْ شَاءَ فَلْيُؤْمِنْ وَمَنْ شَاءَ فَلْيَكْفُرُ﴾ (الكهف: (۲۹) إلخ، والثاني كقول القائل: "لا يأكل من هذه النخلة، ولا يشرب من هذه البئر"، أما الثالث فكقوله : "لا يضع قدمه في دار فلان، ونحو يمين الفور وغيره وأما الرابع كما في النذر بالصلاة والحج، والتوكيل بالخصومة وبعد معرفة ذلك ظهر لك ما في عبارة المتن من الخلل والزلل، ولعل الشارح أراد ذلك بقوله : على ما زعمه والله اعلم (السنبلي) تترك أي بلا نية من المتكلم. (القمر) بدلالة العادة أي العادة في استعمال الألفاظ وفهم المعنى منها ، ثم اعلم أنه إنما تركت الحقيقة بدلالة العادة؛ لأن الكلام موضوع للإفهام، فإذا كان مستعملاً لشيء عرفا، ونقل عن معناه اللغوي، فهذه العادة أي عادة الاستعمال رجحت إرادته فيترك معناه الحقيقي. ثم اعلم أن ترك الحقيقة بدلالة العادة مقيد بما إذا لم يكن الحقيقة مستعملة؛ إذ لو كانت الحقيقة مستعملة كانت أولى عند الإمام من المجاز المتعارف على ما مر . (القمر) كالنذر إلخ فإنه محمول على ما هو معتاد في الشرع، فلو كان الناذر غير صاحب عادة بالمعنى الشرعي وغير عالم به، بل كان من أهل الحرب، فينبغي أن يتصرف نذره إلى اللغة كذا قيل. (القمر)
Explanation of the divisions of the third division: Explanation of the indications for acting by the figurative and leaving the literal. Then the author proceeded after that to explain the indications for acting by the figurative and leaving the literal, and they are five according to his claim. Explanation of the indications for acting by the figurative and leaving the literal. He said: And the literal is left due to the indication of custom, like a vow of prayer and Hajj, for prayer in language is supplication. And they are five: the indication of custom, the indication of the wording itself, the indication of the context of the expression, the indication of the state of the speaker, and the indication of the place of speech. (al-Qamr) 'According to his claim' contains a hint that there is discussion about the five, as the commentator will say. (al-Qamr) 'According to his claim' etc. The commentator only said that, i.e., five according to his claim, because his statement: 'the indication of the wording itself' is included in the indication of custom, as the commentator indicated by his statement: 'And because the seller of fish is not called in custom a seller of meat.' Likewise, his statement: 'the indication of the context of the expression' is also included in the indication of custom, as the commentator also hinted; because this speech only occurs when intending the impossibility of the addressee, and what is meant by 'custom' in the author's statement is 'urf, whether general or specific, as the commentator explained. So the divisions are not actually five. And it was said in some books: Know that the preventing indication from intending the literal is of four types: reason, sense, general custom, and specific custom. The first: like His statement (Qur'an 17:64) 'And incite whomever you can...' so here reason prevents the literal command, because God is wise and does not command evil. And likewise His statement (Qur'an 18:29) 'Then whoever wills, let him believe; and whoever wills, let him disbelieve.' The second: like the statement of a person: 'Do not eat from this palm tree, and do not drink from this well.' The third: like his statement: 'Do not set foot in so-and-so's house,' and like the oath of haste and others. The fourth: like the case of a vow of prayer and Hajj, and agency for litigation. After knowing that, the defect and error in the wording of the text became apparent to you, and perhaps the commentator intended that by his statement 'according to his claim', and God knows best. (al-Sunbulī) 'Is left' i.e., without intention from the speaker. (al-Qamr) 'Due to the indication of custom' i.e., custom in the usage of words and understanding meaning from them. Then know that the literal is only left due to the indication of custom because speech is placed for understanding. So if it is customarily used for something and transferred from its lexical meaning, then this custom (i.e., the custom of usage) gives preponderance to intending it, so its literal meaning is left. Then know that leaving the literal due to custom is conditioned on the literal not being used; for if the literal is used, it is more deserving according to Imam [Abu Hanifa] than the common figurative, as was mentioned. (al-Qamr) Like a vow etc., for it is interpreted according to what is customary in law. So if the one who vowed is not a person of custom in the legal sense and is unaware of it, but rather is from the people of war, then his vow should be interpreted according to language, as was said. (al-Qamr)
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage from the sharh (commentary) on Usul al-Fiqh discusses the indications (qarā'in) that necessitate abandoning the literal meaning (haqīqah) in favor of the figurative (majāz). The matn (original text) claimed five such indications: custom (ʿādah), the wording itself (lafẓ fī nafsih), context (siyāq al-nazm), state of the speaker (ḥāl al-mutakallim), and place of speech (maḥall al-kalām). The commentator (al-Qamr) critically refines this, arguing that the second and third are subsumed under custom (ʿurf/ʿādah). He supports this with examples: 'seller of fish' is not called 'seller of meat' in custom, and the context of speech is also governed by customary usage. Another source (al-Sunbulī) is quoted reducing the categories to four: reason (ʿaql), sense (ḥiss), general custom (urf ʿāmm), and specific custom (urf khāṣṣ). Reason is illustrated with Qur'anic verses where literal interpretation would lead to absurdity (e.g., God commanding sin). Sense applies to physical impossibilities (e.g., 'Do not eat from this palm tree' implies actual eating). The two types of custom cover the remaining. The commentary emphasizes that abandoning haqīqah is conditional: if the literal meaning is actively used, it takes precedence over the figurative 'urfī according to Imam Abu Hanifa. It also notes the example of a vow (nadhr): if a person not accustomed to legal terminology makes a vow, it is interpreted literally, not according to legal custom.

Key Terms
عادة (ʿādah): Customary usage of words; includes both general and specific custom (ʿurf).
قرينة عقل (qarīnat al-ʿaql): An indication from reason that prevents taking the literal meaning, e.g., when it would entail an impossibility or absurdity.
قرينة حس (qarīnat al-ḥiss): An indication from sense perception that prevents the literal meaning, e.g., a physical impossibility.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, why is the 'indication of the wording itself' not a separate category from custom?
A) Because the wording itself is always unambiguous.
B) Because the only way a word can indicate a meaning is through customary usage.
C) Because the commentator considered it irrelevant.
D) Because the author of the matn claimed it is separate.

Key Takeaway
The qara'in are not five independent categories; custom is the primary indicator, with reason as a secondary independent one.

Contextual Indicators and the Madhhab Dispute on the Hadith of Intentions
Explains three types of contextual indicators for metaphorical interpretation and a madhhab dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify three types of contextual indicators (qara'in) that necessitate metaphorical interpretation and apply them to the hadith 'innama al-a'malu bil-niyyat', explaining the Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute on generality.

Core Idea
Contextual indicators force metaphorical interpretation; the hadith of intentions is interpreted by Hanafis as referring only to otherworldly rulings to avoid problematic generality.

In Hanafi usul, the literal meaning (haqiqah) is the default, but when a contextual indicator (qarinah) shows the speaker intended otherwise, we must shift to metaphorical meaning (majaz). This lesson explores three types of such indicators: from the speaker's state (e.g., the oath of al-fawr), from the unsuitability of the context (e.g., the hadith of intentions), and from the context of speech. The lesson also examines the ensuing dispute with the Shafi'i school over the scope of the hadith, highlighting the principles of 'umum al-majaz and 'umum al-mushtarak.

Guided Path
1
Define qarinah and its role
Understand that a qarinah is a contextual indicator that overrides the literal meaning and leads to metaphorical interpretation. Identify its necessity when the literal meaning leads to falsehood, impossibility, or contradiction of the speaker's intent.
Hanafi usul relies heavily on qara'in to resolve ambiguity in legal texts and oaths.
2
Type 1: Qarinah from the speaker's state
Examine the example of yameen al-fawr (oath of instant anger). The husband's anger at the moment of the oath serves as a qarinah that restricts the general condition 'if you go out' to the specific instance at that moment, making the meaning metaphorical.
This is derived from a hadith of Jabir (RA) where an oath made in anger is considered tied to the immediate situation.
3
Type 2: Qarinah from unsuitability of context
Analyze the hadith 'innama al-a'malu bil-niyyat'. Its literal meaning (actions do not exist without intention) is false because actions often occur without conscious intention. Therefore, a qarinah of impossibility forces a metaphorical interpretation, such as 'the reward of deeds is by intentions'.
This illustrates the principle 'adam salahiyat al-mahall (unsuitability of context for literal meaning).
4
Type 3: Qarinah from context of speech
The context in which words are spoken can also indicate metaphor. The passage implies this type in the example of the lunch invitation where the response 'If I eat lunch...' is understood to refer to that specific lunch due to the context of the invitation.
This overlaps with Type 1 but focuses on the context of the conversation rather than the speaker's emotional state.
5
Analyze the madhhab dispute on the hadith
Once the metaphorical meaning is established as 'ruling', the dispute arises: does it include both worldly rulings (validity/invalidity) and otherworldly (reward/punishment), or only the latter? The Hanafi position excludes worldly rulings to avoid 'umum al-mushtarak, while the Shafi'i position avoids 'umum al-majaz.
The thamarah al-khilaf: Hanafis do not require intention for validity in all acts; Shafi'is often do.
6
Conclude with thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference is that Hanafis hold that the validity of acts (sihha/butlan) does not always depend on intention, while Shafi'is derive more rulings from the hadith's generality.
This dispute highlights the delicate balance between literal and metaphorical interpretation in legal texts.

بيان قرائن العمل بالمجاز وترك الحقيقة بيان أقسام التقسيم الثالث حقيقة المشيئة، وحقيقة قوله: "فَلْيَكْفُرُ" بقرينة قوله تعالى: وَأَعْتَدْنَا لِلظَّالِمِينَ نَاراً، وحمل فليكفر على التوبيخ. وبدلالة معنى يرجع إلى المتكلم ،وقصده فيحمل على الأخص مجازا وإن كان اللفظ دالا على العموم بحقيقته. كما في يمين الفور، وهو مشتق من فارت القدر إذا غلت واشتدت، ثم سميت به الحالة أي الفور مأخوذ التي لا لبث فيها ولا ريث باعتبار فوران الغضب كما إذا أرادت امرأة الخروج فقال لها الزوج: "إن خرجت فأنتِ طالق" فمكثت ساعة حتى سكن غضبه ثم خرجت لا تطلق، فإن حقيقة هذا الكلام أن تطلق في كل ما خرجت ولكن معنى الغضب الذي حدث في المتكلم وقت خروجها يدل على أن المراد هي هذه الخرجة المعينة، فيحمل الكلام عليها مجازا بهذه القرينة، ومثله قول الرجل لأحد : تعال تغدّ معي، "فقال": "إن تغديت فعبدي فإن حقيقته أن يعتق عبده أينما تغدي، سواء كان مع الداعي أو وحده في بيته، الغداء عطفا وحقيقة قوله فليكفر إلخ وهي وجوب الكفر. (القمر) كما في يمين الفور: هذا القسم من اليمين استخرجه الإمام بحديث، وهو أن جابرًا وابنه دعيا إلى نصرة ،إنسان فحلفا أن لا ينصراه ثم نصراه بعد ذلك و لم يحنثا، وكان القوم سابقًا يقولون اليمين مؤقتة نحو: "والله لا أفعل اليوم كذا" ومطلقة: نحو "والله لا أفعل كذا"، والإمام استخرج مؤقتة معنى مطلقة لفظًا كذا قال أعظم العلماء مولانا عبد السلام أعظمي له. (القمر) سميت إلخ : يقال: جاء فلان من فوره أي من ساعته. (القمر) أن باعتبار إلخ أي إنما سميت هذا اليمين بيمين الفور لصدورها من المتكلم باعتبار فوران الغضب أي شدته. (القمر) يعتق عبده إلخ لأنه دال لغة على مصدر منكر واقع تحت النفي، فإن التقدير لا أتغدى تغديًا كذا قيل. (القمر) أينما تغدى إلخ أي حقيقة هذا الكلام العموم لدلالته لغة على مصدر منكر واقع في موضع النفي؛ إذا التقدير لا أتغدى تغديًا، فيقتضي أن يحنث بكل تغد يوجد بعد كما لو قاله ابتداء، وقد تركت بدلالة حال المتكلم؛ إذ من المعلوم أنه أخرج الكلام مخرج الجواب لكلام الداعي، فإنه قد دعاه إلى تغدي الغداء الذي بين يديه لا إلى غيره، فيتقيد به، وإذا تقيد كلام الداعي به تقيد الجواب به أيضًا؛ لأنه بناء عليه وصار كأنه قال: "والله لا أتغدى الغداء الذي دعوتني إليه"، وقس عليه ما سبق من قوله لامرأته حين قامت تريد الخروج: "إن خرجت فأنت طالق".(السنبلي) ۳۱۹ بیان قرائن العمل بالمجاز وترك الحقيقة بيان أقسام التقسيم الثالث ولكن معنى التغدية الذي حدث في المتكلم حينئذٍ يدل على أن المراد هو الغداء المدعو إليه حال كونه مع الداعي، فيحمل عليه فقط حتى لو تغدى بعد ذلك في بيته لا يحنث، ولا يعتق عبده. وبدلالة في محل الكلام وعدم صلاحيته للمعنى الحقيقي للزوم الكذب فيمن هو معصوم عنه، فلا بد أن يحمل على المجاز. كقوله علي: "إنما الأعمال بالنيات فإن معناه الحقيقي أن لا توجد أعمال الجوارح إلا بالنية وهو كذب؛ لأن أكثر ما يقع العمل منا في وقت خلو الذهن عن النية، فلا بد أن يحمل على المجاز أي ثواب الأعمال أو حكم الأعمال بالنيات، فإن قدر الثواب فظاهر أنه لا يدل على أن جواز الأعمال في الدنيا موقوف على النية، وإن قدر الحكم فهو نوعان: دنيوي: كالصحة والفساد، وأخروي كالثواب والعقاب والأخروي مراد بالإجماع بيننا وبين الشافعي لله، فلا تجوز أن يراد الدنيوي أيضًا، أما عنده؛ فلأنه يلزم عموم المجاز ، وأما عندنا؛ فلأنه يلزم عموم المشترك، يدل إلخ : فإن المتكلم أخرج الكلام مخرج الجواب . (القمر) وبدلالة محل الكلام فإن المحل لما لم يقبل حكم الحقيقة تعين المجاز مرادًا للتعذر . (المحشي) محل الكلام أي ما وقع فيه الكلام وما يتعلق به.(القمر) وعدم صلاحيته أي عدم صلاحيته محل الكلام كقوله علية إلخ : ومثل صاحب "التحقيق" وغيره لدلالة محل الكلام بقوله: "لا أكل من هذه النخلة أو من هذه القدر، فإن يمينه وقعت على التمر أو الثمن، وعلى ما يطبخ فيها حتى لو أكل عين النخلة أو القدر لا يحنث وكذا في قوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا يَسْتَوِي الْأَعْمَى وَالْبَصِيرُ (فاطر: ١٩) ولا يَسْتَوِي أَصْحَابُ النَّارِ وَأَصْحَابُ الْجَنَّةِ (الحشر:۲۰) فإن فيه حقيقة عدم الاستواء غير مراد وهو العموم بأن لا يكون لهما استواء المجاز مرادا للتعذر. (السنبلي) مراد بالإجماع: فيه أنا سلمنا أن الإجماع منعقد على أن ثواب العمل منوط على النية لكن لا نسلم انعقاد الإجماع على أن الحكم الأخروي مراد في الحديث. (القمر) فلأنه يلزم إلخ يعني أن المراد لما كان حكم الأعمال مجازا وصار الأخروي مرادا منه بالإجماع، فلو أريد الدنيوي أيضا يلزم أن يكون المعنى المجازي عامًا، وعموم المجاز لا يقول به الشافعي ، وفيه أنه قد مرّ أن القول بعدم عموم المجاز افتراء على الشافعي له فتذكر. (القمر) فلأنه يلزم عموم المشترك: يعني أنه لو أريد الحكم الدنيوي مع إرادة الحكم الأخروي يلزم عموم المشترك وهو الحكم، وهو باطل عندنا هكذا قال غير واحد.(القمر) مر تخريجه .
Explanation of the indicators for acting upon the metaphorical and leaving the literal. Explanation of the divisions of the third division: the literal meaning of the will, and the literal meaning of His statement 'let him disbelieve' with the indication of His statement 'We have prepared for the wrongdoers a fire', and carrying 'let him disbelieve' upon reproach. And by the indication of a meaning returning to the speaker and his intent, so it is carried upon the most specific as a metaphor, even if the word indicates generality by its literal meaning. As in the oath of al-fawr, derived from 'the pot boiled' when it boiled and intensified, then named for the state with no pause or delay, considering the boiling of anger. As when a woman wanted to go out, her husband said 'If you go out, you are divorced.' She waited an hour until his anger subsided, then went out: she is not divorced. The literal meaning of this speech is that she would be divorced in every instance of going out, but the meaning of anger that occurred in the speaker at the time of her going out indicates that the intended is this specific going out, so the speech is carried upon it as metaphor by this indicator. Similarly, a man said to someone 'Come eat lunch with me,' and he replied 'If I eat lunch, my slave is free.' Its literal meaning is that his slave would be freed wherever he eats lunch, whether with the inviter or alone at home. (Comments: ...) But the meaning of lunch that occurred in the speaker at that time indicates that the intended is the lunch invited to while with the inviter, so it is carried only upon that, so even if he later eats lunch at home, he does not break the oath nor free his slave. And by an indication in the context of the speech and its unsuitability for the literal meaning due to the necessity of lying from one who is protected from lying, so it must be carried upon the metaphor. Like his statement, may peace be upon him: 'Deeds are but by intentions.' Its literal meaning is that actions of the limbs do not exist except with intention, and that is a lie because most of our actions occur when the mind is free from intention; so it must be carried upon the metaphor, i.e., the reward of deeds or the ruling of deeds is by intentions. If reward is intended, it is apparent that it does not indicate that the validity of deeds in the world depends on intention. If ruling is intended, it is two types: worldly (like validity and invalidity) and otherworldly (like reward and punishment). The otherworldly is intended by consensus between us and al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him. So it is not permissible to intend the worldly as well. According to him, because it necessitates generality of metaphor; and according to us, because it necessitates generality of the common term. (Comments: ...)
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage is from a Hanafi usul work, explaining the types of contextual indicators (qara'in) that lead to preferring metaphorical meaning (majaz) over literal meaning (haqiqah). The author discusses three types: 1) Indicator that refers to the speaker's intent (like the oath of fawr where anger restricts the general condition to a specific instance). 2) Indicator from the unsuitability of the context for literal meaning (like the hadith 'innama al-a'malu bil-niyyat' where the literal meaning is impossible because actions can occur without intention, so it must be taken metaphorically as reward or ruling). 3) Indicator from the context of the speech itself. The example of the oath of fawr is derived from a hadith of Jabir, showing that an oath made in anger is considered 'instantaneous' and only applies to the immediate situation. The famous hadith on intentions is analyzed in detail: its literal meaning is false (since actions can happen without conscious intention), so it must be interpreted metaphorically. The Hanafi position is that the hadith refers to the otherworldly ruling (reward) by consensus, and including the worldly ruling (validity) would lead to unacceptable generality (either of metaphor according to Shafi'i, or of the common term according to Hanafis). The passage also provides examples from Quranic verses to illustrate these principles.

Key Terms
قرينة (qarinah): A contextual indicator that guides the interpretation of a word away from its literal meaning to its metaphorical meaning.
مجاز (majaz): Metaphorical usage of a word, where the literal meaning is abandoned due to a contextual indicator.
حقيقة (haqiqah): The original, literal meaning of a word as per linguistic convention.
يمين الفور (yameen al-fawr): An oath made in the heat of anger, considered to be tied to the immediate moment, so its condition applies only at that moment.
عدم صلاحية المحل ('adam salahiyat al-mahall): The unsuitability of the context or subject to bear the literal meaning, necessitating metaphorical interpretation.
عموم المجاز ('umum al-majaz): The idea that a word used metaphorically can indicate a general meaning.
عموم المشترك ('umum al-mushtarak): The idea that a word with multiple literal meanings (common) can indicate all of them simultaneously.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do Hanafis hold that the hadith 'innama al-a'malu bil-niyyat' refers only to otherworldly rulings (reward)?
A) Because the literal meaning is true.
B) Because including worldly rulings would require generality of the common term, which is invalid.
C) Because al-Shafi'i agreed with them.
D) Because the hadith is weak.

Key Takeaway
Contextual indicators (qara'in) override literal meanings; the hadith of intentions is interpreted by Hanafis as exclusively otherworldly to avoid the flawed generality of a common term.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
10 min
Contextual Clues Requiring Metaphorical Interpretation
Explaining how context forces majaz over haqiqa with two hadith examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of qara'in to determine when a text must be interpreted metaphorically, using the hadith 'إنما الأعمال بالنيات' as a case study.

Core Idea
When the literal meaning leads to falsehood, context requires a metaphorical interpretation to protect the infallibility of prophetic speech.

This passage from Noor al-Anwar explores a critical principle in Hanafi usul: how contextual clues (qara'in) force a metaphorical reading when the literal meaning (haqiqa) would imply falsehood, especially regarding what the Prophet ﷺ said. Using the famous hadith about intentions and the hadith about pardon for mistakes and forgetfulness, the author demonstrates that a figurative interpretation is necessary to uphold the truth of prophetic speech. This lesson will guide you through the reasoning, the scholarly dispute with the Shafi'i school, and the practical implications for fiqh rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the need for metaphorical interpretation
Determine whether the literal meaning of the text leads to falsehood or impossibility. For the hadith 'إنما الأعمال بالنيات', the literal reading (no actions without intention) is false because actions often occur without conscious intention.
This step relies on 'adam salāḥiyat al-ma'nā al-ḥaqīqī' (unsuitability of literal meaning).
2
Locate the contextual clues (qara'in)
Examine the text, its occasion, and the infallibility of the speaker to identify clues indicating a non-literal meaning. Here, the impossibility of the literal meaning serves as a qarina that metaphor is intended.
Qara'in are internal (dālil al-maqām) or external (siyāq al-ḥāl).
3
Determine the intended metaphorical meaning
Propose a plausible figurative meaning that avoids falsehood. For the hadith, it must be either 'reward of actions' or 'ruling of actions' (ḥukm). The passage concludes that the intended meaning is the otherworldly ruling (reward/punishment), not worldly validity.
The consensus (ijma') between Hanafis and Shafi'is confirms the otherworldly meaning is intended.
4
Evaluate the madhhab dispute
Compare the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions. The Shafi'i school includes worldly validity in the scope of the hadith, but Hanafis argue this would entail either 'umūm al-majāz' (generality of metaphor) or 'umūm al-mushtarak' (generality of a shared term), both invalid.
This dispute has practical consequences: Hanafis make intention sunnah for wudu, Shafi'is make it fard.
5
Apply to additional cases
Extend the reasoning to the hadith 'رفع عن أمتي الخطأ والنسيان'. The literal meaning (error and forgetfulness are removed from the ummah) is false, so it means the sin in the afterlife is removed, but worldly liability remains.
The passage also discusses cases of eating forgetfully vs. erroneously during fasting.

بيان قرائن العمل بالمجاز وترك الحقيقة
بيان أقسام التقسيم الثالث ولكن معنى التغدية الذي حدث في المتكلم حينئذٍ يدل على أن المراد هو الغداء المدعو إليه حال كونه مع الداعي، فيحمل عليه فقط حتى لو تغدى بعد ذلك في بيته لا يحنث، ولا يعتق عبده. وبدلالة في محل الكلام وعدم صلاحيته للمعنى الحقيقي للزوم الكذب فيمن هو معصوم عنه، فلا بد أن يحمل على المجاز.

بيان قرائن العمل بالمجاز وترك الحقيقة
بيان أقسام التقسيم الثالث فلا يدل على أن جواز العمل موقوف على النية، فلا تكون النية فرضًا في الوضوء على ما قال الشافعي له، وأما في سائر العبادات المحضة، فالمقصود فيها الثواب، فإذا خلت عن الثواب بدون النية فات الجواز أيضًا بهذه الوتيرة لا بأن النص دال على فوت الجواز. وقوله عليه : ورفع" عن أمتي الخطأ والنسيان"، فإن ظاهره يدل على أن الخطاء والنسيان أمته، وهو كذب باطل، فيحمل على أن حكمه في الآخرة أعني المآثم مرفوع،
لا يوجد من
وأما في الدنيا فغرمه باقٍ في حقوق العباد ألبتة، وكذا في فساد الصوم بالأكل خطاء،
في الوضوء وكذا في الغسل، وتطهير الثياب وغيرهما. (القمر) وأما في سائر العبادات إلخ: دفع دخل مقدر تقريره أن العبادات المحضة كالصلاة والصوم إذا خلت عن النية بطلت فصحتها منوطة على النية، فصار معنى الحديث أن صحة الأعمال بالنيات (القمر) العبادات كالصلاة والصوم وغيرهما. (المحشي) الوتيرة: أي الطريقة وهي ترك الحقيقة والمصير إلى المجاز. (المحشي) لا بأن النص إلخ لأنه ليس معنى الحديث أن صحة الأعمال بالنيات. وهو كذب لوجود الخطاء والنسيان من الأمة المحمدية على صاحبها ألف تحية. (القمر) وكذا في فساد إلخ أي كذا حكم الخطاء باقٍ في فساد إلخ، وتوضيحه أنه إذا أكل في الصوم خطاء بأن كان ذاكرًا للصوم فأفطر من غير قصد كما إذا مضمض فدخل الماء في حلقه يفسد الصوم ويجب القضاء، وكذا إذا تكلم في الصلاة خطاء تفسد الصلاة، لعموم الأحاديث الدالة على عدم إباحة الكلام في الصلاة مطلقا، ولا يصح قياس الأكل خطاء في الصوم على الأكل ناسيا في نهار رمضان؛ فإن العذر حالة النسيان قوي لا جناية فيه أصلاً، وأما الخطاء فلا يخلو عن جناية عدم الاحتياط والتثبت . (القمر) بالأكل خطاً إلخ : قلت: وأما في صورة النسيان كما إذا أكل ناسيا، فلا ينتقض به الصوم ولا إثم أيضًا؛ لما روى الشيخان عن أبي هريرة عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم "من نسي وهو صائم فأكل وشرب فيتم صومه، فإنما أطعمه الله وسقاه الله كذا قاله عبد العلي له. (السنبلي) ** أخرج ابن ماجه في "سننه"، رقم: ۲۰٤٣ ، باب طلاق المكره والناسي عن أبي ذر الغفاري قال قال رسول : إن الله تجاوز عن أمتي الخطاء والنسيان، وما استكرهوا عليه، وفي رواية عن ابن عباس، رقم: ٢٠٤٥، باب طلاق المكره والناسي، إن الله وضع عن أمني .إلخ. وأخرج البيهقي في "السنن الكبرى" ٦٠/١٠، باب التتابع في صوم، وابن حبان، رقم: ۷۲۱۹ ،۲۰۲/۱٦ عن ابن عباس : تجاوز الله عن أمني إلخ. رواه الحاكم، وقال أبو حاتم لا يثبت [إشراق الأبصار ص ١١]
Statement of the clues for applying metaphorical meaning and leaving the literal meaning. Statement of the divisions of the third division, but the meaning of 'feeding' that occurred in the speaker at that time indicates that the intended meaning is the food invited to while he is with the host, so it is applied only to that; even if he later ate in his house, he does not break his oath, and his slave is not freed. And by indication of the context and its unsuitability for the literal meaning due to the necessity of falsehood in one who is infallible from it, so it must be interpreted metaphorically. Like his saying (peace be upon him): 'Actions are but by intentions.' Its literal meaning is that bodily actions do not exist except with intention, which is false because most actions occur when the mind is free of intention, so it must be interpreted metaphorically, i.e., the reward of actions or the ruling of actions are by intentions. If reward is assumed, then it does not indicate that the validity of actions in this world depends on intention. If the ruling is assumed, it is of two types: worldly like validity and invalidity, and otherworldly like reward and punishment. The otherworldly is intended by consensus between us and al-Shafi'i, so it is not permissible to intend the worldly also. According to him, because it would entail the generality of metaphor; according to us, because it would entail the generality of the shared term. ...
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on an earlier matn discussing the third division of metaphors. It explains how context (qara'in) forces a metaphorical interpretation when the literal meaning leads to falsehood or impossibility, especially regarding the speech of the Prophet ﷺ. The author presents two key examples: the hadith 'إنما الأعمال بالنيات' and the hadith 'رفع عن أمتي الخطأ والنسيان'.

For the first hadith, the literal reading would mean no physical actions exist without intention, which is false since many actions occur without conscious intention. Thus, it must be metaphorical: either reward or ruling (hukm) of actions depends on intention. The commentator notes consensus (ijma') that the otherworldly aspect (reward/punishment) is intended, but disputes whether the worldly aspect (validity in this world) is also included. The Hanafi position avoids generality of metaphor (which al-Shafi'i rejects) and generality of shared terms (which Hanafis reject). Hence, the hadith does not establish intention as obligatory (fard) for wudu, contrary to al-Shafi'i.

For the second hadith, the literal meaning would mean that error and forgetfulness themselves are removed from the ummah, which is false. So it must mean the sin (ithm) in the afterlife is removed. In this world, financial liability (ghurm) remains, e.g., compensation for damage caused by error. The passage also discusses the case of eating forgetfully during Ramadan: it does not break the fast, as per authentic hadith. But eating erroneously (khati') breaks the fast and requires qada, because error involves negligence.

The commentary also includes marginal notes (حاشية) by various scholars, clarifying terms and addressing objections.

Key Terms
القرائن (al-qara'in): Contextual clues that indicate the intended meaning when the literal meaning is impossible or false
المجاز (al-majaz): Figurative or metaphorical meaning used when the literal meaning (haqiqa) is not intended due to context
عموم المشترك (umum al-mushtarak): Applying a shared term (like 'hukm') in multiple senses simultaneously, which is invalid according to Hanafis

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why must the hadith 'إنما الأعمال بالنيات' be interpreted metaphorically?
A) Because the literal meaning leads to the impossibility of actions without intention, which contradicts observable reality.
B) Because the hadith is weak and requires reinterpretation.
C) Because the Prophet ﷺ used metaphor in all his sayings.
D) Because the Hanafi school prefers metaphorical interpretation over literal.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, when a literal meaning implies falsehood from the infallible Prophet, contextual clues require a metaphorical interpretation to preserve the truth of revelation.

Definition and Function of Waw in Hanafi Usul
Defining the letter waw's function for legal interpretation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of the letter waw as an absolute conjunction and distinguish it from claims that it implies simultaneity (muqarana) or sequence (tartib).

Core Idea
Waw indicates pure conjunction, not sequence or simultaneity — a foundational rule for interpreting legal texts.

This passage from Noor al-Anwaar establishes a critical linguistic principle for Hanafi usul al-fiqh: the letter waw, when connecting two elements, does not impose any temporal ordering or concurrent timing. Understanding this neutrality is essential for correctly interpreting commands, conditions, and sequences in the Quran and Hadith. We will dissect this definition and see how it contrasts with alternative views within the Hanafi school (some claimed muqarana) and from the Shafi'i school (claimed tartib).

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim about waw
The passage states that waw is for 'absolute conjunction' (al-mutlaq al-atf). Understand that this means waw simply joins two items without adding any temporal nuance.
2
Note the two things waw does NOT imply
The text explicitly negates two interpretations: muqarana (simultaneity) as claimed by some Hanafis, and tartib (sequence) as claimed by some Shafi'is. Memorize these negations.
These negations are the key legal implications.
3
Distinguish between conjunction of singulars and sentences
When waw joins singulars, the sharing is in the subject/object of the ruling. When it joins sentences, the sharing is in the affirmation/existence of the two events.
This distinction matters for how the ruling is applied.
4
Recognize the intra-Hanafi dispute on muqarana
The passage reports that some Hanafi scholars (like Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) claimed waw implies simultaneity. However, the correct and dominant Hanafi position denies this.
This is an example of a legitimate ikhtilaf within the madhhab.
5
Understand the cross-madhhab dispute on tartib
The Shafi'i claim that waw implies order is rejected in the Hanafi school. The practical consequence: when two actions are mentioned with waw, a Hanafi does not require the sequence unless there is additional evidence.
This difference appears in many rulings, such as combining prayer and zakat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال: فالواو المطلق العطف من غير تعرض لمقارنة ولا ترتيب يعني أن الواو لمطلق معية في الزمان الشركة، فإن كان في عطف المفرد على المفرد فالشركة ثابتة في المحكوم عليه أو به وإن كان في عطف الجمل، فالشركة في مجرد الثبوت والوجود، وبالجملة هو لا يتعرض للمقارنة كما زعمه بعض أصحابنا، ولا للترتيب كما زعمه بعض أصحاب الشافعي ، فإذا قيل "جاءني زيد وعمرو" يحتمل أنهما جاءاك معا، أو تقدم أحدهما على الاخر .
He said: The waw is for absolute conjunction without implying simultaneity or sequence. That is, the waw is for absolute togetherness in time and sharing. If it is in the conjunction of a singular to a singular, then sharing is established in the subject or object. If it is in the conjunction of sentences, then sharing is in mere affirmation and existence. In summary, it does not imply simultaneity as claimed by some of our companions, nor sequence as claimed by some of al-Shafi'i's companions. So if it is said 'Zaid and Amr came to me', it is possible that they both came together or that one preceded the other.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage defines the letter waw in the context of legal interpretation. The author asserts that waw is simply a conjunction indicating participation between two items, without any inherent meaning of temporal order or simultaneity. This is the dominant view among Hanafi scholars and most Arab grammarians. Although some Hanafis (e.g., Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) reportedly thought waw implies simultaneity, the correct position is that it does not. Similarly, the Shafi'i claim that waw implies sequence is rejected. The example 'Zaid and Amr came to me' allows both possibilities: they came together or one after the other. This neutrality has significant fiqh implications, such as in understanding sequences in rituals or contracts.

Key Terms
الواو (al-waw): حرف عطف لمطلق المعية في الزمان والاشتراك، لا يدل على مقارنة ولا ترتيب.
المقارنة (al-muqārana): الاجتماع في الزمان.
الترتيب (al-tartib): تأخر ما بعد الواو عما قبلها في الزمان.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as stated in this passage, what does the letter waw indicate when it connects two things?
A) It necessarily indicates that the two things occur together in time.
B) It necessarily indicates that one thing occurs after the other.
C) It indicates absolute conjunction without specifying any temporal relationship.
D) It indicates a legal obligation to perform the first-mentioned action first.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school asserts that the letter waw is a neutral conjunction, implying neither sequence nor simultaneity.
