مبني
Statement of the divisions of the third division. Discussion of the word 'hatta'. It is a particle not connected to what precedes it and has no place in parsing as the first had. And the sign of ghaya (end) is that the first part (sadr) can be extended, and the second part (akhar) is fit to indicate termination, like 'sartu hatta adkhulaha' (I walked until I entered it). Meaning the first part of speech, the last part of speech. It can be extended for a long period and the entering is fit to be its termination. Similarly, 'kharajat al-nisa' hatta kharajat Hind' (the women went out until Hind went out). The sentence can be extended to Hind's going out because she is higher than them or a servant to them, and it is fit for termination. If both conditions are present, then 'hatta' is for ghaya in the verb. If this is not consistent, then it is for majazah (causation) in the meaning of 'lam kay' (i.e., in order to). So if both conditions or one of them are absent, then it is 'lam kay' for causality, so the first is the cause and the second is the effect, due to the appropriateness between ghaya and majazah. Because the action ends with the existence of the recompense just as the thing limited (al-mughayya) ends with the existence of the limit. If this becomes impossible, it is used metaphorically for pure conjunction and the meaning of ghaya is invalidated. It has no parsing for 'hatta' as in 'kharajat Hind' (Hind went out) because the sentence is among indeclinable constructs, so 'hatta' in this position is insha'iyyah (inceptive). The first: i.e., his saying 'hatta adkhulaha' in the first example. Indication meaning either according to reality or according to the speaker's consideration as our saying: 'Mata al-nasu hatta al-anbiya' (people died, even the prophets) for termination etc. I say: This termination and inception do not necessarily have to be external; it suffices that they be in the speaker's consideration, as in 'Mata al-nasu hatta al-anbiya'. (al-Sanbali) Like 'sartu' i.e., in his saying: 'sartu hatta adkhulaha'. (al-Qamar) For termination to it, i.e., for the termination of walking to entering. (al-Qamar) 'kharajat al-nisa': i.e., in his saying: 'kharajat al-nisa hatta kharajat Hind'. (al-Qamar) And it: i.e., Hind's going out is fit to be the termination of the women's going out to Hind's going out. (al-Qamar) The two conditions etc.: They are the possibility of the first part being extended and the validity of the second part indicating termination. (al-Sanbali) In the meaning of 'lam kay' etc., if the first part is fit as a cause for the second, as in 'aslamtu hatta adkhula al-jannah' (I embraced Islam to enter Paradise), so Islam is the cause for entering Paradise. (al-Sanbali) If the two conditions are absent: i.e., the possibility of the first part for extension and the fitness of the second for indicating termination. (al-Qamar) Because the action etc.: That is, the action which is the cause ends with the existence of the recompense and the effect. And an objection is raised that 'hatta' in our saying 'aslamtu hatta adkhula al-jannah' is in the meaning of 'kay' while if by Islam we mean its occurrence, it is not extended; if we mean persistence in it, it does not end with the existence of the limit, which is entering Paradise, but rather Islam at the time of entering Paradise is stronger. So the correct thing is to say: The basis of appropriateness between ghaya and recompense is that the recompense of a thing and its effect are intended from it akin to the limited. This is in 'al-Talwih'. (al-Qamar) The meaning of ghaya is invalidated etc., as in 'ja'ani al-qawmu hatta nama Zayd' (the people came to me until Zayd slept). (al-Sanbali) The limited ends with the existence of the limit. Statement of the divisions of the third division. Discussion of the word 'hatta'. That is, if causation is also impossible, then it is for pure conjunction metaphorically, and the meaning of ghaya is not considered at all. This is a metaphor invented by the jurists, and there is no parallel for it in the speech of the Arabs. From fiqh, he said: And upon this are the issues of 'al-Ziyadat', i.e., upon these three rules. Then he mentioned examples for each of the three rules, the three examples mentioned in 'al-Ziyadat': like 'In lam adribka hatta tasiha fa'abdi hurr' (If I do not strike you until you shout, then my slave is free) – this is an example for ghaya which is in the meaning of 'ila' (up to). Because striking the addressee is a matter that can be extended to shouting by repetition of instances, and shouting is fit as a termination for it due to the arousal of mercy or the occurrence of fear from someone. So if he stops striking before the shout or does not strike at all, he incurs the penalty (yanith). And 'In lam atika hatta tughaddini fa'abdi hurr' (If I do not come to you until you feed me lunch, then my slave is free) – this is an example for majazah (causation). Because coming, although fit for extension, is not fit as a termination for feeding? Actually, feeding is not fit to be a termination for coming; rather, it is a motive for coming. Because feeding is an act of kindness, and similarly coming is not extended. So each of the two conditions is missing; because 'hatta' is for the meaning of ghaya, so 'hatta' is not taken in the meaning of ghaya, but is taken in the meaning of 'lam kay'; because coming is not fit to be a cause for feeding, and lunch is a recompense for coming. This is in 'al-Da'ir' and 'al-Tanwir'. (al-Sanbali) And it is fit for extension etc. What is in 'al-Tanwir' that coming is not extended is interpreted as meaning that by coming is meant arrival, and what the commentator said that by coming is meant movement, ponder. (al-Qamar) Based on this.
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) on the third division of the matn discussing the Arabic particle 'hatta' and its three usages in legal contexts. The author first defines the three meanings: (1) ghaya (limit/end-point), indicating that an action extends up to a point; (2) majazah (causation), equivalent to 'lam kay' (in order to), where the first clause is the cause and the second the effect; and (3) 'atf mahd (pure conjunction), where 'hatta' is used metaphorically to simply conjoin two events. The commentary explains the conditions for each usage. For ghaya, the first clause must be extendable and the second fit to indicate termination. For majazah, these conditions are absent but the first can cause the second. If neither holds, it is pure conjunction, a juristic invention with no Arabic precedent. The passage then gives examples from Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani's 'al-Ziyadat' to illustrate: (a) 'If I do not strike you until you shout, my slave is free' – here 'hatta' is ghaya, so the penalty applies if he stops before the shout; (b) 'If I do not come to you until you feed me lunch, my slave is free' – here 'hatta' is majazah (causation) because feeding is not a limit but a motive, so the penalty does not apply if he comes but is not fed. The commentary also addresses objections regarding the third usage, defending the jurists' authority to recognize such metaphorical usage despite grammarians' disagreement, citing that Imam Muhammad is a reliable source for language.

Key Terms
الغاية (al-ghaya): The meaning of 'hatta' indicating the end-point or limit up to which an action extends.
المجازاة (al-majazah): The meaning of 'hatta' as a conjunction of causation, equivalent to 'lam kay' (in order to), where the first clause causes the second.
الصدر (al-sadr): The first part of the sentence containing the action that may be extended.
الآخر (al-akhar): The second part of the sentence introduced by 'hatta', which may serve as the limit or effect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example 'aslamtu hatta adkhula al-jannah' (I embraced Islam to enter Paradise), why is 'hatta' considered majazah (causation) rather than ghaya?
A) Because embracing Islam is not an extendable action.
B) Because entering Paradise is not a natural limit of embracing Islam.
C) Because both conditions for ghaya are absent: embracing Islam is not extended, and entering Paradise is not a limit but a consequence.
D) Because the jurists arbitrarily chose that meaning.

Key Takeaway
To determine the meaning of 'hatta', first test the two conditions for ghaya; if they fail, check for a causal relationship to apply majazah.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
advanced
12 min
The Third Meaning: Pure Conjunction and the Juristic Authority to Recognize It
Defending the metaphorical usage of hatta as pure conjunction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the metaphorical use of 'hatta' as pure conjunction, evaluate the grammatical objection, and state the supporting evidence from Imam Muhammad's practice.

Core Idea
Hanafi jurists may extend 'hatta' to pure conjunction for legal clarity, even if not attested in classical Arabic.

The third usage of 'hatta' is controversial: when neither ghaya nor majazah is possible, Hanafi jurists recognize a metaphorical use as pure conjunction (like 'faa' or 'thumma'). This lesson examines the objection from grammarians and the Hanafi defense based on the authority of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani and the principle that jurists precede grammarians in extracting meanings for legal rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify when pure conjunction applies
When both conditions for ghaya fail and causation is also impossible (the first does not cause the second), then 'hatta' can be used as pure conjunction, simply connecting two events without implying limit or cause.
Example: 'ja'ani al-qawmu hatta nama Zayd' (the people came to me until Zayd slept) – here the first action does not extend to or cause the second.
2
Understand the grammatical objection
Some grammarians and scholars, such as the commentator of al-Talwih (Shaykh al-Islam), argue that 'hatta' is not used in classical Arabic for pure conjunction, so jurists cannot invent such a usage.
They demand an actual instance in Arabic speech.
3
Examine the Hanafi response
Hanafis respond with two arguments: (1) Imam Muhammad ibn al-Hasan, the author of al-Ziyadat, is a reliable source for language, and his usage is sufficient evidence. (2) Jurists (fuqaha) have precedence over grammarians in deriving meanings from linguistic forms, as they extract meanings for legal rulings.
The metaphor is valid because the relationship of succession (as in 'faa' or 'thumma') is present, and actual hearing of every instance is not a condition for metaphor.
4
Apply the rule to an oath example
In an oath like 'If I do not come to you until you sleep, my slave is free' (assuming no causal link), if 'hatta' is pure conjunction, the oath is violated only if the sleeping does not follow the coming. If he comes and then sleeps, no violation.
This contrasts with ghaya (violation if he stops before the limit) and majazah (violation if the cause does not produce the effect).

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) on the third division of the matn discussing the Arabic particle 'hatta' and its three usages in legal contexts. The author first defines the three meanings: (1) ghaya (limit/end-point), indicating that an action extends up to a point; (2) majazah (causation), equivalent to 'lam kay' (in order to), where the first clause is the cause and the second the effect; and (3) 'atf mahd (pure conjunction), where 'hatta' is used metaphorically to simply conjoin two events. The commentary explains the conditions for each usage. For ghaya, the first clause must be extendable and the second fit to indicate termination. For majazah, these conditions are absent but the first can cause the second. If neither holds, it is pure conjunction, a juristic invention with no Arabic precedent. The passage then gives examples from Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani's 'al-Ziyadat' to illustrate: (a) 'If I do not strike you until you shout, my slave is free' – here 'hatta' is ghaya, so the penalty applies if he stops before the shout; (b) 'If I do not come to you until you feed me lunch, my slave is free' – here 'hatta' is majazah (causation) because feeding is not a limit but a motive, so the penalty does not apply if he comes but is not fed. The commentary also addresses objections regarding the third usage, defending the jurists' authority to recognize such metaphorical usage despite grammarians' disagreement, citing that Imam Muhammad is a reliable source for language.

Key Terms
العطف المحض (al-'atf al-mahd): Pure conjunction, where 'hatta' simply connects two events without implying limit or causation, used metaphorically by Hanafi jurists.
الاستعارة (al-isti'ara): Metaphorical use of 'hatta' for pure conjunction, invented by jurists.
الزيادات (al-Ziyadat): A work by Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani containing examples of the three meanings of 'hatta'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response in the passage, why is Imam Muhammad's usage of 'hatta' for pure conjunction sufficient evidence?
A) Because Imam Muhammad was a grammarian.
B) Because Imam Muhammad is a reliable source from whom language can be taken.
C) Because the Caliph ordered it.
D) Because grammarians approved it.

Key Takeaway
Hanafi usul accepts the metaphorical use of 'hatta' for pure conjunction based on the authority of Imam Muhammad and the jurists' precedence over grammarians.

The Meaning of 'Baa' in Wiping the Head: A Hanafi Usul Analysis
Clarifying that 'baa' is for adhesion, not partition or extra, and how partial wiping is derived

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three madhhab interpretations of the particle 'baa' in Q.5:6 and evaluate the Hanafi argument that 'baa' indicates adhesion, not partition or extra.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school maintains 'baa' means adhesion; partial wiping derives from instrumental usage, not the particle itself.

In this lesson, we examine a classic usul dispute: what does the Arabic particle 'baa' mean in the Quranic command to wipe the head? Al-Shafi'i and Malik offered alternative readings that lead to different rulings on the extent of wiping. The Hanafi school, grounded in linguistic principles, argues that 'baa' retains its original meaning of adhesion, and the partial nature of wiping is explained by the instrumental function of the hand. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to analyze Quranic texts using usul al-fiqh tools.

Guided Path
1
State the three positions on 'baa'
Summarize the Shafi'i (tab'īḍ), Maliki (ṣilah), and Hanafi (ilṣāq) interpretations of the particle in Q.5:6.
Each position leads to a different ruling on the extent of head wiping.
2
Identify the linguistic principle behind the Hanafi rejection
Pinpoint that the Hanafis reject tab'īḍ and ṣilah because they are metaphorical or secondary departures from the original meaning (ḥaqīqa).
The principle is that original meanings are retained unless necessity demands a metaphorical shift.
3
Explain the instrumental derivation of partial wiping
Articulate how the Hanafis derive partial wiping from the verb structure: the head is the locus (maḥall) and the hand is the instrument (āla); only part of the instrument is needed.
This is the key Hanafi argument that reconciles the text with practice.
4
Contrast the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
State the practical ruling for each school: Hanafi – wiping any part suffices; Shafi'i – at least three fingers; Maliki – entire head.
The dispute directly affects how Muslims perform wudu.
5
Evaluate the tarjih (preferred position)
Explain why the Hanafi view is preferred: it adheres to linguistic principles and avoids the burden of evidence required for metaphorical interpretations.
This shows the methodological rigor of Hanafi usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الشافعي : الباء في قوله تعالى: ﴿وَامْسَحُوا بِرُءُوسِكُمْ﴾ للتبعيض، فيكون المعنى وامسحوا بعض رؤوسكم، والبعض مطلق بين أن يكون شعراً أو ما فوقه حتى قريب الكل، فعلى أي بعض يمسحُ يكون آتيا بالمأمور به. وقال مالك بحبه: إنها صلة أي زائدة، فكان المعنى وَامْسَحُوا رُءُوسَكُمْ، والظاهر منه الكل، فيكون مسح كل الرأس فرضًا. وليس كذلك أي ليس للتبعيض والزيادة؛ لأن التبعيض مجاز فلا يصار إليه، ولو كان التبعيض حقيقة وهو موجب من لزم الاشتراك والترادف، وكلاهما خلاف الأصل، وكذلك الزيادة أيضًا خلاف الأصل. بل هي للإلصاق حقيقة على أصل وضعها وإنما جاء التبعيض في مسح الرأس بطريق آخر كما قال. لكنها إذا دخلت في آلة المسح كان الفعل متعديًا إلى محله، فيتناول كله كما إذا قيل: أي اليد مسحت الحائط بيدي، فالحائط محل الفعل ومفعول له يراد به كله. واليد آلة دخل عليها الباء يراد بها البعض؛ إذ المعتبر في الآلة قدر ما يوجد به الفعل.
And al-Shafi'i said: The 'baa' in the statement of Allah the Exalted: 'and wipe your heads' (Al-Ma'idah:6) is for partition (tab'īḍ), so the meaning is: wipe some of your heads – and 'some' is unrestricted whether hair or what is above it, even close to all. So upon any part one wipes, he fulfills the commanded act. And Malik said – may Allah have mercy on him – that it is an extra (ṣilah), i.e., redundant, so the meaning is: wipe your heads, and the apparent meaning is all, so wiping the entire head becomes obligatory. But it is not like that – i.e., it is not for partition nor extra, because partition is metaphorical (majāz) and should not be resorted to. And if partition were the literal meaning, it would necessitate homonymy (ishtirāk) and synonymy (tarāduf), both of which are against the principle. Likewise, extra is also against the principle. Rather, it is for adhesion (ilṣāq) literally according to its original usage. And the notion of wiping part of the head comes through another way, as he said. But when it enters upon the instrument of wiping, the verb becomes transitive to its locus, encompassing it wholly – as when one says: 'I wiped the wall with my hand', so the wall is the locus of the act and its object, intended to be entirely wiped, while the hand is the instrument upon which 'baa' enters, and part of it is intended, for what matters in the instrument is the amount with which the act is performed.
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage is a dispute (ikhtilāf) between the Hanafi madhhab and al-Shafi'i and Malik regarding the interpretation of the particle 'baa' in the verse of wudu: 'wa-msaḥū bi-ru'ūsikum' (al-Ma'idah 6). Al-Shafi'i interprets 'baa' as indicating partition (tab'īḍ), meaning wiping some part of the head, which he considers partial. Malik views it as extra (ṣilah), making the verb transitive directly, so the entire head must be wiped. The Hanafi position, here presented by the commentator, is that 'baa' retains its original meaning of adhesion (ilṣāq). They argue that partition is a metaphorical usage and should not be adopted without necessity; if it were literal, it would lead to undesirable homonymy and synonymy. Extra is also against the principle of language. Instead, partial wiping is derived from the fact that 'baa' indicates the instrument (āla): when one says 'I wiped the wall with my hand', the entire wall is the locus but only a part of the hand (the part that makes contact) is intended. Thus, the required wiping of the head means wiping with a wet hand over any part of the head – the head is the locus, the hand is the instrument. This is the way the Hanafi school reconciles the text with the requirement of partial wiping (masḥ ar-ra's).

Key Terms
التبعيض (al-tab'īḍ): The interpretation of 'baa' as indicating a part of the object
الصلة (al-ṣilah): A redundant element added for emphasis or to satisfy a grammatical requirement
الإلصاق (al-ilṣāq): The core meaning of 'baa': adhesion or attachment

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, how is the obligation of wiping part of the head derived from the verse 'wa-msaḥū bi-ru'ūsikum'?
A) 'Baa' indicates partition (tab'īḍ)
B) 'Baa' is extra (ṣilah) so the entire head must be wiped
C) 'Baa' indicates adhesion, and partial wiping comes from the instrumental usage: the head is the locus, the hand is the instrument
D) 'Baa' indicates adhesion, so the entire head must be wiped

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi interpretation of 'baa' as adhesion is grounded in the principle that original linguistic meanings are retained unless necessity demands otherwise, and the partial wiping is derived from the instrumentality of the wiping hand.

The Hanafi Analysis of the Letter 'Ba' in the Wudu Verse
Hanafi analysis of the letter 'ba' in Quran 5:6

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the meaning of the letter 'ba' in Quran 5:6 and derive the ruling for wiping the head using usul principles.

Core Idea
The 'ba' is for adhesion; partial wiping derives from the head being an instrument, not from the letter itself.

This passage from a Hanafi usul work dives into the precise meaning of the letter 'ba' in the verse of wudu (Quran 5:6). The interpretation of this single preposition has significant fiqh consequences: how much of the head must be wiped? The passage presents the Hanafi view against those of Imam Malik and Imam al-Shafi'i, grounding its position in linguistic principles and the instrumental role of the head.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central linguistic dispute
The issue is the meaning of the 'ba' in 'wa-msaḥū bi-ru'ūsikum'. Three possibilities are discussed: partiality (tab'īḍ), redundancy (zā'ida), and adhesion (ilṣāq). Identify each school's claim.
2
Examine the Hanafi rebuttal
The Hanafi argument uses the principle that the original meaning of 'ba' is adhesion. Partiality would be metaphorical (majāz), and redundancy violates the rule against superfluity. Understand why these are rejected.
3
Follow the alternative derivation of partiality
The passage explains that partiality arises not from the 'ba' itself but from the syntactic role: when 'ba' makes the noun an instrument, only a part of the instrument is needed to achieve the purpose. Trace this analogy with 'I wiped the wall with my hand'.
4
Derive the minimum amount
The instrument (head) only requires a part sufficient for the act. The Hanafi school sets this as three fingers, based on the majority of the hand. Understand the reasoning.
5
Note the second Hanafi narration
The passage mentions another narration from Abu Hanifa that the verse is mujmal (ambiguous) regarding the quantity. Recognize that both narrations reject the full-head wiping, but differ in specification.

بحث "الباء"
فيكون المعنى وامسحوا بعض رؤوسكم، والبعض مطلق بين أن يكون شعراً أو ما فوقه حتى قريب الكل، فعلى أي بعض يمسحُ يكون آتيا بالمأمور به، وقال مالك بحبه: إنها صلة أي زائدة، فكان المعنى وَامْسَحُوا رُؤُوسِكُمْ، والظاهر منه الكل، فيكون مسح كل الرأس فرضًا.

لكنها إذا دخلت في آلة المسح كان الفعل متعديًا إلى محله، فيتناول كله كما إذا قيل: أي اليد مسحت الحائط بيدي، فالحائط محل الفعل ومفعول له يراد به كله. واليد آلة دخل عليها الباء يراد بها البعض؛ إذ المعتبر في الآلة قدر ما يحصل به المقصود، وإذا دخلت في محل المسح بقي الفعل متعديًا إلى الآلة كما إذا أي الممسوح قيل: مسحتُ بالحائط أو قيل وامسحوا برؤوسكم، فحينئذٍ يكون المسح متعديًا إلى الآلة، فكأنه قيل مسحت اليد بالحائط فيشبه المحل بالوسائل في أخذ بعضه. فلا يقتضي استيعاب الرأس، وإنما يقتضي إلصاق الآلة بالمحل، وذلك لا يستوعب الكل عادة، فصار المراد به أكثر اليد، وذلك مقدار ثلاث أصابع؛ لأن الأصابع أصل في اليد والكف تابع والثلاث أكثرها، فأقيم مقام الكل.
فصار التبعيض مرادًا بهذا الطريق لا كما زعم الشافعي أن الباء للتبعيض هذا من إحدى روايتي أبي حنيفة لله و لم يتعرض للرواية الأخرى، وهي أنه مجمل في حق المقدار؟
Statement of the sections of the third division. Discussion of the letter 'ba'. So the meaning becomes: 'and wipe some of your heads', and 'some' is general, whether it is hair or what is above it, even up to near the whole. So on whichever part one wipes, he has performed the commanded act. Malik said regarding it: It is an extra letter, i.e., redundant, so the meaning becomes 'and wipe your heads', and the apparent meaning is the whole, so wiping the entire head becomes obligatory. (Al-Maidah: 6) And it is not like that, i.e., it is not for partiality and redundancy; because partiality is metaphorical so it is not resorted to, and if partiality were literal, it would necessitate homonymy and synonymy, both contrary to the principle. And likewise redundancy is also contrary to principle. Rather, the 'ba' is for adhesion literally according to its original usage, and partiality in wiping the head came from another way as he said. However, when it enters the instrument of wiping, the verb becomes transitive to its place (object), so it includes the whole, as if it is said: 'I wiped the wall with my hand.' So the wall is the place of the action and the direct object intended wholly. The hand is an instrument upon which the 'ba' entered, and only a part is intended; for the instrument is considered in the amount that achieves the purpose. But when it enters the place of wiping, the verb remains transitive to the instrument, as if it is said: 'I wiped with the wall' or 'wipe with your heads.' So then the wiping becomes transitive to the instrument, as if it is said: 'I wiped the hand with the wall,' so the place resembles tools in taking a part of it. Thus it does not necessitate encompassing the head, but rather necessitates adhesion of the instrument to the place, and that does not normally encompass the whole. So the intended amount is the greater part of the hand, which is the measure of three fingers; because the fingers are the origin of the hand and the palm is subordinate, and three is the majority of them, so it stands for the whole. Thus the partiality is intended by this way, not as al-Shafi'i claimed that the 'ba' is for partiality. This is from one of the two narrations from Abu Hanifa, and he did not address the other narration, which is that it is ambiguous (mujmal) regarding the quantity.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage is a detailed Hanafi usul analysis of the letter 'ba' in the verse of wudu (Quran 5:6). The author is expanding on the third division (presumably of a larger classification). The issue is whether the 'ba' in 'wa-msaḥū bi-ru'ūsikum' indicates partiality (tab'īḍ), is redundant (zā'ida), or indicates adhesion (ilṣāq). The Hanafi position is that the 'ba' is for adhesion, as per its original meaning. If it were for partiality, that would be metaphorical and not the default. If it were redundant, it would violate the principle of no redundancy. The partiality of wiping the head is derived from a different linguistic route: when the verb 'massaha' is made transitive to the instrument (the head becomes the instrument), only a part of the instrument is required to achieve the purpose. This is analogous to saying 'I wiped the wall with my hand' — the wall (place) is wiped entirely, but the hand (instrument) is used only partially. Likewise, 'wipe with your heads' means the head becomes the instrument, so only a part of it need be wiped, and the minimum is three fingers (the majority of the hand). This contradicts the view attributed to al-Shafi'i that the 'ba' itself indicates partiality, and also contrasts with Malik's view that the 'ba' is redundant implying the whole head. The passage also notes two narrations from Abu Hanifa: one is the above, and the other deems the verse ambiguous (mujmal) regarding the quantity, not specifying any particular amount.

Key Terms
الباء (al-bā'): The letter 'ba' in the verse of wudu; the Hanafi position is that it is for adhesion (ilṣāq).
التبعيض (al-tab'īḍ): Partiality; indicating that only a part of the head is to be wiped.
الإلصاق (al-ilṣāq): Adhesion; the meaning that the 'ba' brings about contact between the action and the object.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi analysis of Quran 5:6, what is the original meaning of the letter 'ba' in 'wa-msaḥū bi-ru'ūsikum'?
A) It indicates partiality (tab'īḍ).
B) It is redundant (zā'ida).
C) It indicates adhesion (ilṣāq).
D) It indicates the whole (isti'āb).

Key Takeaway
The letter 'ba' in the wudu verse is for adhesion (ilṣāq); the partial wiping of the head is derived from the instrumental role of the head, not from the 'ba' itself.

The Particle Ba in Wiping: Partiality vs. Extra
Usul analysis of the particle ba in wudu and tayammum

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul rule regarding the particle ba to derive the extent of wiping in wudu and tayammum, and evaluate the intra-madhhab dispute between the zahir al-riwaya and the narration of Hasan ibn Ziyad.

Core Idea
When ba enters upon a location, it indicates partiality—only a quarter of the head is obligatory to wipe.

The particle ba in the Quranic verse of wiping (4:43) raises a fundamental usul question: does it require complete coverage or just a part? This passage unpacks the Hanafi position by analyzing the hadith of the Prophet's wiping of his forelock, and contrasts the treatment of ba in wudu versus tayammum, where substitutional reasoning changes the ruling. You will learn how the same particle can yield different obligations based on context and sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the usul problem
Recognize that the verse 'famsahu bi ru'usikum' is ambiguous: does ba mean 'with' (instrumental) or 'part of' (partiality)? The Hanafi method resolves this by citing the Prophet's action as a bayan (explanation).
The ambiguity (ijmal) is resolved by sunnah.
2
Analyze the hadith proof
The hadith of al-Mughira shows the Prophet wiping only his forelock (nasiya). Since the forelock is one quarter of the head, ba entering on a location (mahall) indicates a part, not the whole.
This establishes the fard of wiping a quarter.
3
Distinguish the tayammum case
In tayammum, the same verse uses ba, but the ruling requires complete coverage of face and hands. This is because tayammum is a substitute for wudu (khalaf), and the well-known sunnah confirms full wiping. So ba here is treated as extra (zā'ida).
The distinction lies in the substitutional relationship.
4
Examine the intra-madhhab dispute
The narration of Hasan ibn Ziyad holds that ba indicates partiality even in tayammum, so only the majority of each limb need be wiped. The zahir al-riwaya rejects this, but the text preserves it for practical ease (balwa).
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The preferred (mufta bihi) position is complete coverage in tayammum, but the alternative is valid. Understand the epistemological weight: zahir al-riwaya vs. nawadir.
Hardship (balwa) is a factor in preserving the weak narration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان أقسام التقسيم الثالث
عَالِيَةٌ
بحث "الباء"
لأنه لم يعلم أن المراد كل الرأس أو بعضه، فيكون فعل النبي ﷺ أنه هو مسح على ناصيته بيانًا له، والناصية هي مقدار ربع الرأس، فيكون مسح ربع الرأس فرضا، سواء كان بثلاث أصابع أو كلها؛ لأن الكلام فيها طويل، وإنما يثبت استيعاب مسح الوجه واليد في التيمم لقوله تعالى: ﴿فَامْسَحُوا بِوُجُوهِكُمْ وَأَيْدِيكُمْ﴾ [النساء: 43]؛ لأنه خلف عن الوضوء، فيعامل معاملته في الوجه واليد؛ ولأنه ثبت الاستيعاب فيه بالسنة المشهورة، للرأس.(القمر) مقدار ربع الرأس فإن الناصية هي أحد الجوانب الأربع للرأس. (القمر) لأن الكلام فيها متعلق بقوله : ولم يتعرض إلخ والله أعلم ماذا أراد الشارح بالكلام، وما يختلج في القلب :هو أنا لا نسلم أولاً أن الآية مجملة ولو كانت مجملة لتوقف السلف من الصحابة والتابعين في الاستدلال بها، و لم ينقل التوقف ولو سلم أن الآية مجملة، فنقول: إن الباء في حديث المغيرة داخلة على الناصية كما مر في رواية "مسلم"، وإذا دخلت الباء على المحل يشبه بالوسائل فيراد البعض لا الكل فما لزم منه إلا مسح بعض الناصية لا كل الناصية، فكيف يثبت مسح ربع الرأس فتدبر . (القمر) وإنما يثبت إلخ جواب سؤال مقدر تقريره أن الباء في آية التيمم داخلة على المحل والفعل متعد إلى الآلة، والتقدير: فامسح الأيدي بوجوهكم وأيديكم، فينبغي على قاعدتكم أنه لا يستوعب مسح الوجه واليد في التيمم مع أنكم قلتم باستيعابه ثم اعلم أن شرط الاستيعاب في التيمم إنما هو على ظاهر الرواية، وأما على رواية الحسن عن أبي حنيفة له باستيعابه ليس بشرط بل يكفي مسح الأكثر من كل عضو، وقال شمس الأئمة الحلواني: ينبغي أن يحفظ رواية الحسن جدًا لكثرة البلوى فيه كذا في "ذخيرة العقبى". (القمر) لأنه خلف إلخ: أي لأن التيمم خلف عن الوضوء، فإن قوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِن كُنتُم مَرْضَى أَوْ عَلَى سَفَرٍ أَوْ جَاءَ أَحَدٌ مِنْكُمْ مِنَ الْغَائِطِ أَوْ لامَسْتُمُ النِّسَاءَ فَلَمْ تَجِدُوا مَاءً فَتَيَمَّمُوا صَعِيداً طَيِّبا﴾ [النساء: 43] صريح في بيان الخلفية، فيعامل معاملة الوضوء في الوجه واليد فكما أن استيعاب غسل الوجه واليد في الوضوء ضروري، فكذا استيعاب مسحهما في التيمم يكون ضروريًا، فالباء في الآية صلة أي زائدة. (القمر) ولأنه إلخ : معطوف على قوله : لأنه إلخ. (القمر) بالسنة المشهورة أي لا بالآية فالسنة المشهورة بيان للآية في حق الاستيعاب، فجعلت الباء في الآية صلة زائدة بدلالة الحديث المشهور. (القمر)
أخرج مسلم في "صحيحه" رقم ٢٧٤ ، باب المسح على الناصية والعمامة عن المغيرة بن شعبة أن النبي توضأ فمسح بناصيته وعلى العمامة وعلى الخفين.
Statement of the divisions of the third division. High. Discussion of 'Ba'. Because it is not known whether the whole head or some of it is intended, so the action of the Prophet ﷺ that he wiped his forelock becomes an explanation of it, and the forelock is the amount of a quarter of the head. So wiping a quarter of the head is obligatory, whether with three fingers or all of them; because the discussion about it is long. And the complete coverage of wiping the face and hands in tayammum is established by His statement: 'So wipe your faces and your hands' (4:43); because it is a substitute for wudu, so it is treated like wudu regarding the face and hand; and because complete coverage is established in it by the well-known Sunnah, for the head. (al-Qamar) 'The amount of a quarter of the head' because the forelock is one of the four sides of the head. (al-Qamar) 'Because the discussion about it is related to his statement' etc. And Allah knows best what the commentator meant by the discussion, and what occurs to the heart is: first, we do not concede that the verse is ambiguous; even if it were ambiguous, the early generations of Companions and Followers would have paused in deducing from it, and pausing is not transmitted. And even if we conceded that the verse is ambiguous, we say: the 'ba' in the hadith of al-Mughira enters upon the forelock as mentioned in Muslim's narration; and when 'ba' enters upon a location, it resembles the instrumental meaning, so a part is intended, not the whole, so only wiping part of the forelock follows, not all of it, so how is wiping a quarter of the head established? Reflect. (al-Qamar) 'And it is established' etc. is the answer to an implied question. Its formulation is that the 'ba' in the verse of tayammum enters upon the location and the verb is transitive to the instrument, so the estimation is: wipe the hands with your faces and hands; so according to your principle, it would not require complete coverage of the face and hand in tayammum, while you say it is required. Then know that the condition of complete coverage in tayammum is only according to the apparent narration; but according to the narration of Hasan from Abu Hanifa, complete coverage is not a condition, rather wiping the majority of each limb suffices. And Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani said: The narration of Hasan should be preserved greatly due to the frequency of hardship involved. This is in Dhakhirat al-Uqba. (al-Qamar) 'Because it is a substitute' etc.: meaning tayammum is a substitute for wudu. For His statement: 'If you are sick or on a journey or one of you comes from the toilet or you have touched women and find no water, then perform tayammum with clean earth' (4:43) is explicit in stating substitution; so it is treated like wudu regarding the face and hands. Just as complete washing of the face and hands in wudu is necessary, so complete wiping of them in tayammum is necessary. So the 'ba' in the verse is copulative, i.e., extra. (al-Qamar) 'And because' etc.: conjoined to 'because' etc. (al-Qamar) 'By the well-known Sunnah' i.e., not by the verse; the well-known Sunnah explains the verse regarding complete coverage, so the 'ba' in the verse is made extra (zā'ida) by the indication of the well-known hadith. (al-Qamar) Muslim reported in his Sahih, no. 274, chapter on wiping over the forelock and turban from al-Mughira b. Shu'ba that the Prophet performed wudu and wiped his forelock and over the turban and over the khuffs.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul analysis of the particle 'ba' (باء) as it appears in the Quranic verse of wiping the head (4:43). The commentator (al-Qamar) grapples with how 'ba' determines the extent of wiping. The core issue: does 'ba' indicate partiality or complete coverage? The Hanafi position, based on the hadith of al-Mughira, is that 'ba' entering upon a location (like 'head') signifies a part, not the whole. Consequently, the obligatory (fard) amount is a quarter of the head, represented by the forelock (nāṣiya). The commentator rejects the claim that the verse is ambiguous (mujmal), arguing that early scholars did not hesitate to use it as proof. For tayammum, the ruling differs: complete coverage of the face and hands is required because tayammum is a substitute (khalaf) for wudu, and the well-known sunnah confirms full wiping. However, there is a minority narration (riwāya) from Hasan ibn Ziyād that only the majority of each limb is required—a view the commentator notes as practically useful due to frequent hardship. The passage also explains that in the tayammum verse, 'ba' is treated as extra (zā'ida) or copulative, not instrumental, to harmonize with the sunnah. The hadith of Muslim is cited as proof that the Prophet wiped only his forelock, not the entire head.

Key Terms
الباء (al-bā'): The letter ba in the Quranic verse of wiping (4:43). The text analyzes whether it indicates partiality (li'l-tab'īḍ) or is extra (zā'ida).
الناصية (al-nāṣiya): The forelock; one of the four sides of the head, equivalent to a quarter. Used to determine the obligatory amount of wiping.
الاستيعاب (al-isti'āb): Complete coverage of the limb in wiping (tayammum). Required in the main narration but not in the narration of Hasan.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi analysis of the verse (4:43) 'famsahu bi wujuhikum wa aydikum', what does the 'ba' indicate regarding wiping the head in wudu?
A) Complete coverage of the entire head is required.
B) Only a quarter of the head must be wiped.
C) The entire face and hands must be wiped in tayammum.
D) 'Ba' is extra (zā'ida) and has no meaning.

Key Takeaway
The particle ba in Hanafi usul can indicate partiality when entering on a location, as in wiping the head, but when the context involves substitution (tayammum), it is treated as extra to align with the well-known sunnah requiring full coverage.

Interpreting 'من' as Partitive vs Explicative in Usul al-Fiqh
Textual analysis of the word 'من' in a conditional manumission statement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether 'من' is partitive or explicative in a given Arabic legal phrase and apply the Hanafi principle of giving priority to the original meaning.

Core Idea
The original meaning of 'من' is partition; deviation to bayān requires a context that cannot be reconciled with partition.

In this lesson, we examine a classic usul al-fiqh problem: how to interpret the word 'من' in a master's instruction to his agent. The word 'من' can indicate a part (tab‘īḍ) or serve as explanation (bayān). The Hanafi school, following Abu Hanifa, prioritizes the original partitive meaning, leading to a specific legal outcome when the agent acts. Understanding this distinction is key to mastering the Hanafi approach to linguistic interpretation (takhṣīṣ al-‘āmm).

Guided Path
1
Identify the textual element
Locate the word 'من' in the legal statement and determine its role in the sentence.
Here the phrase is 'من شئت من عبيدي عتقه فأعتقه' – the first 'من' is the focus.
2
Determine the original meaning
Establish the default meaning of 'من' in Arabic according to Hanafi usul. The original is tab‘īḍ (partitive).
Other meanings are considered metaphorical (majāz) unless evidence pushes to the primary.
3
Assess context for a secondary meaning
See if the context forces a departure from the original. Here, the presence of the word 'شئت' (you wish) might suggest explicative, but Abu Hanifa rejects that as redundant.
If both words can be operative with partitive meaning, that is preferred.
4
Derive the legal implication
Apply the chosen meaning to the ruling. If partitive, the agent can only free some slaves; if explicative, all can be freed.
The practical consequence: one slave remains unfreed if all are freed at once.
5
Note the ikhtilaf and its basis
Contrast with the Sahibayn's view that 'من' is bayān, and analyze the linguistic and analogical arguments.
The analogy with 'أي عبيدي' cases helps illustrate the conditionality of generality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بحث كلمة "من" ومن للتبعيض هذا أصل وضعها والبواقي من المعاني مجاز فيها. فإذا قال: "من شئت من عبيدي عتقه فأعتقه" له أن يعتقهم إلا واحدًا منهم عند أبي حنيفة حليه وذلك لأن كلمة "من" للعموم، وكلمة "من" للتبعيض، فيجب أن يحمل على بعض عام ليستقيم العمل بهما، فللمخاطب أن يعتق من شاء مِن أيّ بعض عام، فيبقى الواحد منهم، وعندهما "من" للبيان، فله أن يعتق كلاً منهم كما في قوله: "من شاء من عبيدي عتقه فأعتقه" فإن شاء الكل عتقوا جميعًا، والفرق لأبي حنيفة له مثل ما مرّ في أي عبيدي ضربك؛ لأن المشية صفة عامة فيه نسبت إلى كلمة "من" فيعم بعموم الصفة، هذا أصل وضعها أي عند أكثر الفقهاء، وقال جمهور أهل اللغة: إن "من" في الأصل لابتداء الغاية المكانية أو الزمانية، وقال بعض إن من في الأصل للتبيين واختار صاحب "المسلم" أن "من" مشتركة بين هذه المعاني للتبادر. (القمر) إلا واحدًا إلخ لأن كلمة "من" للتبعيض، فيجب على الوكيل أن يبقي بعض العبيد على العبدية، وإلا لم يكن فعل الوكيل مطابقًا للتوكيل فلا ينفذ. (السنبلي) وكلمة "من" للتبعيض : فالمخاطب صار وكيلاً بإعتاق بعض من العبيد. (القمر) فيبقى الواحد منهم: فإن أعتقهم المخاطب على التعاقب لا يكون الأخير حرا، وإن أعتقهم معا عتقوا إلا واحد منهم، والخيار في التعيين إلى المولى كذا قيل. (القمر) والفرق وجه الفرق على ما هو المشهور أن في الأول وصف بالضاربية، فيعم لعموم الصفة، وفي الثاني قطع عن الوصفية لكونه مسندًا إلى المخاطب دون أي فلا يعم ويصار إلى أخص الخصوص، والمراد بالأول: من شئت من عتقه فأعتقه، وبالثاني: قوله من شاء من عبيدي عتقه فأعتقه. (السنبلي) مثل ما مر في إلخ قد مرّ سابقاً أنه إذا قال: "أي عبيدي ضربك فهو حر فضربوه أنهم يعتقون، وإذا قال: "أي عبيدي ضربته فهو حر فضرب المخاطب جميعهم، فلا يعتقون بل يعتق بعضهم، ووجه الفرق: أن في الأول وصف أيا بالضاربية، فتعم بعموم الصفة، وفي الثاني قطعت أي عن الوصف؛ لأن الضرب مسند إلى المخاطب دون أي فلا تعم أي فكذلك الفرق ههنا؛ لأن المشية إلخ. (القمر) أي عبيدي: وفي أي عبيدي ضربته فهو حر (المحشي) لأن المشية دليل لقوله: فإن شاء الكل عتقوا جميعا. (المحشي) صفة عامة فيه أي في قوله من شاء من عبيدي عتقه فأعتقه. (القمر)
Discussion of the word 'من'. 'من' is for partition (al-tab‘īḍ); this is its original usage, and the rest of the meanings are metaphorical. So if he says: 'Whomever you wish of my slaves, emancipate him, so emancipate him,' he has the right to emancipate all except one according to Abu Hanifa. That is because the word 'من' is for generality (al-‘umūm) and the word 'من' is for partition, so it must be interpreted as some of a general part to make both operative. Thus the addressee may emancipate whomever he wishes from any part of the general, so one of them remains. According to the two companions, 'من' is for explanation (al-bayān), so he may emancipate each of them, just as in his statement: 'Whomever wishes of my slaves, emancipate him, so emancipate him.' If he wishes all, they are all emancipated. The distinction according to Abu Hanifa is similar to what preceded in 'any of my slaves who beats you' because the will is a general attribute attributed to the word 'من', so it becomes general by the generality of the attribute. This is the original usage according to most jurists. The majority of linguists say that 'من' originally indicates the starting point of place or time. Some say 'من' originally is for explanation. The author of 'al-Muslim' chose that 'من' is shared among these meanings due to immediate understanding. (al-Qamar) 'except one etc.' because the word 'من' is for partition, so it is incumbent upon the agent to keep some of the slaves in slavery, otherwise the agent's action would not conform to the authorization and would not be effective. (al-Sunbuli) 'and the word 'من' is for partition': So the addressee becomes an agent for emancipating some of the slaves. (al-Qamar) 'so one of them remains': If the addressee emancipates them successively, the last one is not free; if he emancipates them together, they are all emancipated except one. The choice of which one remains is the master's, it is said. (al-Qamar) 'the distinction' is the well-known difference: in the first, description by beating is attributed to 'ayy', so it becomes general by the generality of the description; in the second, 'ayy' is cut off from description because the beating is attributed to the addressee, not to 'ayy', so 'ayy' does not become general, and one resorts to the most specific. The first refers to his statement: 'Whomever you wish of my slaves, emancipate him, so emancipate him.' The second refers to his statement: 'Whoever wishes of my slaves, emancipate him, so emancipate him.' (al-Sunbuli) 'like what passed in etc.' It has passed earlier that if he says: 'Any of my slaves who beats you is free,' and they beat him, they are emancipated; if he says: 'Any of my slaves I beat is free,' and the addressee beats all of them, they are not emancipated but some are emancipated. The distinction: in the first, 'ayy' is described with beating, so it becomes general by the generality of the description; in the second, 'ayy' is cut off from description because the beating is attributed to the addressee, not 'ayy', so 'ayy' does not become general. Similarly here, because the will etc. (al-Qamar) 'any of my slaves' and in 'any of my slaves I beat is free' (al-Muhashi) because the will is evidence for his statement: 'If he wishes all, they are all emancipated.' (al-Muhashi) 'a general attribute' in his statement 'Whoever wishes of my slaves, emancipate him, so emancipate him.' (al-Qamar)
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of the word 'من' in a conditional manumission scenario. The owner says: 'من شئت من عبيدي عتقه فأعتقه' (Whomever you wish of my slaves, emancipate him, so emancipate him). Abu Hanifa argues that 'من' is originally for partition (tab‘īḍ), so the agent must keep at least one slave in bondage; he can emancipate all except one. The two companions say 'من' here is for explanation (bayān), allowing all to be freed if the agent wishes. The passage also draws an analogy with the earlier discussion of 'أي عبيدي' (any of my slaves). The distinction is based on whether the description (the will) is attached to 'من' or not. The commentary cites different linguistic views on 'من'—most jurists say it is for partition, linguists say it is for starting point, some say for explanation, and one author claims it is shared (mushtarak). The passage then explains the practical application: if the agent frees them one after another, the last is not free; if all at once, all are free except one, and the master chooses which one remains.

Key Terms
من (min): In this context, analyzed as either partitive (tab‘īḍ) or explicative (bayān).
تبعيض (tab‘īḍ): Indicates a part of a whole; here it means the emancipation applies to some slaves, not all.
بيان (bayān): Explanation; here 'من' explains or specifies the group from which the choice is made, without requiring a part to remain.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a master says 'من شئت من عبيدي عتقه فأعتقه' and the agent emancipates all the slaves at once, how many become free?
A) All of them, because 'من' is for explanation.
B) None, because the agent exceeded his authority.
C) All except one, because 'من' is for partition.
D) Only one, because the will must be specific.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi interpretation of 'من' prioritizes its original partitive meaning, which requires leaving at least one individual unaffected when the text allows, a principle deeply rooted in ensuring that all linguistic components of the statement are given effect.

Rule of Inclusion and Exclusion of Ghaya (Limits)
Defines types of ghaya and whether they are included in the ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for determining whether a limit (ghaya) is included in or excluded from the limited term (moghya), and identify which type of ghaya applies to a given Quranic or legal text.

Core Idea
A non-self-standing limit enters the limited; a self-standing limit is excluded.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, the word 'ila' (to) often marks a limit (ghaya). The key question is: does this limit enter the ruling or stand outside? This passage clarifies the rule based on whether the limit is self-standing or not, using Quranic examples and addressing the dissenting view of Zufar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of ghaya
Determine if the limit (ghaya) is self-standing (exists independently) or non-self-standing (depends on the limited term for existence). Example: elbows depend on hands; night does not depend on fasting.
2
Check if the original speech encompasses the limit
If the beginning of the legal text (e.g., 'hands') naturally includes the limit (e.g., up to armpit), then the limit is encompassed. This determines whether the mention serves to exclude beyond or to extend.
3
Apply the inclusion rule
If non-self-standing and encompassed: the limit enters the ruling (inclusion). If self-standing or not encompassed: the limit is excluded (stands outside).
4
Consider overriding factors
Even if encompassed, custom (urf) or stronger evidence may override inclusion. Example: 'read to chapter on qiyas' – custom excludes the chapter.
5
Analyze the ikhtilaf with Zufar
Zufar held that all limits are excluded. The Hanafi majority refute him using the elbows example. Understand the thamarah (e.g., in wudu, elbows must be washed).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بحث كلمة "إلى" بيان أقسام التقسيم الثالث عن الآجال المضروبة للديون والثمن في قوله بعت هذا وأجلت الثمن إلى شهر أو أجرته إلى رمضان أو إلى الغد ،ونحوه، فإن كل هذه وإن كانت قائمة بنفسها ظاهرًا لكنها وجدت بعد التكلم واحترز بقولنا : غير مفتقرة في وجودها عن الليل، فإنه مفتقر في وجوده إلى النهار، وأما دخول المسجد الأقصى في قوله تعالى: ﴿سُبْحَانَ الَّذِي أَسْرَى بِعَبْدِهِ لَيْلاً مِنَ الْمَسْجِدِ الْحَرَامِ إِلَى الْمَسْجِدِ الْأَقْصَى﴾ [الإسراء: ١] فبالأخبار المشهورة لا بالنص. وإن لم تكن قائمة بنفسها، فإن كان أصل الكلام متناولاً للغاية كان ذكرها لإخراج ما وراءها فتدخل كما في المرافق في قوله تعالى: ﴿وَأَيْدِيَكُمْ إِلَى الْمَرَافِقِ﴾ [المائدة : ٦]، فإنها ليست قائمة بنفسها، وصدر الكلام وهو الأيدي متناول لها؛ لأنها متناول إلى الإبط، فيكون ذكرها لإخراج ما وراءها، فتدخل بنفسها فبطل ما قال زفر له: إن كل غاية لا تدخل تحت المغيا، عن الآجال إلخ اعلم أنه ليس اختلاف رواية في آجال الديون والثمن والبيع والإجارة، بل الغاية لا تدخل فيها بالاتفاق لأن صدر الكلام مطلق والمطلق لا يقتضي التأبيد حتى يكون الغاية لإسقاط ما وراءها. (القمر) واحترز بقولنا إلخ أي احترزنا بقولنا: غير مفتقرة في وجودها إلى المغيا عن الليل إلخ، وعن المرافق، فإن المرفق لا يوجد بدون اليد فهو محتاج في وجوده إلى اليد. (القمر) فإنه مفتقر إلخ لأن الليل هو زمان مبدؤه غروب الشمس، ولا تصغ إلى ما قال صاحب "مسير" "الدائر" من أن الليل قائم بنفسه؛ لأنه لا يفتقر في وجوده إلى غيره، يصح التمثيل به للغاية التي ليست قائمة بنفسها انتهى فتدبر (القمر) وأما دخول إلخ جواب سؤال مقدر تقريره: أن المسجد الأقصى في قوله تعالى: ﴿سُبْحَانَ الَّذِي أَسْرَى بِعَبْدِهِ لَيْلاً مِنَ الْمَسْجِدِ الْحَرَامِ إِلَى الْمَسْجِدِ الْأَقْصَى﴾ [الإسراء:١] غاية قائمة بنفسها فينبغي على قاعدتكم أن لا تدخل مع أنه ثبت أن النبي دخل بيت المقدس ليلة الإسراء. (القمر) كما في المرافق لأن ذكرها ليس لمد الحكم إليها؛ لأن الحكم ممتد، فإذا كانت لإسقاط ما ورائها بقيت هي داخلة تحت حكم الصدر. [فتح الغفار: ٢٠٩] فلا لأنها أي لأن الأيدي في نفسها مع قطع النظر عن ذكر الغاية متناولة إلى الإبط.(القمر) فتدخل أي المرافق في حكم ما قبلها وهو الغسل. (القمر) فبطل ما قال زفر له حكاية لطيفة: وهو أنه حاج الأصمعي مع زفر له في دخول الغاية وعدمه، فقال لزفر: ما قولك في رجل قيل له كم سنك، فقال: ما بين ستين إلى سبعين أيكون ابن تسع سنين فتحير زفر له. (القمر) وتسمى هذه غاية الإسقاط أي غاية الغسل لأجل إسقاط ما وراءها أو غاية لفظ الإسقاط أي مسقطين إلى المرافق فهي خارجة عن الإسقاط، وينتقض هذا بقوله: قرأت هذا الكتاب إلى باب القياس"، فإن باب القياس خارج عن القراءة، وإن كان الكتاب متناولاً له عملاً بالعرف. أي الغسل وإن لم يتناولها أو كان فيه شك فذكرها لمد الحكم إليها فلا تدخل كالليل في الصوم في قوله تعالى: ﴿ ثُمَّ أَتِمُّوا الصِّيَامَ إِلَى اللَّيْل﴾ [البقرة : ١٨٧] مثال لما لم يتناولها الصدر، أي غاية الغسل إلخ يعني أن قوله تعالى: إلَى الْمَرَافِقِ متعلق بقوله تعالى: ﴿فَاغْسِلُوا﴾ [المائدة: 6] وغاية للغسل لكن المقصود منه إسقاط ما وراء المرافق عن حكم الغسل، فتدخل المرافق فيه. (القمر) أو غاية إلخ يعني أن قوله تعالى: ﴿إِلَى الْمَرَافِقِ﴾ [المائدة: ٦] غاية لفظ الإسقاط ومتعلق به لا بقوله تعالى: فَاغْسِلُوا أو فيه أن الإسقاط ليس بمذكور ولا مضمر بل لا يخطر بالبال، فكيف يكون إلى غاية له، ومتعلقا به فتأمل. (القمر) أو غاية لفظ الإسقاط إلخ : أي للقول بغاية الإسقاط في هذا المقام تفسيران أحدهما: أن صدر الكلام إذا كان متناولاً للمغيا كاليد، فإنها اسم للمجموع إلى الإبط كان ذكر الغاية لإسقاط ما وراءها لا لمد الحكم إليها؛ لأن الامتداد حاصل فيكون قوله تعالى : إلَى الْمَرَافِقِ متعلقا بقوله تعالى: فَاغْسِلُوا وغاية له لكن لأجل إسقاط ما وراء المرافق عن حكم الغسل والثاني: أنه غاية للإسقاط ومتعلق به كأنه قيل: اغسلوا أيديكم مسقطين إلى المرافق، فيخرج من الإسقاط، فيبقى داخلة تحت الغسل، والأول أوجه؛ لظهور أن الجار والمجرور متعلق بالفعل المذكور. (السنبلي) فهي إلخ فالمرافق خارجة عن الإسقاط، فتبقى داخلة تحت الغسل.(القمر) وينتقض هذا إلخ ويمكن أن يجاب عن النقض بأن قاعدة دخول الغاية إذا كان صدر الكلام متناولاً لها مقيدة بما إذا لم يوجد دليل آخر أقوى مقتض لعدم الدخول، وأما إذا وجد دليل عدم الدخول، فلا تدخل الغاية، وحينئذٍ فلا نقض على تلك القاعدة بقوله: قرأت هذا الكتاب" إلخ لوجود دليل دال على عدم دخول الغاية ههنا وهو العرف. (القمر) عملاً إلخ مرتبط بقوله: خارج (القمر) وإن لم يتناولها أي إن لم يتناول صدر الكلام الغاية. (القمر) فيه : أي في تناول صدر الكلام للغاية. (القمر) شك إلخ لأن قوله: احتمل التأييد والتوقيت أيضًا بأن يكون إلى رجب أو إلى ما ورائها، فيكون في دخول رجب فيما قبله شك فلا تدخل أي الغاية في حكم ما قبلها. (القمر)
Discussion of the word 'ila' (to/until). Explanation of the divisions of the third division regarding the terms set for debts and prices in his statement: 'I sold this and deferred the price to a month' or 'I rented it to Ramadan' or 'to tomorrow' and the like. All these, although they are self-standing apparently, they occur after the speech. And we guarded by our statement: 'not dependent in their existence on [the thing limited]' against the night, for it is dependent in its existence on the day. As for the entry into al-Masjid al-Aqsa in His statement: 'Glory be to He who took His servant by night from al-Masjid al-Haram to al-Masjid al-Aqsa' [al-Isra 17:1], it is by well-known reports, not by the text. And if they are not self-standing, then if the original speech encompasses the limit, its mention is to exclude what is beyond it, so it enters. As with the elbows in His statement: 'and your hands to the elbows' [al-Ma'idah 5:6], for they are not self-standing, and the beginning of the speech, which is 'hands', encompasses them because it encompasses up to the armpit, so its mention is to exclude what is beyond them, so they enter by themselves. Thus what Zufar said is invalidated: that every limit does not enter under the limited... etc. Know that there is no difference in transmission regarding the terms of debts, price, sale, and hire, rather the limit does not enter therein by consensus because the beginning of the speech is absolute and the absolute does not necessitate perpetuity such that the limit would be for removing what is beyond it. (al-Qamar) And we guarded by our statement etc., meaning we guarded by our statement 'not dependent in their existence on the limited' against the night etc., and against the elbows, for the elbow does not exist without the hand, so it is in need in its existence of the hand. (al-Qamar) 'For it is dependent' etc. because the night is a time whose beginning is sunset, and do not listen to what the author of 'Musayyir' 'al-Da'ir' said that the night is self-standing because it is not dependent in its existence on something else, so it is valid to use it as an example for the limit that is not self-standing. End, so reflect. (al-Qamar) 'As for the entry' etc. is an answer to an implied question: that al-Masjid al-Aqsa in His statement: 'Glory be to He who took His servant by night from al-Masjid al-Haram to al-Masjid al-Aqsa' [al-Isra 17:1] is a self-standing limit, so according to your rule it should not enter, while it is established that the Prophet entered the Sacred House on the night of Isra'. (al-Qamar) Like the elbows, because its mention is not to extend the ruling to it, because the ruling is continuous, so if it is for removing what is beyond it, it remains included under the ruling of the beginning. [Fath al-Ghaffar: 209] So not, because they, i.e., because the hands themselves, regardless of the mention of the limit, encompass up to the armpit. (al-Qamar) So they enter, i.e., the elbows, into the ruling of what precedes it, which is washing. (al-Qamar) Thus what Zufar said is invalidated. A nice anecdote: that al-Asma'i debated with Zufar regarding the inclusion and exclusion of the limit, and he said to Zufar: 'What do you say about a man who was asked how old he was, and he said: between sixty and seventy? Would he be nine years old?' So Zufar was bewildered. (al-Qamar) And this is called the limit of removal, i.e., the limit of washing for the purpose of removing what is beyond it, or the limit of the word 'removal', i.e., 'removing up to the elbows', so they are excluded from the removal. And this is contradicted by his statement: 'I read this book up to the chapter on qiyas', for the chapter on qiyas is outside the reading, even if the book encompasses it, acting according to custom. (al-Qamar) 'i.e., washing.' And if it does not encompass it or there is doubt, then mentioning it is to extend the ruling to it, so it does not enter, like the night in fasting in His statement: 'Then complete the fast to the night' [al-Baqarah 2:187], an example of what the beginning does not encompass. (al-Qamar) 'i.e., the limit of washing' etc., meaning that His statement: 'to the elbows' is attached to His statement: 'wash' and is a limit for washing, but the purpose is to remove what is beyond the elbows from the ruling of washing, so the elbows enter into it. (al-Qamar) 'Or the limit' etc., meaning that His statement: 'to the elbows' is a limit for the word 'removal' and attached to it, not to His statement: 'wash'; or in it is that removal is not mentioned nor implied, indeed it does not even come to mind, so how can 'to' be its limit and attached to it? Reflect. (al-Qamar) 'Or the limit of the word removal' etc.: i.e., for the saying of the limit of removal in this context there are two interpretations: one, that if the beginning of the speech encompasses the limited, like the hand, for it is a name for the whole up to the armpit, then mentioning the limit is to remove what is beyond it, not to extend the ruling to it because the extension is already accomplished, so His statement: 'to the elbows' is attached to His statement: 'wash' and is a limit for it, but for the purpose of removing what is beyond the elbows from the ruling of washing. The second: that it is a limit for the removal and attached to it, as if it were said: 'Wash your hands, removing up to the elbows', so it is excluded from the removal and thus remains included under washing. The first is more appropriate because it is apparent that the prepositional phrase is attached to the mentioned verb. (al-Sanabli) 'So they are' etc., so the elbows are excluded from the removal and thus remain included under washing. (al-Qamar) 'And this is contradicted' etc. It can be answered that the rule of inclusion of the limit when the beginning of the speech encompasses it is restricted to cases where there is no other stronger evidence requiring non-inclusion. If there is evidence for non-inclusion, then the limit does not enter. And thus there is no contradiction to that rule by his statement: 'I read this book' etc. because there exists evidence indicating non-inclusion here, which is custom. (al-Qamar) 'Acting', etc., connected to his word: 'outside'. (al-Qamar) 'And if it does not encompass it', i.e., if the beginning of the speech does not encompass the limit. (al-Qamar) 'therein', i.e., in the encompassing of the beginning of the speech for the limit. (al-Qamar) 'doubt' etc., because his statement could imply continuity and also timing, such as 'up to Rajab' or 'up to what is beyond it', so there is doubt in the inclusion of Rajab in what precedes it, so the limit does not enter into the ruling of what precedes it. (al-Qamar)
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary (sharh) on the rule concerning the inclusion or exclusion of the limit (ghaya) when a term like 'ila' is used in legal texts. The discussion stems from a division of limits into two types based on whether the limit is self-standing or not. The commentator explains that a limit which is not self-standing (i.e., depends on the limited term for its existence, like the elbow depends on the hand) is included in the ruling when the original term encompasses it. For example, in the Quranic command to wash the hands to the elbows, the hand itself extends to the armpit, so the elbows are included in the washing because the limit serves to exclude what is beyond, not to extend the ruling. This refutes Zufar's absolute claim that no limit enters the limited. In contrast, a self-standing limit like 'night' in fasting to the night is excluded because the original term (fasting) does not encompass the night; the night begins after the fast ends. The passage also addresses the case of al-Masjid al-Aqsa, which is self-standing, but the Prophet's entry is established through well-known reports (not the text itself), showing an exception. Another nuance is when the original text encompasses the limit but custom or stronger evidence indicates exclusion, as in 'I read this book up to the chapter on qiyas,' where custom dictates the chapter is outside. Finally, if there is doubt whether the original text encompasses the limit, the limit is excluded due to uncertainty. The commentator includes a humorous anecdote to illustrate the absurdity of Zufar's position. The overall rule: a non-self-standing limit is included; a self-standing limit is excluded; but custom or stronger evidence can override.

Key Terms
غاية (ghaya): A limit or endpoint mentioned in a legal text using words like 'ila' (to/until) or similar.
مغيا (moghya): The thing limited, i.e., the object or ruling that is bounded by the ghaya.
غاية الإسقاط (ghayat al-isqat): A limit used to exclude what is beyond it, while the limit itself remains included.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why are the elbows included in the command to wash the hands up to the elbows?
A) Because the word 'ila' includes the limit.
B) Because the hand naturally extends to the armpit, so the elbow is part of the hand.
C) Because Zufar said so.
D) Because custom dictates it.

Key Takeaway
A non-self-standing limit is included in the ruling if the original term encompasses it; otherwise, it is excluded.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
advanced
12 min
The Particle 'ilā': Inclusion or Exclusion of the Limit
Analyzing 'ilā' as a limit for exclusion or extension

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two functions of the limit (ghāyah) in Hanafi uṣūl and apply the rule of inclusion or exclusion to Quranic verses and oaths.

Core Idea
The limit 'ilā' either excludes beyond (ghāyat al-isqāt) or extends the ruling but remains outside (ghāyat al-imtidād).

The particle 'ilā' (to/up to) frequently appears in legal texts, but its precise function—whether it includes or excludes the mentioned limit—is a classic point of uṣūl analysis. In this lesson, we dissect a passage from Nūr al-Anwār that distinguishes two types of limits and explores a dispute over oaths. Understanding this distinction will sharpen your ability to derive rulings from Quranic commands and prophetic statements, and to resolve ambiguities in legal language.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the two interpretations of 'ilā'
Identify the two definitions: ghāyat al-isqāt (limit of exclusion) where the beginning already encompasses the limit, so it excludes what is beyond; and ghāyat al-imtidād (limit of extension) where the limit extends the ruling to itself but remains outside.
The passage begins with these definitions using the washing verse as an example.
2
Analyze the Quranic example: washing to elbows
Apply the criterion of ṣadr al-kalām (beginning of speech). In 'wash your hands to the elbows', the term 'hand' (yad) includes the forearm, so the limit excludes only the upper arm, making the elbows included in washing.
Note the two sub-interpretations: limit attached to 'wash' vs. limit attached to 'excluding'.
3
Account for custom as an exception
The refutation using 'I read this book up to the chapter of qiyās' shows that custom (ʿurf) can override the default rule and exclude the limit.
Custom provides stronger evidence for non-inclusion.
4
Examine the case of doubt in oaths
When ṣadr does not encompass the limit or there is doubt, the limit is not included (ẓāhir al-riwāyah). However, a narration from Hasan ibn Ziyad holds it is included because the beginning implied perpetuity.
This leads to a practical difference in whether an oath is broken.
5
Distinguish the two types in various contexts
Apply the rule: if ṣadr encompasses → ghāyat al-isqāt (limit included); if not → ghāyat al-imtidād (limit excluded). Recognize custom overrides the default.
Practice with examples from Quran and everyday oaths.

بحث كلمة " إلى " بيان أقسام التقسيم الثالث وتسمى هذه غاية الإسقاط أي غاية الغسل لأجل إسقاط ما وراءها أو غاية لفظ الإسقاط أي مسقطين إلى المرافق فهي خارجة عن الإسقاط، وينتقض هذا بقوله: قرأت هذا الكتاب إلى باب القياس"، فإن باب القياس خارج عن القراءة، وإن كان الكتاب متناولاً له عملاً بالعرف. أي الغسل وإن لم يتناولها أو كان فيه شك فذكرها لمد الحكم إليها فلا تدخل كالليل في الصوم في قوله تعالى: ﴿ ثُمَّ أَتِمُّوا الصِّيَامَ إِلَى اللَّيْل مثال لما لم يتناولها الصدر، ﴿ثُمَّ (البقرة : ۱۸۷) أي غاية الغسل إلخ يعني أن قوله تعالى: إلَى الْمَرَافِقِ متعلق بقوله تعالى: ﴿فَاغْسِلُوا (المائدة: 1) وغاية للغسل لكن المقصود منه إسقاط ما وراء المرافق عن حكم الغسل، فتدخل المرافق فيه. (القمر) أو غاية إلخ يعني أن قوله تعالى: ﴿إِلَى الْمَرَافِقِ) (المائدة: ٦) غاية لفظ الإسقاط ومتعلق به لا بقوله تعالى: فَاغْسِلُوا أو فيه أن الإسقاط ليس بمذكور ولا مضمر بل لا يخطر بالبال، فكيف يكون إلى غاية له، ومتعلقا به فتأمل. (القمر) أو غاية لفظ الإسقاط إلخ : أي للقول بغاية الإسقاط في هذا المقام تفسيران أحدهما: أن صدر الكلام إذا كان متناولاً للمغيا كاليد، فإنها اسم للمجموع إلى الإبط كان ذكر الغاية لإسقاط ما وراءها لا لمد الحكم إليها؛ لأن الامتداد حاصل فيكون قوله تعالى : إلَى الْمَرَافِقِ متعلقا بقوله تعالى: فَاغْسِلُوا وغاية له لكن لأجل إسقاط ما وراء المرافق عن حكم الغسل والثاني: أنه غاية للإسقاط ومتعلق به كأنه قيل: اغسلوا أيديكم مسقطين إلى المرافق، فيخرج من الإسقاط، فيبقى داخلة تحت الغسل، والأول أوجه؛ لظهور أن الجار والمجرور متعلق بالفعل المذكور. (السنبلي) فهي إلخ فالمرافق خارجة عن الإسقاط، فتبقى داخلة تحت الغسل.(القمر) وينتقض هذا إلخ ويمكن أن يجاب عن النقض بأن قاعدة دخول الغاية إذا كان صدر الكلام متناولاً لها مقيدة بما إذا لم يوجد دليل آخر أقوى مقتض لعدم الدخول، وأما إذا وجد دليل عدم الدخول، فلا تدخل الغاية، وحينئذٍ فلا نقض على تلك القاعدة بقوله: قرأت هذا الكتاب" إلخ لوجود دليل دال على عدم دخول الغاية ههنا وهو العرف. (القمر) عملاً إلخ مرتبط بقوله: خارج (القمر) وإن لم يتناولها أي إن لم يتناول صدر الكلام الغاية. (القمر) فيه : أي في تناول صدر الكلام للغاية. (القمر) شك إلخ لأن قوله: احتمل التأييد والتوقيت أيضًا بأن يكون إلى رجب أو إلى ما ورائها، فيكون في دخول رجب فيما قبله شك فلا تدخل أي الغاية في حكم ما قبلها. (القمر) فإن الصوم لغة الإمساك ساعةً، فذكر الليل لأجل مد الصوم إلى نفسه، فلا يدخل هو تحت الصوم، ومثال ما فيه الشك مثل الآجال في الأيمان كما إذا حلف لا يكلم إلى رجب فإن في دخول رجب فيما قبله شكا، فلا يدخل في ظاهر الرواية عنه، وهو قولهما، وفي رواية الحسن عنه أنه يدخل؛ لأن أول الكلام كان للتأبيد فلا تخرج الغاية عما قبلها، وتسمى هذه غاية الامتداد؛ لأن الغاية مدت الحكم إلى نفسها وبقيت بنفسها خارجة عنه.
Discussion of the word 'ilā'. Explanation of the divisions of the third division. This is called the limit of exclusion: i.e., the limit of washing for the purpose of excluding what is beyond it, or the limit of the word exclusion: i.e., 'washing up to the elbows' so they are excluded from the exclusion. And this is refuted by his saying: 'I read this book up to the chapter of qiyās,' for the chapter of qiyās is outside the reading, even though the book encompassed it by custom. [Commentary:] 'or the limit of washing' etc. Means that His saying 'to the elbows' is attached to His saying 'wash' and is a limit for washing, but its purpose is to exclude what is beyond the elbows from the ruling of washing, so the elbows are included in it. (al-Qamar) 'or the limit' etc. Means that His saying 'to the elbows' is a limit for the word exclusion and attached to it, not to His saying 'wash'; otherwise, the exclusion is not mentioned nor implied, nor does it occur to the mind, so how can it be its limit? So reflect. (al-Qamar) 'or the limit of the word exclusion' etc.: i.e., for the statement of the limit of exclusion in this context there are two interpretations. One: if the beginning of speech encompasses the limited, like 'hand' which is a name for the whole up to the armpit, then mentioning the limit is to exclude what is beyond it, not to extend the ruling to it, because extension is already obtained. So His saying 'to the elbows' is attached to His saying 'wash' and is a limit for it, but for the purpose of excluding what is beyond the elbows from the ruling of washing. The second: it is a limit for exclusion and attached to it, as if it were said: 'wash your hands, excluding up to the elbows,' so it comes out of exclusion and remains included under washing. The first is more apparent because the prepositional phrase is clearly attached to the mentioned verb. (al-Sinbali) 'so they are' etc. So the elbows are outside the exclusion, thus they remain included under washing. (al-Qamar) 'and this is refuted' etc. The refutation can be answered by saying that the rule of inclusion of the limit when the beginning encompasses it is conditioned upon there being no other stronger evidence requiring non-inclusion. If there is evidence for non-inclusion, then the limit is not included. So there is no refutation on that rule by his saying 'I read this book' etc., because there is evidence indicating non-inclusion of the limit here, which is custom. (al-Qamar) 'by custom' etc. is connected to his word 'outside'. (al-Qamar) 'and if it does not encompass them' means if the beginning of speech does not encompass the limit. (al-Qamar) 'in it' means in the encompassing of the limit by the beginning. (al-Qamar) 'or there is doubt' etc. Because his saying can be interpreted as both affirmation and timing, i.e., 'until Rajab' or 'until what is beyond it,' so there is doubt about the inclusion of Rajab in what precedes it, so the limit is not included in the ruling of what precedes it. (al-Qamar) For fasting linguistically means abstaining for a moment, so mentioning 'night' is to extend fasting to itself, so it is not included under fasting. An example of doubt is like the terms in oaths: if someone swears not to speak until Rajab, then there is doubt about the inclusion of Rajab in what precedes it, so it is not included in the apparent narration from him—this is the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). In a narration from Hasan (ibn Ziyad) from him, it is included because the beginning of speech implied perpetuity, so the limit does not exclude what precedes it. This is called the limit of extension, because the limit extends the ruling to itself while remaining outside it.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul analysis of the particle 'ilā' (to/up to) in legal texts. The author discusses two major interpretations of the 'limit' (ghāyah). The first is 'ghāyat al-isqāṭ' (limit of exclusion): when the beginning of a statement (ṣadr al-kalām) already encompasses the limited item, like 'hand' covering the whole arm to the armpit, then mentioning the limit (e.g., 'to the elbows') serves to exclude what is beyond it, not to extend the ruling. Thus, the elbows themselves are included in the washing. The second interpretation is that 'ilā' is attached to the act of exclusion itself, as if saying 'wash excluding up to the elbows,' so the elbows are excluded from exclusion, meaning they are included. The text prefers the first interpretation because the prepositional phrase naturally attaches to the verb. A refutation is raised using the example 'I read this book up to the chapter of qiyās' where the chapter is not read. The answer is that custom (ʿurf) provides a stronger evidence for non-inclusion in that case, so the general rule is not broken. The passage then covers cases where the beginning does not encompass the limit (e.g., 'complete the fast until night': fasting is defined as abstaining for a moment, so 'night' is not encompassed by the beginning, thus it is not included). Finally, it discusses cases of doubt, like oaths 'I will not speak until Rajab.' According to the apparent narration (ẓāhir al-riwāyah) from Imam Abu Hanifa, Rajab is not included because of doubt; this is also the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. A narration from Hasan ibn Ziyad says it is included because the oath implied perpetuity. This latter type is called 'ghāyat al-imtidād' (limit of extension) where the limit extends the ruling to itself but remains outside it. The discussion is essential for understanding how limits function in legal texts, especially in Quranic verses and oaths.

Key Terms
غاية الإسقاط (ghāyat al-isqāṭ): The limit that serves to exclude what is beyond it; the limited item is already encompassed by the beginning, so the limit is for exclusion only.
غاية الامتداد (ghāyat al-imtidād): The limit that extends the ruling to itself while remaining outside it; used when the beginning does not encompass the limit.
صدر الكلام (ṣadr al-kalām): The beginning or opening of a statement that establishes the initial scope of the ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example 'I read this book up to the chapter of qiyās', why is the chapter of qiyās not considered read according to Hanafi uṣūl?
A) Because the beginning 'I read' does not encompass the chapter.
B) Because custom (ʿurf) dictates that reading a book up to a chapter does not include that chapter.
C) Because the limit always excludes what is beyond.
D) Because the chapter of qiyās is too difficult to read.

Key Takeaway
The particle 'ilā' in legal language can function as a limit of exclusion (ghāyat al-isqāṭ) or extension (ghāyat al-imtidād); the key determinant is whether the beginning encompasses the limit, with custom or doubt acting as override.
